Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
2 JANUARY 1945
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 2nd January, 1945.
The Senate met at half after Two of
the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER
SPEAKER in the
Chair.
Paper Presented.
Ministry of Finance:
Report of Public Record Office of
Northern Ireland for the years 1941,
1942, 1943.
Laid before the House pursuant to Act)
and ordered to lie on the Table.
Mayor of Londonderry's Knighthood.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I think it would be the
wish of the House that, before turning
to the business of the day, I should
express the very great pleasure it has
given me personally as Leader of the
House, and, I feel sure, the Members of
the House, to see the honour which has
been conferred by His Majesty the King
on the Mayor of Londonderry. I think
it is always a pleasure to see merit rewarded, but it is a peculiar pleasure when
the honour comes to an old and valued
friend and colleague. (Hon. Members:
Hear, hear.)
ORDER OF THE DAY.
HOUSING (No. 2) BILL.
Order for Second Reading read.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Before
moving the Second Reading of this Bill I
think I should express a welcome on behalf of the House to my right hon. Friend
the Minister of Health and Local Government. This is is not his first visit to this
House, and I feel sure hon. Members will
have very pleasant recollections of the
last time he was here. I beg to move,
"That this Bill be now read a Second
time."
No legislative proposal which the Government has brought forward for many
years has been so fully discussed or has
aroused such interest among the public
as this Housing Bill. Indeed I feel that
the course of the debate has rather tended
to obscure some of the more salient
features of this legislation. I propose this
afternoon to put before you as shortly as
I can what I consider to be the main
points of principle in regard to this Bill
and then to leave it to my right honourable Friend the Minister of Health to .
deal with any other questions in which
Senators are interested when he speaks
later in the debate.
The House may think that this is rather
cowardly on my part, and I am not sure
that it is not, but you must remember
that in this matter of housing the
Minister steps into the ring at least
three stone above my fighting weight
and, in addition, he has recently had
very thorough work-out in another
place.
a
The first important point which I
would emphasise is the extreme gravity of
the housing position in Northern Ireland.
There are about 320,000 houses in the
country. Of these, 42,000 are totally
unfit, and of the ones that are in good
repair or repairable no fewer than 74,000
are overcrowded. A survey of housing
conditions in the Province was made in
the summer of 1943 under the auspices
of the Ministry of Home Affairs. The
main features of the survey were embodied
in the Report of the Planning Advisory
Board, which was published in July last.
That Report estimated our immediate
needs at 97,000 houses. This is a very
large number-nearly one-third of the
total number of houses in the countryand I recall that, when the Report first
appeared, surprise was expressed that so
large a figure should be published by so
responsible a Committee and endorsed by
the Ministry of Health and the Government.
But that figure of 97,000 was a conservative one.
It made no allowance for
23,000 houses which now need very substantial repairs, or for 57,000 houses
which are deficient in light or ventilation.
It made no allowance for the fact that it
has not yet been possible to commence
building on any scale and that two years'
further deterioration in the position will
have occurred by the summer of 1945.
We may say that our first task is to provide at least 100,000 houses, and to provide them as quickly as we can. It must
be borne in mind that we are not only
short of sound habitable houses, but that
many of the houses which are structurally
sound, and which for the present we must
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
use, are well below modern standards in
such matters as size, overcrowding on the
sites and lack of the amenities and comforts which have for long been usual on
the best housing estates. Even when we
have got our first 100,000, we have still,
therefore, an immense further task ahead
of us in raising the general standard of
houses to the best modern
workers'
level.
I should like for a moment to look at
the first stage alone, 100,000 houses, and
measure it against possibilities in terms of
actual building. Between the wars we
built about 50,000 workers' houses in 21
years, an average of under 2,500 houses a
year, with a peak production in the best
year, 1931, of about 6,000 houses. The
House will note that it took us 13 years
to work up to peak production.
lowest target we can possibly aim at now
is 100,000 houses in 10 years, and we
must, if humanly possible, improve on
this. We must, therefore, multiply our
average pre-war rate by at least 4, and
reach our maximum rate of well over
double our previous peak, not in 13 years.
but in 2 or 3 years.
The
This is an immense task. It can be
solved I am confident it will be solvedbut it would be childish to suppose that it
can be solved by the normal easy-going
procedure of pre-war days. The Government have recognised that it is in
essence a technical production problem,
similar to many of the munitions problems which faced us at the
beginning of the war. Therefore, some
months ago, they re-organised their own
administrative machinery dealing with
building. There is now a special Cabinet
Committee, under the Prime Minister as
chairman, comprising the Minister of
Finance, the Minister of Health, and
myself. One of the Permanent Secretaries has been relieved of all other duties
to devote his whole time to building
problems, and with him has been associated a picked team of the senior officers
from the various Ministries concerned.
This "task-force" if I may so call it, has
already for some time been studying the
extremely complex technical questions
involved, in consultation with the Coordinating Council of the Building
industry, which, as the House knows,
represents all sections of that industryprofessional, employers, and labour. I
should like to take this opportunity of
expressing the appreciation of the Government of the public spirit which has been
shown by the leaders of the industry, and
of the invaluable assistance which they
are giving.
I now come to the present Bill, which
is one of the earliest steps in the general
preparation for this great undertakingthe first pole in the scaffolding round the
new building. The first important point
which arises is the alteration of procedure
in relation to subsidies. In the past in
Northern Ireland, subsidies in the urban
areas have been made by single lump sum
payments, and in the rural areas by a percentage grant upon the cost of schemes
carried out under the Labourers Acts.
The lump sum system is open to many
objections. It has been blamed, perhaps
unfairly, for keeping up the cost of
building, although it is remarkable that
when the first lump sum subsidy in
Great Britain came to an end in 1921,
the cost of building dropped within a few
months by almost precisely the amount of
the previous subsidy. This may have
been merely a coincidence since the
general level of prices was falling rapidly
at that time, but it was at least a very
striking coincidence. Whatever may be
the effect of the lump sum subsidy on
the cost of building, there is no question
that it represents a strong temptation
to shoddy workmanship, since once a
house is built and the subsidy paid,
the subsidising authority has no control
over or concern with the house. Perhaps
it is sufficient to say that in Great Britain
where the experience of different forms
of subsidy is far longer and larger than
our own, there is a general concensus
of opinion that, except in exceptional
cases, the lump sum is
factory method.
a very unsatis-
The percentage system as paid under
the Labourers Code has many advantages,
but only as long as building costs are
reasonably stable. The trouble with a
percentage grant at a time like the
present is that there are three parties
to the finance of house-building--the
State, the local authority and the tenant.
The tenant's payment of rent obviously
cannot be moved continually to keep in
exact proportion to the cost of building.
The result is, therefore, that if housing
is financed by the State on a percentage
basis, the local authority which under
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
2 JANUARY 1945
Second Reading
this system must make good any deficiency
after the Government grant and the
rents have come in, would stand
to gain or lose immoderately if building
costs went up or down to any substantial
extent from the figure on which the percentage had been struck.
The level of post-war building costs is
in the highest degree uncertain. Attempts
made by highly expert authorities to
estimate the probable level vary by as
much as 100 per cent. The only certain
fact is that at the moment building costs
are approximately 100 per cent. above
1939, largely through circumstances beyond anyone's control, but this cost is
completely out of proportion to the
general level of prices. The general experience has been that the long-term
tendency of building costs is to conform
pretty closely to the general level of prices
and we can only anticipate that probably
-indeed it is almost a certainty-there
will be wide fluctuations in building costs in the post-war period. In any
case the future is completely uncertain
in regard to it.
In circumstances of such uncertainty,
it would obviously be in the highest degree unwise to attempt to strike any
subsidy rate until the cost of housing can
be determined in the light of actual building experience. An amount of Government subsidy for houses of various sizes
is set out in the Bill, but I want to make
it clear that these amounts are merely
those which in Scotland in 1938 were
found to be adequate to promote a flow
of rehousing and slum clearance schemes.
Those are the nearest figures we can find
to our conditions. It is very unlikely
that they will prove to be exactly matched
to post-war housing in Northern Ireland,
and the Government have given a direct
undertaking that these rates will be reviewed, and reviewed retrospectively, so
that no local authority will suffer, when
enough houses have been built to enable
the level of costs to be more accurately
determined.
Another important new feature of this
Bill is that for the first time in our legislation the principle has been definitely
laid down that a local authority must
make a specific contribution from rates
towards the cost of each house which it
builds. In the past the general idea was
that a local authority might undertake
housing almost on a commercial basis,
and that it should seek to discharge its
housing responsibilities with the minimum
of cost to the rates. I am aware that at
times local authorities did not do that,
and, in fact, did have to subsidise rents
from rates to a certain extent. The point,
however, is now laid down that the rates
are to make a direct payment towards the
cost of each house provided. I should perhaps make it clear that in cities, towns
and rural areas alike there is a specific
statutory responsibility on the local
authority to see that the housing needs
of its area are adequately met. It is the
Government's intention that in future
these responsibilities should continue to
be discharged by all local authorities
within the limitations of their financial
and administrative ability.
It is obvious, however, in view of the
number of houses to be built and the rate
at which they must be built, that in the
case of many local authorities, the limitation to their financial and administrative ability will be reached long before
the need for houses has been met. No
one, for instance, could expect Dungannon, a town of 1,000 houses, to build
the further 450 houses which the Housing
Survey shows it to need. Cases of this
kind, where the housing problem is completely beyond the authorities' powers,
are, I am sorry to say, more, numerous
than those where the authority could
reasonably be expected to solve its problems without any special measures of
assistance.
If circumstances were more normal, it
would be reasonable to anticipate that
private finance, as in the past, could play
a large part in helping to solve this
problem, but with the present level of
building costs, and the. strong probability,
which amounts almost to a certainty, that
these costs will fall very substantially in
the course of a few years, the position is
extremely unattractive from the point of
view of the investment of private capital
in houses to be let. The urgent necessity
of the moment is for houses which can be
let rather than sold, and be let at rates
related to current wage levels and the
current cost of living, rather than to the
present very high cost of building. It is
this simple economic point that at present
prevents private finance from coming into
the housing market to any substantial
extent.
Housing (No. 2) Bill--
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
The House will note that I have spoken
of private finance, because. it is on the
financial side only that private enterprise
will not be able, in my opinion, to enter
into housing under present circumstances.
The actual building of houses will, of
course, be carried out by private enterprise in the normal way.
There is no
suggestion, except perhaps for a very
small amount of experimental work, that
houses should be constructed by direct
labour. The intention is to use the
normal resources of the building industry
in the normal manner. I do not wish the
House to conclude that either I or the
Government believe that this state of
affairs will continue. On the contrary,
we hope confidently that within a very
few years private finance. will be playing
its accustomed important role in the provision of houses for the people.
The Minister of Health in Great
Britain has said, in several speeches, that
he will try to devise some formula which
will enable the private individual again.
to put his money into the ownership of
workers' houses, but I need not indicate
to Senators how difficult it is to devise a
formula of this nature, bearing in mind
how substantially the State must contribute to keep the rents at an economic
level, and how difficult it would be to
devise a fair rate of return on the capital
which the private landlord would be
putting into the scheme. Such a formula
might be devised in a time of stable costs,
but in the present uncertainty, I think it
unwise to count on a substantial contribution by private enterprise in the immediate future, when our needs are at their
very greatest. I would like. at this point.
to repeat the assurance already given in
the other House, that so soon as a
formula, which will enable private building capital to re-enter the housing field,
has been devised in Great Britain, equally
advantageous terms will be offered in this
Province. Further than that no honest
man can go at the present moment.
In so far as the housing problem may
prove beyond the resources of our local
authorities and private enterprise the
Government feels bound to play its part,
and it is for that reason that the proposal
to establish a Housing Trust appears in
this Bill. The local authorities built
about 8,000 houses in the 20 years between
the wars.
They can and must build
many more in the next 10 years, but there
will be a wide gap between what they
can possibly build and the 100,000 houses
which represent our very minimum requirements. The Trust will, I hope, be
an effective, flexible instrument to supplement the work of the local authorities
and to co-operate with them in meeting
the housing needs of our country. Local
authorities whose financial and administrative resources are adequate to the task
of meeting their housing needs will not
want the Trust's help, and I am sure
that the Trust, which will be busy enough
working with the authorities who really
need it, will welcome the news that
any local authority feels able to
tackle its housing problem without
Trust assistance.
May I make two final points about the
Trust which I should not mention had
they not been the subject of a great deal
of misapprehension in Parliamentary and
local government circles. First of all,
houses built by the Trust are paid for by
the Government, and while the local
authority receives the rates from the
houses it does not make any contribution direct or indirect towards their
cost.
The second point is one which has been
put forward on various occasions to the
effect that instead of setting up a Trust
the Government should give extra money
to the local authorities. As I have already
indicated, the subsidies set out in the Bill
are purely token ones, and to promise
extra money now would merely be to
pre-judge the ultimate subsidy settlement
when building costs can be more accurately determined. Apart from that altogether the local authorities' problem is
not purely or even mainly one of money;
it is that many of them would find the
actual business of carrying out a housing
programme which, in many cases, will be
20 or 30 times larger than any they have
ever attempted before, completely beyond
their administrative and technical
resources.
It is not only that the volume of the
work to be done in a relatively short time
would hopelessly overload the technical
staff of many of the smaller authorities,
but that while we confidently hope that
a very considerable proportion of the programme may be carried out by traditional
methods of building, it seems almost
inevitable at the present moment that,
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
2 JANUARY 1945
especially in the earlier years, there must
be considerable resort to what are known
as unconventional building methods. As
the House are aware, much experiment
has taken place in recent years in Great
Britain, and several of these new methods
do undoubtedly give a very substantial
saving, especially in the use of skilled
labour on the site-and such saving may
be essential in the early years—and in
some cases in the materials which
likely to be in short supply.
are
I may say that these experiments have
been very closely followed by the
technical officers of the Government. At
the moment some of the staff are in
England engaged on the matter. All experience hitherto has shown, however, that
while these unconventional methods can
produce satisfactory houses, they do require a higher degree of skilled and
specialised supervision.
The pre-war work done by the Scottish
Housing Association was satisfactory
only where there was skilled supervision.
In connection with pre-fabrication highly
skilled supervision is required on the site,
and it is highly improbable that local
authorities would have at their disposal the
qualified technical men necessary to give
the type of supervision that may be
necessary. It seems to me that the
Housing Trust will be a peculiarly suitable body to supervise this type of work.
Indeed, its prototype, the Scottish
Housing Trust was originally formed to
carry out special types of building because
the Scottish local authorities felt they
were not technically equipped to do so.
Although it only started in 1937 the
Scottish Association was extremely successful. I think I have covered all the chief
points to which I wish to direct the
attention of the House.
I described this Bill as the first stage
of the scaffolding to be put round our
post-war building programme, and that
is really all it is. The actual building is
going to be an emergency job, and the
spirit of urgency and drive cannot be
conveyed in Acts of Parliament, though
I would remind the House that it can
easily be destroyed by too meticulous an
introduction of safeguards.
The Bill is to some extent experimental.
I am not apologising for that.
One cannot conduct an operation of war, and this
is in essence a war job, without taking
a
some risks and breaking some conventions,
but I do claim that the essentials of
democratic procedure, and above all the
principle of local responsibility for local
needs, have been maintained. It would
have been easy to have constructed
more centralised scheme, and many very
cogent arguments could be advanced on
business and technical grounds for doing
so, but the Government have preferred,
even at the cost of some theoretical loss
in efficiency to stick to democratic
methods, because they believe that everything will depend upon the energy and
good will of the local authorities, the
building industry and the workers, and
that all men work better when they are
given responsibility and are trusted to
carry that responsibility. I do not
believe that we shall be disappointed. I
beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I should
like, first of all, to thank the Leader of
the House for his very able, statesmanlike, informative and optimistic speech
in introducing the Second Reading of
this important Bill. Perhaps I might
also be allowed to congratulate the Minister of Health and Local Government
in having steered this Bill through a
very stormy sea in another place. It
must be a comfort to him now to feel that
it has reached a somewhat quiet haven in
which important measures of this sort
do not usually suffer shipwreck.
We all realise the importance of this
Bill, and I am sure there are very few
who would not be impressed with the
necessity for such a Bill by the facts
which were put before us by the Leader
of the House. For many reasons, some
of which the Leader of the House cited,
building programme is far in
arrears and the situation is very serious.
I came across a single example recently
which will illustrate what is happening
in every area of our city and in many of
our provincial towns. A young couple
had the courage to get married, but they
could not find a house. They felt it was
our
undesirable to attempt to set up house
with their parents. They were workingclass people, and in order to survive in.
the situation in which they found themselves the young woman had to go out to
service and the young man found lodging
in a boarding house for which he had to
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
pay 30s a week out of his meagre earnings
of between £2 and £3. For that he got
his board, but he had to sleep three in a
bed. That gives some idea of the
pressure which there must be in many
houses all over the country..
Another interesting fact relevant to
this lamentable need of houses is that in
the more densely populated areas of our
city, according to the annual report made
by the Medical Superintendant Officer
of Health, the incidence of tuberculosis
is about twice as high in these crowded
areas as it is in other areas where there
is better housing accommodation and
where the houses are better. I need not
labour the fact that an efficient housing
Bill is very necessary, and that it is
essential for the well-being and health
and happiness of our people after the war
that efficient measures should be taken
to make up to some extent the arrears
in the building of homes for the people.
There are many features in this Bill
which appeal to me. The Leader of the
House in introducing it paid a good deal
of attention to the fact that it establishes
or makes provision for establishing a
Housing Trust. That proposal, in spite
of the criticisms which have been made
about it-perhaps it is as a result to some
extent of the amendments which were
made in another place is a proposal
which commends itself to me. There are
good precedents for such a thing.
We
have an exact replica of what this Bill
proposes in the Special Housing Association in Scotland which promised to do
great and valuable work and would have
done such work had it not been for the
can
outbreak of the war. We have a precedent for such an industrial organisation
somewhat closely associated with the
Government but, nevertheless, with a
limited independence of its own in the
various public boards which have been
established to carry out certain work
which best have been done in that
way. I might refer to the Electricity
Board for Northern Ireland, and, taking
my courage in both hands, I might even
refer to the Transport Board which, in
my opinion, has done good work and
which has been very satisfactory in its
outcome for the great mass of people who
are employed in that industry. In
addition to all that such a board, I think,
will be capable of excercising an interested
oversight over the whole development of
building in Northern Ireland. It will
be able to initiate and encourage buildmost
ing where it is
needed. In
fact that point was emphasised by the
Leader of the House in introducing
the Bill.
In view of the fact that someone has
suggested that this Board will be a group
of amateurs I think it very likely that
such a Board, with one sole purpose, will
in a short time become a very efficient
body, a real group of experts, and will
perhaps do far more in the interests of
the housing of the people than anyone
ever anticipated. So far then for the
question of the Trust as a feature of the
Bill.
Another novelty in this Housing Bill is
that it proposes that the houses to be
built should be of different sizes and
dimensions, so that provision may be
made for people who have the blessing,
shall I say, of having a big family. That,
perhaps, has not for various reasons been
a blessing to many people in the past.
One of those reasons was that they could
not easily get housing accommodation to
shelter their families. This Bill proposes
that there shall be houses built suitable
for couples who have no children, or older
couples, and that hostels or flats shall be
made available for workers who do not
feel the need for ordinary domestic accommodation. I think there is a great
advantage in that, and it seems to me to
have been a defect in many of the
housing schemes of a municipal nature
in the past.
I have already referred to the report
of the Medical Superintendent Officer of
Health in Belfast in regard to overcrowded areas. Another fact relevant in
this connection is that his nurses find
that owing to the limited capacity of
accommodation in some of the houses the
people there have to sleep not two in a
bed, but sometimes three, four, five and
even six in a bed.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: It would
have to be a good bed.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: It makes
one uncomfortable to think about it.
There is a good deal to be said for the
proposal that different sizes of houses
should be built. It is only a permanent
body, such as a Government, a housing
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
2 JANUARY 1945
trust, or a public utility society, which
could afford to undertake building of this
varied type, and which would be in a
position to manage it in such a way that
it would meet the needs of various types
of families.
Another feature of the Bill that I
think is valuable and novel is that the
Minister is spreading responsibility for
housing over a greater number of
authorities. The rural district councils
are now to have their share of responsibility for the building of houses. In fact,
according to figures which I have seen,
local authorities to the number of 1,250
will have the building of houses on their
conscience in the future, and that should
surely make for a better housing programme. It is a merit of this Bill that
the Minister is undertaking not only the
building of houses but the improvement
and repair of houses, and I hope that
while the Trust may find it necessary to
encourage and undertake building in
Belfast they will not think it beyond
their province to undertake the repair
and improvement of houses in the city.
I know whole streets of houses that could
be transformed into comfortable dwellings.
With the help of a good architect every
three houses could be transformed into
two and perhaps a couple or three houses
here and there could be pulled down altogether so as to allow a little more
ventilation in these long streets, especially
where houses are built flush with the
footpath.
Another feature of the Bill is the
generous definition which the Minister
has placed on the term "worker." It
used to be "working classes." In the
other House that term was objected to
and the term "worker" inserted. A
worker includes all persons working for
and earning wages up to a maximum of
£8 a week. I was interested to know
that in the Bill of 1908 the figure was
30s; now it is to be £8; the figure is
increased five times. If a subsidy were
increased in the same proportion it would
make for the building of houses when
private enterprise is in a position to
undertake that building.
Another feature of the Bill that commends itself to me is the power given to
local authorities to provide recreation
grounds, not only in connection with
housing schemes but generally. In the
past, as Members of the House know, that
power has been limited to municipalities
and county councils. Now it is to be
extended to urban councils. I have had
one or two angry letters from publicspirited men in Dungannon-this has been
referred to by the Leader of the Housewho complain that the young people have
no place to play except in the streets, and
that the local authority has no power to
establish any recreation grounds for the
young people. These are features of the
Bill which commend themselves to me,
and there are many others to which
reference could be made, but as others
wish to speak I do not desire to say anything further along that line.
The Bill, however, may be improved,
I think, in certain respects. Vigorous
and enthusiastic as the criticism of the
Bill was in the other House, I think they
left something still for us in this House
to do. First of all, I think it is a defect
in the Bill that it does not require local
authorities, or the Trust for that matter,
to bring their new housing schemes into
relation with the new town planning
schemes which we hope all local authorities will draw up, which they ought to
draw up, and which I trust, before very
long, Parliament will compel them to
draw up if they are negligent in this
matter. I think it would be a calamity
if these houses were to be built in a
haphazard way, as houses were often
built that were erected between the wars.
I know that local authorities, in drawing
up schemes, will be required to submit
them to the Ministry and that the Ministry will scrutinise them very carefully,
but the Ministry will be very much handicapped if it has not before it a general
town plan for the future development of
the entire area in which these houses are
to be built. (Hon. Members: Hear,
hear.) I think the Bill could be improved
if an amendment were inserted requiring
local authorities, when they present their
schemes and all the particulars required,
to submit as well a general town plan for
their whole area, showing how it
could be made most suitable and
beneficial, and for the happiness of
the whole people.
This matter has secured attention and
approval by those interested in this
matter on the other side. Lord Woolton,
who is Minister of Reconstruction, speaking in October in the House of Lords,
said:
G

THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
Nothing was of so much importance
to the welfare of the country in the
immediate future as the problem of
housing. As soon as we had the labour,
it must be made into a great military
operation to ensure that the maximum
number of houses was available.
And then he goes on to add this-and
his words are very significant and
weighty-
We had to build in a hurry, but there
was no reason why we should build
without planning. Just as there was
justification for planning in the
military sphere before the invasion of
Normandy, so there was justification
for planning for housing, for knowing
where the land and the materials were
going to be.
In a debate that took place in
November the Archbishop of York
emphasised the same point. He said:
After the last war we made the fatal
mistake of building houses before we
planned. We allowed housing estates
to spring up haphazard. This time we
must have a central planning authority
not to supercede the local authorities,
but to be co-ordinating advisory con- .
sultatives.
Now we here have got a Planning
Advisory Board and we have another
smaller body called, I think, the Planning
Commission, and I would suggest that
these two bodies should have their work
speeded up so that the new houses that
are to be built will be placed to the best
advantage from the point of view of the
health and well-being of the community.
It need not involve any delay in the
building of houses.
Another feature of the Bill which I
think is a weakness is that it makes no
effort to do what the Imperial authorities are doing for Great Britain,
namely, to put a ceiling on the prices of
the land that will be acquired for the
building of houses. I think it is very
unpatriotic for a people in a situation
such as we are placed in to speculate during a war like this in the neighbourhood
of towns, particularly, and when there
is such a terrific and calamitous housing
shortage. Mr. Churchill led the Imperial
authority to the policy of making the
1939 prices the standard prices for land
that would be required for the building
of these houses when the war is concluded. Mr. Churchill, speaking on that
subject a few months ago, said:
Then we are told by the busy wiseacres, "How can you build houses
without the land to put them on?
When are you going to tell us your
plans?"
He went on:
But we have declared in 1941 that
all land needed for public purposes
shall be taken at prices based on
the standards of values of March
1939.
31,
As Members may know, the Imperial
authority since then has acted on that
policy and has legislated accordingly with
certain exceptions. I do not see any
reason why the workers in Ulster should
not have the advantage of a step-by-step
policy with England in regard to that
matter.
Of course, we know when land is taken
otherwise than by agreement the question
of price will be settled by arbitration.
Hon. Members must remember that the
arbitrators will be morally, and perhaps
legally, bound to take into account, in
giving their award, the standard current
prices of the day. If we are, to have
anything like the advantages that the
working class people, in regard to ground
rents, are to have in England, it would
require legislation, and I hope that the
Minister will impress that upon his
colleagues and have the land acquired at
a reasonable rate, and consequently the
rents of the houses reduced in that connection. I may say from the figures,
which are only token figures, that the
rents are not going to be anything to
make a song about, and consequently
there is every reason for keeping down
all expenses, such as the price of land on
which the houses are to be built, if the
people are going to have these houses at
rents which they can pay.
Another weakness of the Bill, though
perhaps it is not so important, is that
there is no grading as to the help that
is to be given to local authorities in
accordance with the ability of the local
authorities to pay. Some areas are weak
financially, and others are strong financially. A penny in the £ in Belfast will.
I am told, bring in £12,000, whereas in
Derry a penny in the £ will bring in
about £700, but the subsidy to the local
Housing (No. 2) Bill-

authority for houses they build, as I
gather from the Bill, will be the same
whether the area is financially strong or

improvement if the subsidy had been.
granted on a sliding scale, so that the
burden on the ratepayers in each area
would be proportionate to the ability of
the ratepayers to pay.
I may say that, so far as I am concerned,
I am always anxious to relieve the ratepayers of any additional charge whatever,
because, when the charge is put on the
rates it falls on the very poorest of the
people, whereas when the money necessary
comes out of the National Exchequer the
burden falls more equitably upon people
who are in a better position to pay.
There is a precedent for doing something
of that sort in the English Housing Bill
of 1938. In England, the. normal subsidy was £5 10s for 40 years. In
agricultural areas it may be £10. And
even in special circumstances the subsidy
might rise as high as £12 a year per
house. At the same time, I am mindful
of the facts relevant to this matter put
before us by the Leader of the House,
that if local authorities were not, for
financial reasons, in a position to meet
their own housing needs, the Housing
Trust was always available and would be
a source of remedy to which they could
apply.

That is about all I have to say on the

all the land the Housing Trust will need
-in the various urban areas particularly.
He is now to have a Trust which can hold
property in perpetuity. Big prices may
not already be put on all this land, and
if he is not going to have a ceiling to the
price or cost of land it would be a wise
policy for him to secure at the earliest
feasible moment the land which will inevitably be necessary for the building of
houses. You cannot build houses in the
air. There has always been objection on
the part of the Government of Northern
Ireland, and I suppose elsewhere to own
small house property, and therefore to
take land for the building of small house
property. There is no such objection
now when there is a Trust which is to be
independent of the Government. I hope
the Minister will keep this suggestion in
mind. It will also have the advantage
that when the men are discharged from
munition work he will be able to put

built.
reasons.
Another suggestion, also of a courageous
nature, is this and I am glad the Lord
Mayor of Belfast is not here to hear it.
I hope that except in exceptional circumstances the Minister of Health and Local
Government will not allow a single
additional house to be built inside the
area of Belfast. I say that for three
First of all on the ground of
defence. While we all hope there will
be no further war, it will be a scandal
and a calamity if another war does come
upon us that we in our day did not do
all that was possible to disperse our cities
to which people are flocking in far too
great numbers for their health or their
morals. That is the first reason. The
second reason I have already alluded to,
is the fact that people could be far more
healthily housed if they were housed in
satellite towns or provincial towns extended to some extent, and not only have
dwellings assigned but industries also to
the satellite towns. There is a third
reason, and that is that Belfast has far,
far too little open space left in it in comparison with other cities across the water.
Belfast has one acre of open space for
every 847 members of the population,
whereas Leeds has one acre of open space
for every 83 members of the population.
That is to say, Leeds in proportion to the
population has ten times as much open.
space as Belfast.
The last suggestion I would make is
also a courageous one, and that is, I hope
the Minister will be very courageous in
the spending of public money for the
building of houses. I would say to him
-Spend, spend, spend: not necessarily
waste. While I hope he will use all precautions against waste, nevertheless so
long as good work is being done, so long
as the housing programme is being extended I hope he will not think in thousands or hundreds of thousands, but in

THE SENATE.

Wednesday, 3rd January, 1945.
The Senate met at half after Two of the
Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in the Chair.
ORDER OF THE DAY.
HOUSING (No. 2) BILL.
Order read for resuming adjourned
Debate on Question (2nd January), "That
the Bill be now read a Second time.".
(Sir Roland Nugent.)
Question again proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: Undoubtedly the Bill
before us is one of very great interest,
both academically and practically to the
people who hope to be concerned. But
besides being a Bill of interest, it is a
Bill of some difficulty. It is not at all
easy in some cases to follow the Bill and
work out the effect which it is likely to
have if it becomes an Act.
I am not going to harrow the feelings
of the House by dwelling on the inconveniences which are at present being
experienced owing to the lack of proper
housing accommodation. We all recognise it. The fact is there; it is
urgent and it is clamant. Our business,
as I see it, is not to try to ameliorate
conditions. What we want is houses,
things with four walls and a roof, and
we want those now. We do not want
elaborate pleasure gardens; we do not
want anything so much as the bare house.
I must say that I am inclined somewhat
to regret some of the observations that
fell from the right hon. Gentleman in
front of me when we met yesterday. He
did, I am afraid, rather give colour to
the suggestion put forward from rather
uninformed sources to the effect that
after the war this Northern Ireland of
ours is going to be a land flowing with
milk and honey, a land with every
Utopian scheme the mind can picture,
where everything is absolute peace and
joy without expense, or, if expense is to
be incurred, it will, at all events, be at
the expense of somebody else.
I do not think that that is going to
happen. Whatever we do
we cannot
produce that effect. What I hope we
can do is to produce houses one way or
another. The shortage, which does give
us very considerable anxiety, seems to me
to be due in the main to three causes,
and it is perhaps worth while to spend a
minute or two examining those causes, so
that we may now, and in future actions,
do everything that is possible to avoid such
a recurrence. The three main causes seem
to me to be divisible into three main
factors. The first is what I am going
to call time, the second inaction, and the
third, enemy action.
What I mean by time is that the
natural effluxion of time brings an increase in the number of house-seeking
people. Some want houses because they
are going to get married, others want
houses because families have grown up
and their houses are too small, and others,
and they are not an inconsiderable
factor, want houses because they are
moving into one locality from some other
locality. Also, apart from the human
element as regards the time factor, you
have got a very important item in the
obsolescence of existing houses.
A
great many-I am thinking more particularly of the smaller type-were not
originally built to endure for any great
length of time. It may have been
supposed that they would last for that
length of time, but as time went on it
became apparent that they would not.
They began to fall into disrepair.
has
Then, again, advancing time
brought with it Rent and Mortgage
Restriction Acts. They have not got any
less stringent during the last few years.
I am afraid there is no doubt that they
have had a most marked and baleful influence on private enterprise as applied to
the building of houses. It is hard to
arrest it, because, as the House will
remember, houses erected by local authorities were not subject to these Acts, and
something of the sort is mentioned in the
Bill before us. Also, there has been a
suspension of building generally during
the last five years, and so it is very hard
to say there was this or that shortage in
this or that locality due to this or that
cause particularly. But you cannot get
away from the introduction of new house
seekers, nor from the invasion, so to
speak, of this or that area by people
seeking to move for one reason or another,
frequently owing to causes not unconnected with the war.
As regards what I call, for the sake of
brevity, inaction, it is usual to blame the
local authorities. I must say that to
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
3 JANUARY 1945
some extent I feel bound to try to defend
those local authorities. It must be remembered, and it is accepted as a canon,
that it is the duty of the local authority
to provide houses for, shall we say, the
working classes. The right hon. Gentleman the Minister of Health and building,
and so on, is with us this afternoon, and
I am rather anxious to know whether he
is going to tell us the foundation of this
idea.
I think it has been reported of late to
be a statutory obligation. Where does
that come from? I know that since 1890,
which, after all, is half a century ago,
that idea has held the field. The Act of
1890, as the House will remember, was
a consolidation Act. Where do you get
that statutory declaration that it is the
duty of the local authority to provide
houses for people of a certain kind living
within its boundary? I do not know that
that matters very much. It is more a
historical fact than anything else. It is
accepted as a canon and there is nothing
in the Bill before us to weaken it.
During the passage of years there has
been a very great change in the circumstances of the people whom it is sought to
affect by these housing schemes. Wages
have risen enormously. Whether you take
the present rate as standardised—that is,
of course, one of the questions that are
in the lap of the future; we do not know,
but at all events there has been a definite
rise in that respect. Again, as the right
hon. and rev. Gentleman in front of me
reminded the House yesterday, there is
now a proposal that it is to be the duty
of the local authority to provide houses
for people, irrespective of whether they
are workers o rnot, with incomes up to
£8 a week. That is the proposal in the
Bill.
The right hon. Gentleman reminded us
that in the Act of 1908 the rate was fixed
at 30s a week. He did not go on to
remind us that in the Act of 1939 it was
raised from 30s to 45s. Even so, there
is a great deal of difference between 45s
and 160s. The reason I introduce that
is to show that the demands on the local
authorities have risen enormously, and
they have struck out on to rather an
uncharted sea. They have brought in
provisions for housing accommodation for
people who are not of the working class,
and if you go too far you get an alleged
duty to provide houses for people who
are what you might describe as the building society class. A wage of £8 a week
is over £400 a year, but that is the proposal before us.
Then again, as instancing the increasing demand on local authorities, there
has been an increasing tendency to ask
for better accommodation. Nowadays it
appears to be suggested-it was suggested
in that rather valuable White Paper, the
Interim Report of the Planning Commission dealing with housing in Northern
Ireland that practically all these houses
ought to have bathrooms. In the Act of
1890-if you must take that as the starting point for the provision of houses for
the working classes-it was never contemplated to put a bathroom in a house of
that description. As a matter of fact,
you have not to go very much behind the
Act of 1890 to find houses of a distinctly
better class than that which had not got
a bathroom. Plumbing had not advanced
to that extent. I suppose everybody saw
the old play Milestones in which a bathroom was held up as a modern luxury, and
as being almost a miracle of plumbing
science. It was treated in a light spirit
as an absurdity. It was never in the
Act of 1890 suggested that houses should
be equipped with conveniences of that
description. There is no objection to
them in one way, but there is a difficulty
in paying for them. Have people any
right to come down on the local rates,
with or without assistance from the State,
for the provision of luxuries of that
sort.
The demand for improved conditions and
for an extension of the class of householder has, I think, been recognised by
Parliament, because it has been the practice for some years for Parliament to
assist local authorities by giving grants
from public funds. That is a comparatively.
new invention. I am afraid I do not
remember when the first Parliamentary
grant to local authorities was made. There
was nothing about it in the Act of 1890,
and there have been so many Acts since
that I do not remember which was the
original Act, but it has been the practice
for Parliament to supplement anything
that could be done by the local authorities.
Again, as regards the comparative
inaction of these local authorities, looking
back over the last few years we must
admit that materials have not been forthcoming, nor labour,
nor labour, but as regards

understood not long ago that there were

erect houses? The fact remains that since

Then there is the question of rates,
and it is nearly always on this question
of the restriction of rates that the local
authority is blamed. Some ten years or
more ago I set my hand to a Report in
which it was stated that the further
elasticity of local finance-finance derivable from the rates themselves-was very
nearly exhausted. That was so, and I
do not think the position has improved
since that time. I live in a rural district
and I know that my local rates are about
as high as they ever were. Although the
incidence of rating in the rural districts
more than in the towns-although they
come into it also-has been considerably
altered by derating-and that is a very
important matter-on some there are
rates for drainage and criminal injury
which are applicable to the land as well as

for
supplemented by 2d the Bann
drainage, by 1d on the whole rural district for criminal injuries, and by 3d
for criminal injuries levied directly on
the district electoral division, in which I
happen to live. That is 6d additional
to the local rates, and when the local
authorities are faced with little things of
that sort you cannot blame them for keeping a very watchful eye on the effect which
further building programmes are likely
to have on their rates.
Again, in the country districts, particularly before the war, they were not
very anxious to build houses for agricultural labourers, when there seemed every
reason to suppose that labour for those.
agriculturalists would not be forthcoming
in the future. You cannot let farming
go down with a bang, as it did after the
last war, and expect to put it back again
at five minutes' notice. The agricultural
population was very much reduced after
the last war, and you cannot expect it to
increase after the present war unless more
satisfactory arrangements are made.
think there is a great deal to be said in

I
The third point I mentioned was the
action of the enemy. It has been both
direct and indirect. The direct action
has mostly been experienced in Belfast,
and you can perhaps scarcely expect
Belfast to be in too great a hurry to build
a lot more houses of the class destroyed
in order that they might be destroyed
again the next time a bombing party
came along. At all events, it is fair to
say, I think, that the energy and enterprise displayed in Belfast in the way of
repairing those. bomb-damaged houses has
been anything but dilatory. I saw some
figures a few weeks ago showing that a
very large number of houses in Belfast
had been rendered habitable since the
bombing raids. All these three causes
keep reacting on each other. There is
the question of the clearance of slums.
Always, when a new rehousing scheme
came forward, it had tacked on to it
something dealing with the clearing away
of slums. I think the Lord Mayor once
said there are no slums in Belfast, and
giving the word "slum" the meaning I

That may

Then there is the indirect action, which
has had the effect of reducing the supply
of materials from across the water, because owing to the, let us say, dislocation
of transport arrangements, things could
not come forward as they ordinarly would.
There has also been actual loss through
direct enemy action, and all those things
account for the situation in which we find
ourselves to-day. That is the situation
as one sees it. At the moment, all these
factors have been militating against the
further provision of houses, and you have
got a clamant and urgent demand for
houses. The Government, not for the
first time, has tried to tackle the situation, and the Bill before us to-day is their
latest attempt. But there was an attempt
made earlier this year, and I hope we
will be told how that Act is getting on.
At all events, this is a new Bill. It has
been some time on its way to this House,
but it is the Bill now before us.
The Bill is divided into four parts. I
am not going to bother about Part 3, the
miscellaneous part of the Bill, or Part 4,
the supplemental part. I am going to
talk only about Parts 1 and 2. Part 1
is the part of the Bill which deals with
the new arrangements for subsidising
houses built by local authorities. It departs, as the right hon. Gentleman told
us yesterday, from previous practice.
Instead of a lump sum, payable once and
for all on a building, it proposes to pay
annual instalments of so much a year,
and the rates to be paid for the varying
kinds of houses are set out in the Bill.
But if I understand the right hon.
Gentleman the Leader of the. House correctly, he said those figures are to some
extent empirical. That makes it a little.
difficult, perhaps, for us to enact them,
but still we can take them, well knowing
that this is not the last housing Bill we
will have.
We have been told that this is a
step in the progress towards building
100,000 houses within a certain time.
That means about one in 13 members of
the population. It is rather a tall figure,
but we are all agreed that we want to
advance in the direction of producing
more houses. I share that desire, but I
want to advance by the right and not by
the left, and there is too much of that
left about the Bill before us-at least so
I think.
Dealing for the moment with Part I
of the Bill, which really deals with the
contributions under the new form, by the
local authorities, one is constrained to
ask whether this time the figures are the
best we can do. One has to give weight
to what I have said a few moments ago
about their being empirical figures, but
are these figures the best we can do?
Have we put before the British Treasury
fairly, squarely, and categorically the
fact that there is a very great amount of
leeway to make up in Northern Ireland
to get our housing accommodation into
something approaching parity with that
which prevails in Great Britain. The
point, of course, is that while these contributions will not be paid directly from
the Imperial Exchequer, they will have,
in effect, a direct influence and must
gravitate towards a reduction in what is
called our Imperial contribution. It
must come to that.
There is no use talking about keeping
back part of the Imperial contribution.
That is an absurd form of argument which
is sometimes adopted rather by the unthinking. That is not possible, but the
fact does remain that if we increase our
expenditure on objects which were allowed
under the terms of the old Colwyn award,
that increased expenditure does come
off the Imperial contribution, so that ultimately it comes from the Imperial
Exchequer by the rather Irish method of
paying out of it something that never
went into it. I think that is pretty much
the position. Have we done our best to
get the very best terms that can be
allowed? What is happening to the Act
of 1944, an Act about which I think we
ought to be told something? I do not
think there is much more I need say about
Part 1 as a whole. Any other remarks
I have to make will, be more of detail,
which, if the House will bear with me, I
will deal with later.
I now come to Part 2 of the Bill, which
deals with the proposed establishment of
a Trust. There is much that is attractive at first sight about a proposal of this
sort. In fact, I cannot help thinking
myself that if the matter had been gone
about properly and a proper Trust set up
it might have been very, very helpful.
Housing (No. 2) Bill-

Second Reading
THE SENATE
Mr. Leslie]
It
But the Trust adumbrated in this Bill
seems to me to be in no way helpful.
is wrong in every way. There is a great
deal too much of the Ministry about it.
It is adopting a new phase of bureaucracy which we do not want. The country
is sick and tired of bureaucracy, and yet,
knowing this in another place, they have
passed this Bill and sent to us a Bill which
I cannot but describe as having something more than a tinge of departmental
arrogance about it, for the thing is
certainly tinged by that kind of arrogance
which cannot admit that anything can be
done except under the immediate control
of a Government department, which may
or may not be qualified to deal with the
position presented.
I think that if the Trust is to be of
any use and as I have said, there are
cases when it might be very helpful-it
might have been better if it had been
divorced entirely from the Ministry, and
if the members of the Trust had been
appointed directly by the Governor, and
responsible, not directly perhaps to Parliament, but as regards matters of finance,
where some sort of control or liaison must
necessarily be established, to the Ministry of Finance and not to the Ministry
of Health and Local Government. Therewould then not have been the same
chance of friction as you will have now.
The present proposal seems to be that
this Trust has to act in every way in
complete subservience to the Ministry of
Health and Local Government.
I cannot myself conceive how it could be expected that any self-respecting body
would accept a position on that Trust
under the terms indicated in the Bill
before us, because of that complete subservience to the Ministry. If people
could be got to take such a position are
they going to command public confidence?
If they do not the thing is a failure.
It is said that this new Trust will
act in co-ordination with the local
authorities. Co-ordination is a very
blessed word, as blessed as the word
"Mesopotamia" was reported to be.
What does it mean? People who read
Sir Austen Chamberlain's book published
some years ago called "Down the Years"
will recall that he defined co-ordination
as the subjugation of everybody else to
the most masterly person amongst the
co-ordinating consultants. I have very
little faith in co-ordination, but I have
faith in the fact that if this Trust goes
on as it is proposed it will provoke
nothing but disappointment and disquiet. The local authorities will be
disappointed. They will say, "Here is
a Trust coming forward which will build
all the houses we want at the expense of
the taxpayer or at the expense of the
Government," because there is a popular
opinion that the Government has got a
lot of money. It is not much use
Members of Parliament getting up and
saying that the Government have not a
penny, and all the money that the
Government get has to be taken from the
taxpayers. That is not appreciated outside these walls.
That is the idea of the local authorities.
They will say, "We want houses
certainly, and we want them built by the
new Trust, which will not mean any cost
to the rates. Not only that, but when
the houses are built, rates will be paid on
them and that will fall into our pockets."
However, I do not think that the Trust
is going to be run quite on those lines,
if the House decides to allow the Trust
to start life at all. That is one reason
why I think there will be disappointment.
We are not told in the Bill how long the
Trust will continue. There is a sort of
hint, I think, in the second sub-clause
of Clause 16, though that hint is rather
obscure. What it says is that towards
the expenses of the Trust in the supplying of houses, payment is to be made
equal to the payment which the Government would have given to a local
authority, plus the amount which the
local authority was contributing. That
seems to point to the fact that the Trust
can only build up to 1st October, 1947.
There is no provision for anything after
that date. Whether it is intended that
that should be the termination of actual
building activity of the Trust or not I do.
not know. What I do know is that it
appears to us that the money to be paid
is equal to the aggregate of two contributions, and those two contributions are
only paid in respect of houses completed
by the date mentioned. We could not
alter that in this House if we wanted to,
because it would impose an additional
burden on the public, and that you would
not allow us to suggest. No doubt the
right hon. Gentleman, the Minister of
Health and Local Government, will deal
Housing (No. 2) Bill-

3 JANUARY 1945
with this point when he replies to the
Debate. At present, to my mind, it is
rather full of difficulty.
Again is not that proposal to give
grants to local authorities and the Trust
rather like putting up two kings of
Brentford? Is it contended that if the
Trust gets going and a local authority
gets going in the same area they will not
be competing against each other for such
material and labour as there is in that

having been established by the subjugation
of one or the other authority you might
get over that difficulty, but still there it
is. I cannot see that this Trust, on which
very considerable difference of opinion
and very considerable and regrettable
controversy has been allowed to arise, is
going to help us. I would appeal to the
Government to take it out. If they had
not put it in, the Bill would have been
through a couple of months ago, and they
might perhaps have started to build houses
by now. The Minister of Health and

might have had two months' start whereas
they have done nothing for more than
two months. At all events, the introduction of this Trust into the Bill has not
accelerated the production of houses.
If the Trust had been brought in as
a separate measure and separated altogether from this Ministry of Health and
Local Government I think it would have
been very useful, and I think you would

this Trust to come into operation as

House decides to give the Bill as it now
stands a Second Reading. First of all I
begin with Clause 1, and I want to know

novelty, and what is the extent of the

byre be allowed to rank as a new house?
Previously, taking the Act of last year as
an example, the house had not only to be
completed by the date specified, but the
house was to be constructed and
the
scheme approved, I think, after 1st April
of this year. There was, so to speak, in

of a terminus a quo.
There is nothing in this Bill to tell us
what constitutes a new house.
We are
told with that meticulous care which one
learns to expect that no man shall be
paid under two sets of enactments for the
same house. We are not told whether a
man may switch from one code to another.
I think that is a situation that requires
a little clearing up. When are these new
houses to be started? We know when
they are to be completed. The only other
point I have anything to say about in
Clause 1 is that I do notice a very considerable generosity for housing accommodation supplied in the form of flats.
There is to be £15 extra. It seems to be
rather a large amount out of public money,
but another place voted it. As the flat
question is likely to arise in Belfast, I
would like to know from the Lord Mayor
what view is entertained by competent
authorities with regard to that £15, and
whether it is too much or too little.
We come to the third Clause, which
deals with the new forms of accounting.
The only criticism I have to make is on
the third sub-Clause. What is to happen
in the event of an account being closed?
It is suggested in the Bill that the
Ministry has to decide what is to happen
to the money, but I am very much inclined to think that the decision ought
not to be taken without the concurrence
of the Ministry of Finance. No doubt
the money from the House Repairing

that point will be given attention and information given to us on it. I think we
are entitled to it.

It is rather being imposed upon them.

Is there any definite reason for doing so,

course, these Labourers Acts have been
going since 1883, and they have worked
not too badly. There are more cottages
now, and some districts have done better
than others. I am afraid Fermanagh and
Tyrone lag behind, but we are told we

many labourers' cottages have been provided there. I do not think you can say

failure. It is now proposed to throw the
whole thing into one code. I do not think
that is feasible, but, on the other hand,
I am not going to oppose it because it is
50-50-it may work or it may not.
The House will remember that in the

was a provision that before land was

There was a provision that the rural

I now come to Clause 8-the suggestion
that the land taken for one purpose may
be appropriated for another. I should
like to be told if it is likely to be effective
and, if so, to what extent? It does not
seem important, but it is a question

Then I come to the proposal in Clause
14 with special reference to the Trust.
That is in effect a proposal to give the
Trust-we do not know who the members
will be-power to acquire. land compulsorily. To that I cannot agree. Compulsory acquisition of land is always a
matter of difficulty. It is a matter which
should be left-it has been the practice
to do so--in the hands of Parliament
itself, as instance the familiar case, of the
railways. A railway company may want
land, and does want land. It may want
it very urgently and very much in the
public interest, but the company cannot
take it-it has to go to Parliament for
authority. A railway company has to
recognise that this country is governed in
fact, as well as in theory, by Parliament.
That is a parliamentary method.
I
Again, for local houses you have given
power to local authorities to acquire land
compulsorily. I do not think they have
to use it very much, because as a rule all
the land that the competent local
authority requires can be got. The compulsory power is very rarely used, but it
used to be used years ago by agreement,
because there was a better method of
getting the conveyancing through.
cannot accept the giving of that power to
a Board established in this manner-a
Board which is not parliamentary, is not
working under direct parliamentary
sanction, and is not worked by local
authorities. If you got rid of compulsory
acquisition you would get rid of one of
the causes that will mitigate against the
goodwill that is to be desired. The same
Clause-Clause 14-gives you a very
strong example of the powers of this Trust
as apart from administration to which I
have already referred.
My next note is a rather curious one.
It is Clause 18 (2)-what is called the

has been inserted in Bills, not only of this

years, but I draw attention to this Clause
to-day in order to give the Minister of
Health and Local Government an opportunity of explaining to us what success
he has had in his negotiations with labour.
I think he is reported as saying in another

doubt he will be able to tell us. I think
we should ask what we are getting in
exchange from responsible speakers on
behalf of labour who are in a position of
control.
Then, again, we have another Clause

accommodation unless such person has

years ending on the date of the letting
been resident in the United Kingdom.
That has been inserted in Acts dealing

with local government housing. I do not

That is

hands and ought to have any perquisites that are going.

put something on paper if necessary. I

rather suggests something that happened
I think in the time of the Premiership
of Walpole and the Excise men. The

The next point to which I wish to draw
the attention of the House is the Rent and
Mortgage Restriction Act. That is really
a limited Act because it did not apply
to local authorities. Therefore it is
natural that you give the same exemption
to Trust houses. I am not sure, but I
think we require to say that it should only
apply so long as these houses remain the

Trust. Rightly or wrongly there is a

Rent Restriction Act. I do not know
whether it could be successfully argued
by the term provided that the local
authority was no longer providing the
accommodation. It is only a matter of
inserting a word to safeguard the position.
I have not much to say on the Interpretation Clause except in regard to the

It is mischevious Socialism by reason of
the fact that if you take too many of these

comparison with the insurance level. It

May I, before I sit down, thank the
House for the consideration extended to
me and for listening to me for a comparatively long period? It is only a
further exhibition of that kindly courtesy
which they have extended towards me for
over twenty years.
The LORD MAYOR OF BELFAST
(Sir Crawford McCullagh): Allow me to
say at the outset that I support the Bill.
Indeed I may say that I would support
almost any measure that would produce
houses. I want also to admit that the
Government by this Bill are making an
honest attempt to solve this question.
Whether it will be successful or not remains to be seen. I want, however, to
strike a warning note to the public. Every
day we meet people who are under the
impression that they will get houses
immediately under this Bill. That is not
so. There are to-day in the City Hall
applications for 7,500 houses. I am
troubled every day with people writing in
making priority claims and demanding to
get houses under the Bill. It cannot be
done. I only hope that the Government
in the effort they are making will not be
disappointed. I trust that it will be up
to expectations. That is as much as I
possibly can say here. A great deal will
depend on the amount of materials that
is available. They are not available at
present on a large scale. The root of this
whole trouble from the beginning to the
end has been the tremendously high cost
of houses. Whether this Bill will solve
the question or not remains, as I have
already said, to be seen.
This Bill is divided into two parts.
Part I deals with the contribution or
subsidy from the Government and the
contributions from the local authorities.
This has caused some criticism because
the system is rather new. The subsidy
is being spread over 40 years. This,
as I say, has been criticised, but it
makes no difference. It works out exactly
the same in the end. I have got figures
taken out and checked, and there is no
dispute as far as they are concerned. For
the third class of house with three apartments the Ministry are giving a contribution of £10 10s and the local authority
£4 10s; for the second class of house the
Ministry are giving £11 15s and the local
authority £4 15s; and for the third class
of house the Ministry are giving £13 and
the local authority £5, making £18. If
we take the £18 house it works out that
the Government's contribution is £289
and the local authority's £111, making in
all £400, which is just £10 more than
the subsidy that the Government are giving for the war-time houses, of which 750
have been allotted to Belfast. I have to
use only estimates because there is no
definite figure. Senator Leslie wanted to
know if anything was being done about
those houses. Tenders for their erection
have been invited this week.
Mr. LESLIE: Are they being built
under the earlier Act of this year?
The LORD MAYOR OF BELFAST:
Yes, the £390 subsidy. The estimated
cost of these houses is £930 each. It may
be more, and it may be less. Do not take
me definitely as binding you down to that
figure. The estimated price, which is all
that we can go by, is £930. Take the
Government subsidy off that, and it
works out that the house will cost £540.
Take that house at £540, and take all the
charges against it, including rates, it
Housing (No. 2) Bill-

3 JANUARY 1945
works out at a rent of £1 Os 6d a week.
That is supposed to be a worker's house.
You cannot expect a worker to pay a rent
of £1 Os 6d a week. Some other means
will have to be adopted or tried to bring
the rent down to a figure which workers
can reasonably pay. Perhaps the citizens
of Belfast have been spoiled, because the
rents of houses in the city not so very long
ago were something like. 2s 6d a week for
kitchen houses, and 5s 6d and 6s 6d a
week for parlour houses. Those were the
rents workers were accustomed to pay.
I mention that to show clearly and distinctly that this was all brought about
by private enterprise. There was no
subsidy in this place.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: And
no rent restrictions.
war
The LORD MAYOR OF BELFAST:
Quite right. Here are some remarkable
figures. Belfast had before this
117,000 dwellings, and you would hardly
believe that nearly half of those-56,000
-were damaged; 3,200 were completely
destroyed and 48,600 have been repaired.
The War Damage Commission have paid
over £2,000,000 for repairing those houses
and they are still paying. That is a very
important point, and has a great bearing
on this Bill. Between the last war and
1939 the Corporation of Belfast built
2,562 houses and private enterprise built
20,420. You will notice the comparison.
I say if you want to solve this problem,
you will have to bring in private enterprise. I do not say you are. not to go on
with the Bill; on the contrary, use every
means in your power to get houses, but
in my opinion this problem cannot be
solved until you bring private enterprise.
into it. I have heard the Minister say
he is in favour of private enterprise. If
private enterprise. is brought in you will
get houses. I admit that the Government could not include in this Bill a
subsidy for private enterprise. Until we
find out what the subsidy by the Government across the water will be, we can do
There is no
nothing in that respect.
doubt whatever that the Bill only goes
a little distance.; it certainly does not go
far enough, and it could not possibly go
any further until we find out what the
subsidy is going to be in England.
Part 2 of the Bill, which was referred
.to by Senator Leslie, is rather different
in this respect. It is claimed that the
Trust to be set up is to build houses which
will cost the local authorities nothing,
that the Government is going to bear the
whole expense.
How it will do that I do
not know. The Bill does not say how it
can be done. In fact it is claimed that
the Bill will be an advantage; the local
authorities will make money out of the
houses. In other words, they will get
rates from the houses that are to be built.
This brings me to a very important matter
I think the
dealing with the Trust.
Government when they are given powers
to acquire land should have a further
power given to them, namely, that where
houses are built outside the ambit of a
local authority the land taken should be
vested in the local authority. That will
not please everybody. Take the city of
Belfast. We all want houses. I have
not met one who says otherwise. There
is no land available in Belfast on which
the number of houses required can be
built. The land is all used up. It is
true we have little patches here and there
and that there is vacant ground in highlyrated localities, but you cannot build
houses there. You cannot get the ground
to-day to build the houses required for
the city of Belfast. Consequently if the
Government want houses built they will
have to do something to meet that difficulty, because that is the real difficulty.
What I did suggest was a meeting of the
authorities concerned, and where they
have to go outside the boundary-of
Belfast or any other place in Northern
Ireland-for building ground they should
have power to vest that ground in the
local authority.
What do you find? It is the same all
over, not only in Northern Ireland but
in England. There is a tendency for
people to move and build houses outside
the boundary. And why? To escape
the rates. That is one of the reasons.
It is the big reason. They have all the
amenities of the city. If the city were
not there those houses would not be built.
consequently I still hold that where
workers' houses are being built for
workers of Belfast they should be built in
the city of Belfast. The same would
apply to Derry or Ballymoney if you like.
Wherever they are built they should be
built for the workers in the area. I had
a very distinguished visitor to-day and
discussed this question. It is the same
trouble in New Zealand as we have here.

The city grows and the people go out to
save the rates. You have it all over the
country.
I had a very eminent gentleman with
me, the mayor
of a certain city. He
came to give an address in the City Hall
on civic government. Amongst other

After the address was over I asked, drawing his attention to this statement, "What
do do in your home town?" He said,
you
"We solved the question all right.
We
do not trust to luck." He said where a
man builds a house or a firm builds a
factory outside the boundary they make
him contribute what he is entitled to pay
towards the rates. If he does not pay
towards the rates he will not get the
benefit of the services. That is a definite
rule. They pay as much outside the
boundary as they pay inside. They have
not only the amenities of the city, but
they have the services, which cost a big
lot. He said a certain firm built a big
factory outside the boundary, and they
would not subscribe to the rates at all
but said they would trust to luck. They
built a magnificent factory, and one day
this factory caught fire. They phoned
the authorities to send out the brigade.
They said "No, put out the fire yourself."
The result was they stood and watched
that factory burn to the ground. That
was according to the regulations.
I do not suggest we should go that
length at all. I say that is going a bit
too far. But the fact remains I do
think there will have to be another Bill
brought in in which power should be
given to meet this great difficulty that I
have pointed out, otherwise you can take
it from me you will never get the houses
in the city of Belfast that you require.
I finish as I began. I will give all the
support I can to this Bill. Indeed I will
support any measure or any Bill that will
bring houses to our city.
