Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
agree with the right hon. and learned
Senator opposite, and with the Lord
Mayor of Belfast, that the Housing Trust
does provide the country and the Government with a great opportunity, provided
that the Trust is so organised as to be
efficient. And in this particular Bill the
Government's credit is heavily involved
with the success of the Bill, in view of
the statement made in another place.
But in that other place the Bill was
passed by so slender a majority that it
almost amounted to a condemnation of
the Bill, so many Members abstained
when the final vote was taken. I think
so important a measure should be so
drafted as to command the respect of
both Houses of Parliament, even though
it may not be expected to command the
enthusiasm of the Members. But I am
not going to judge this Bill on its reception in another place. If I did I should
certainly vote against it.
I think the Bill is in some respects
premature. There is no one who can give
any idea of the costs of building after the
war, and no one can estimate the capacity
of the Government or of the country to
finance it. Businesses in this country
are not so extensive as they are in England, and whether we can finance these
undertakings very largely depends on the
amount of export trade we can recapture.
As we do not know what finances are
going to be like after the war, I do not
see how the Government can guarantee
to make this Bill effective. When the
right hon. Professor opposite spoke of
spending millions on housing, I think he
was really speaking to a much larger
audience than this House and one
perhaps not quite so thoughtful. As an
ex-Minister he must know the terms of
our financial agreement with Great
Britain, and that we are not in a position
to carry out his advice.
The fact remains that whether we call
it a subsidy or not the Government
are providing a subsidy for building
houses at a time when the British Government do not feel justified in doing so.
Although the Minister of Health and
Local Government has said that when the
British Government feels justified in giving a subsidy to private enterprise he will
get a corresponding subsidy for this
country I wonder whether he really will
be able to do so, because it is quite likely
that the British Government will say,
"Now we are giving a subsidy to private
enterprise. You have had your subsidy
already," because any subsidy he gives to
building now must come out of the funds
of which the British Government are the
so-called residuary legatees. So I am
very anxious about the position. I fear
that unless there is a definite agreement
on any subsidy which is given and will be.

passed to private enterprise we will find
ourselves answered in that way.
Are we
sure on any financial agreement reached

any Government in power after the war?
No building on a large scale can possibly
take place until two or three years after

may happen. A new Government may

A General Election is expected
next year.
How do we know we shall

In the Housing Trust I am afraid the
Government have merely added a new
local authority to those already in existence, but in this case it will be a local
authority without experience or knowledge of the highly technical work which
the local authorities have to do. According
to the Minister of Health and Local
Government the members of the Trust
will be men with plenty of leisure and who
still have the overwhelming confidence of
the country. Now who are these men
and where are they to be found? Men
with plenty of leisure are usually those
whose best working days are done, or they
are those whose capacity or disposition
does not justify very great public confidence. If they have plenty of leisure
it means that people have not much confidence in placing undertakings in their
hands. I think men placed in the responsible position of providing houses for
the people should be energetic business
men with a great deal of imagination and
drive. I think they must be prepared
to take risks and they must not be men
too hide-bound by building traditions.
The Leader of the House talks of plans
for building a large number of houses. I
think he said that 100,000 houses were
immediately required and, in addition,
23,000 required repair and 50,000 were
deficient in light and ventilation. 42,000
were unfit. Now I am not sure whether
his figures correspond with the Housing
of Northern Ireland Interim Report-

I am rather afraid that a good deal of
the criticism of this Bill has been directed
solely towards the question of finance
and not enough has been directed towards
the question of material. During the
war there has been the unfortunate result
that a great many brickworks have
closed down.. According to the report of
the Planning Advisory Board I think I
am right in saying about 25,000,000
bricks increase is required before the
house deficiency can be made up. What
is the Government doing in this matter?
Is it placing long-term contracts with the
brickworks to ensure that when these
houses can be built there will be an
adequate supply of bricks, because if it
is not doing so you will find that bricks
have a scarcity value and later will
probably be unobtainable.
The real bottleneck in this case is not
finance or materials, it is labour. According to the Planning Advisory Board if
the maximum amount of labour is available there will only be some 5,000 houses
a year produced in Northern Ireland,
but most authorities agree that you
should
put the amount of labour available round
about 40 per cent. of the maximum,
which would make the maximum number

THE SENATE
[Major Perceval-Maxwell]
of houses that can be produced somewhere
in the nature of 2,500 a year. According to my calculations it will take 80 years
to make up the deficit of houses on that
basis.
I should like to ask what the right hon.
Gentleman is doing about this, because
in England there is the advantage that
the Government can control labour
and direct it. In this country the
only people they can control are the
employers of labour. In this case it
is no good planning to have enormous numbers of houses built unless
there are plans to produce the necessary
material to build them with. The
bottleneck of labour is really the plumbers.
There are very few skilled plumbers
available. Even if you have the necessary bricks and mortar you cannot build
houses with bathrooms and other things
which the right hon. Gentleman has
forecast unless you have the plumbers to
put in the piping.
Notwithstanding my criticism of the
Bill, I desire, like everybody else, to see
houses built, and I do not regard it as incumbent on me, a Conservative, to oppose
the Bill on the ground that it is a Socialist
measure. I consider that in so far as it
guarantees a long-term policy of expansion for the building industry and stabilisation of production costs, with a guaranteed use for money, and material, and
labour, it will have a good effect on industry as a whole, including that part
of industry which deals with building,
and, of course, including that part of industry-it is a rapidly contracting part
now-which is still managed on individualistic principles.
I am afraid that one of the great
defects of this Bill is that the Housing
Trust are themselves too hide-bound, and,
as other Members have said, they are
bound fast to the Ministry of Health.
They will require considerable tact and
circumspection if they are to steer a safe
course between the Scylla of obstructive
officialism and the Charybdis of public
antagonism. I think they will meet
opposition on both these grounds because
they must realise that their work may
throw a great strain on the local authorities who have to provide all the necessary
services such as water and drainage for
the people in the houses. It must not be
forgotten that if you collect people in an
area in which they are not ordinarily
collected you throw a strain on the
religious authorities of providing
churches. Many of the religious authorities have a very big building policy for
churches and schools after the war.
Then you throw more strain
on the
hospitals and the supply services. There
have to be shops there.
The Minister has been very careful
not to say anything about a water supply
to these communities which he forecast in
another place. How are they going to
get water? I raised this question some
years ago and asked for a centralised
water supply for the whole Province.
The answer I then got was that there are
wells in Northern Ireland. Were they
not good enough for the people.? They
were quite happy with them. This policy
of housing people in good houses, with
plenty of baths and lavatories, and so
on, will require something more constructive than a policy of wells, and I hope
the right hon. Gentleman has thought
out how he is going to bring water to an
almost waterless country. When I say
waterless I refer to the land, not to the
air or sky, because we have no big rivers
except the Bann. The water position is
very difficult, as I know very well from
my experience when this country became
full of troops.
I should like to point out that building
houses for people to live in is a partnership between those who supply the. houses
and services and those who live in the
houses. You cannot coerce any party for
the exclusive benefit of the others. The
Housing Trust, being so much under the
control of the Ministry, will find that a
good deal of coercion is done, and a good
many stupid letters are written as to why
people. do perfectly obvious things. All
this will hold up the provision of houses.
The learned Professor has claimed that
the Government should fix the price of
land.
Does he realise. that nearly twothirds of the people of this country are
proprietors of land in some direct or
indirect way, and that the other third are
the relations of the. proprietors, and perhaps hope to be proprietors themselves
one day? Thus the position here is very
different from that in England, where
the land is held by comparatively few
people who can be deprived of their
land without anyone very much caring.

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: We
have listened attentively to some very
able speeches on this Bill. I must say
that I always admire the right hon. and
learned Gentleman (Mr. Leslie), who has
such a wonderfully retentive memory and
who takes such a tremendous interest in
our legislation. He certainly was very
interesting. None of the speakers has
denied the fact that we are very much in
need of houses not only in Belfast but
throughout the whole of Northern
Ireland. I wonder does everyone
recognise how urgently they are required.
It is a desperate position the people are
in. People like myself who are serving
on local authorities have an opportunity
of learning the conditions under which
the people are living. The Lord Mayor
has pointed out that a considerable
number of people approach him and send
him letters. Only a short time ago our
medical officer of health in Belfast asked
his health visitors to report anything unusual they noticed when going their
rounds. One of the health visitors re-
I had a call on Thursday night last
from a very respectable and very intelligent young man, who wanted to know if
I could assist him in any way to get a
house or apartments. He lived in Ballyclare and occupied a small cottage which
had been condemned, because it was not
fit for habitation. The water was coming
in through the flat roof. He told me he
had spent two days looking in vain for a
house in the city, and no one would give
him apartments because he had children.
I advised him to write to the town clerk
or the surveyor at Ballyclare and point
out his position. I explained to him that
the authorities there were doing something for the welfare of the children,
because in the course of conversation he
had told me that two of his children had
whooping cough. Think of the conditions
that exist not only in Belfast but in
country districts like that, where you
would think there was plenty of ground
and there would not be much difficulty in
putting up huts or other temporary structures to accommodate a man and wife and
five children. The lives of children are
very valuable, and they should receive
every attention to ensure that they grow
up strong and healthy.
The proposed Housing Trust seems to
be the cause of a tremendous volume of
criticism, but, like the Lord Mayor of
Belfast, I feel so disturbed about the
present position that I would not care
what was recommended as a solution to
this problem. Reference has been made
to the risk we have got to run, and I
would advise the House to take the risk of
letting this Bill pass, and take the
chance of getting some houses built within
a comparatively short time. The Minister
has put forward the proposal for this
Trust because he thinks that through the
Trust he will have a better opportunity
of getting houses built quickly. It is
because of that belief that I shall give
the Bill every support. The need for
houses is so very urgent that whether the
Minister recommended a Trust or some
form of monopoly I would give it all the

The Trust is not likely to build houses
in the city of Belfast. There is no ground
available in the city on which the Trust
could build a colony of houses or any
considerable number. The houses that
are required in Belfast must be built by

those who owned property that was built
before 1914. They have decided what
amount they will award in respect of property built after 1914, but arrangements
should be made as soon as possible to
erect houses on at least some of those
blitzed sites.
In the Division in which I live they
have some of the finest working-class sites
in the city of Belfast available. In streets
which were blitzed and in which there
were a considerable number of casualties
the tenants took a pride in their houses
which, both inside and outside, were
always kept in good order. Those houses
were erected before 1914, and if it were
possible to make arrangements with the
War Damage Commission to allow
builders to erect houses on those sites it
would give great satisfaction to the
people. In such cases it
be necessary
may
to increase the rents. I do not think
there should be any rent restriction in
respect of such houses, because the cost
of erecting new houses on those sites
would be three or four times the pre-war
cost. Therefore we should not quibble
so much about the rent. People are in
such desperation for houses that they
would be willing to pay almost any rent.
I know the workers do not want to be
planted in country districts. Someone
suggested we should start colonies in the
country, with gardens attached to the
houses, but if a man works in the shipyard, the mill, or the factory he wants to
get home as quickly as possible and not
have to travel in trains or buses which
may be packed and be very uncomfortable.
We have had experience of this in the
city of Belfast. The Corporation built a
number of houses on the Whitewell Road,
Greencastle, and, while the Corporation
houses in Belfast were fully occupied,
those on the Whitewell Road were never
occupied until the shortage came, simply
because they were in an outlying district
and the people had to travel by tram and
pay fares in order to reach them. That
proves that the working classes do not
want to be planted in country districts
away from their work if it is possible to
avoid it. I would urge the Minister to
see what can be done to provide the
necessary finance to induce small builders
to erect houses on blitzed sites in the
city. I would not suggest that houses
should be built on every blitzed site in
Belfast, because in some of the situations
the houses were worse than slums, and it
was a good thing to see them cleared out.
Reference has been made to baths in
houses. While you might erect houses
without baths, the standard of living of
the people has improved, and if the
workers are inclined to use baths, as I
believe they are, by all means give
them the opportunity. Baths are very
necessary in a house where a man is
working at a dirty trade like coal heaving, or even in the shipyard where there
is a tremendous amount of dust and dirt.
Baths are very necessary in cases like
that, and they are far more necessary for
the children. Therefore, I do not think
a house should be condemned simply because provision is made for baths.
far better to try and raise the standard
of living of the worker than having it
go back to what it was some years ago.
Therefore, I have no objection to the
baths.

who wishes to speculate on building or go

because of all the cities I have been in
none came up to the standard of ours, as
it was at that time, when house rents
were cheap and the houses were good.
If something could be done to restore
private enterprise to the extent to which
it operated in the years of which I speak,
I believe it would be for the general good,
because the houses would be erected and
would be available at reasonable rents.
Further, competition in building would
reduce the cost of houses, and that in
turn would mean lower rents. I do not
wish to occupy any more of the time of
the. House, but I do say again that as far
as this Bill is concerned I intend to give
it my whole-hearted support. I sincerely
hope that if the Minister finds there is
any difficulty in getting the Trust to get
a move. on, he will do something in the
way of introducing some other scheme.
which will get things done more quickly,
because I say sincerely that it is most
necessary that something should be done
as soon as possible if we are going to
save the lives of the younger people who
are coming on.
Mr. LENNON: I beg to move, "That
this debate be now adjourned."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Ordered, That the debate be resumed
to-morrow.

ORDER OF THE DAY.
HOUSING (No. 2) BILL.
Order read for resuming adjourned
debate on Question (2nd January), "That
the Bill be now read a Second Time".
(Sir Roland Nugent).
Question again proposed.
Mr. McALLISTER: Now that this
domestic dispute has ended so agreeably
I should like to address myself to the Bill
before the House. I am prepared to
support any Government, any Party, and
anybody who is prepared to make a bona
fide and genuine effort to provide reasonable housing accommodation for all the
people, irrespective of party or anything
else. Sometimes I think Governments
do not realise that once they accept office
they are called upon to discharge a most
sacrosant trust. They are trustees not
only of their own followers who elect
them, but of the rights, liberties, and
privileges of every section of the community, including the minority in the.
Six Counties. The principal controversy
over this Bill has centred around the
Housing Trust. I say frankly that the
system of nomination to that Trust, the
machinery whereby it has been carried
through, is absolutely antagonistic to the
views I have always held and do hold as
to democracy.
When I read the Bill I made it my
business to have the most exhaustive inquiries made amongst that one-third of
the population of the Six Counties whom
I directly represent in this House. I
found that,
that, without exception, the
answer was that the Housing Trust, in
its administration, could not be more
unfair to the minority in the Six Counties
than many of the local authorities have
been. I am confirmed in that opinion by
a unanimous resolution of a national
organisation to which I belong, which
passed a resolution not to oppose the Trust
on the ground I have stated. I do not
intend at this stage to discuss this Bill
seriatim, or to go into details. I intend
to leave that to the Committee stage, but
in order to justify my position in regard
to the Housing Trust I am bound to point
out what is a well-known fact, because it
has appeared in the newspapers, namely,
the terrible unfairness and injustice of
many of the local authorities in Northern
Ireland towards the minority.
Are those local authorities solely to
blame? Many of these local authorities
have carried on in this way because of the
gerrymandering by the Government of
electoral areas, which was done to deprive
the minority of their rightful representation on these local authorities. Pursuing
the lead given by the Government to its
logical conclusion, they came to the decision that it was right to deprive the
minority of all their civil rights in Northern Ireland. Hence my justification
for not opposing the Housing Trust.
There is another Clause of this Bill I
intend to deal with-Clause 19, the residential Clause. We are told that this
Bill has been introduced from very high
ideals, namely, to carry out what is after
all an elementary Christian duty, and a
humanitarian duty. That is to provide
reasonably comfortable houses for the
working people. If that is the object of
this Bill it should have been above party
politics, but I say without hesitation that
Clause 19 puts the impress of party
politics upon the measure, and by putting
this Clause in the Bill the Government
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
4 JANUARY 1945
have made it a party Bill. I would ask
the Government for their own sake and
for their own credit to delete that Clause.
What is going to be the reaction not only
in Ireland, but across the water and in
America when the people of those countries come to understand that a local
Parliament in Ireland, a Parliament for
a small corner of this historic Irish nation
which is older than England, will pass
legislation preventing citizens of Ireland
from coming within its scope, while opening the doors wide to
wide to residents of
England, Scotland and Wales. What is
going to be the reaction?
I am informed, and I believe, that
Irish descendants form 30 per cent. of the
great American Army of to-day, which
we are told is fighting for liberty and
justice and against privilege. What is
going to be the reaction of that 30 per
cent. and their kith and kin in America
when they know that legislation stamped
with purely party politics has been introduced into this House at a time when
we are told we are fighting for justice
and freedom for all?
There is another question that has
occurred to me which I should like to
address to the Minister of Health and
Local Government, whom I am glad to
see in this House, and with whom I am
pleased to renew acquaintance across the
Table. There seems to be no machinery
whatever within the scope of this Bill
which will ensure that the houses built
under the Bill will be let at a rent the
working man can pay. There seems to be
no machinery in the Bill at all dealing
with that.
There is another question I should like
to ask the Minister of Health and Local
Government. As I interpret the definition of a worker it means that any person
whose average income does not exceed £8
per week comes within this Bill and is
entitled to benefit under it. If my interpretation is wrong I would ask the right
hon. Genleman to clarify the position
and let me know. I will not enter into
any further discussion on the details of
the Bill. I prefer to leave that to the
Committee stage. This Second Reading
debate has gone on longer than most
discussions in this House, and I will
reserve detailed criticism for the Committee stage.
Mr. BROWNE: After the interesting
peeches-I think there were six or seven
of them-to which we have listened and
and the x-ray examination of the Bill by
the right hon. Gentleman-Senator
Leslie, whose analysis of any Measure,
whether we agree with him or not, is
always an education, there is very little
to be said. The Leader of the House, in
his very clear exposition of the Bill, spoke
of the crying need for houses, and we
had a graphic description of the appalling
conditions which exist in some quarters.
I do not think any further descriptions
are required to convince us of the deplorable housing conditions in certain
quarters. We are sadly aware of them
and we are equally convinced of the necessity for a thorough-going remedy.
The Lord Mayor has warned us that
we are not to expect too much after this
Bill has reached the Statute Book, and
the Leader of the House has given expresion to a similar view. We do recognise
that it is a physical impossibility within
a short time to carry out a programme
involving the erection of some 100,000
houses. That is the estimate that has
been given for Ulster's immediate needs.
A period of years will be necessary. We
cannot say when the war will end, and all
building materials are scarce. We have
been officially told that Ulster's housing
needs cannot be met by local authorities.
The cost would be far beyond their means,
and would impose a crushing burden on
the rates. That is why the Bill provides
for the setting up of a Housing Trust
financed by the State. I take it that the
Trust will not be a rival to local authoritities. It will come into the picture when
local authorities have made their contribution to the building programme, a
kind of supplement to local effort.
It is said that the operations of the
Housing Trust will not involve any
burden on local rates. Not a penny of
the ratepayers' money is to be spent on
the Trust's houses. Frankly speaking,
I do not find much difference between
paying taxes and paying rates. The
money comes out of the same pocket,
though taxes are imposed upon broader
shoulders. The establishment of the
Trust will certainly mark a new departure
in housing policy. Hitherto private
enterprise and local authorities have provided us with most of the houses, and
the Minister says that even when the
Housing Trust and the local authorities
get to work there will be plenty of room
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Browne]
for private enterprise to play its part in
the solution of the housing problem. It
is satisfactory to know that, for in the past
we have been well served by private
enterprise.
The Lord Mayor, who has a wide and
practical knowledge of the housing
question, apparently pins his faith to
private enterprise. He has pointed out
that between the last war and 1939 the
Corporation built 2,562 houses but
private enterprise built 20,000, so that if
we want to solve the housing problem we
will have to bring in private enterprise.
The Leader of the House has stated that
only 40,000 or 50,000 workers' houses
were erected in the 21 years prior to the
war. I am just wondering whether that
was due to the fact that in recent years
houses could not be built and let at
economic rents. If they are to be let at
rents which workers can afford to
pay-I am not referring to those in
receipt of £8 a week-the State must
come in with substantial financial help.
It was very gratifying to hear the
assurance given by the Leader of the
House that as soon as a formula which
would enable private capital to re-enter
the housing field had been devised in
Great Britain equally advantageous terms
will be offered in Northern Ireland.
Rev. Professor Corkey advised the Minister that when spending public money
he should think not only in thousands but
in millions. I fear he must not only
think in millions but spend in millions,
especially when we remember that the
Government up to 1939 contributed in
subsidies towards the building of houses
and cottages no less a sum than
£3,364,677. And the position then was
nothing like so serious as it is to-day.
I am glad to see that it is proposed to
give powers to local authorities and the
Trust enabling them to provide for the
erection of churches, schools, shops and
other buildings, laying out of recreation
grounds and open spaces, and so on. If
more colonies, villages or communities
could be formed or established in country
districts, I think it would be a step in
the right direction. Our aim should be
not to encourage the further growth of
industry and population within the city
of Belfast, but to provide for their better
distribution and grouping. I do not
know whether an extension of the city
boundary would meet the situation or not.
Unfortunately, in the past there has been
a strong tendency for people to concentrate in the city. I remember the late
Sir Robert Liddell telling me that it was
almost impossible to keep workers at
Donacloney. He had provided them with
reading rooms, various forms of recreation, sports and other amenities, but they
preferred Belfast, with its crowded conditions in the working-class districts, to
the open spaces, fresh air and healthy
surroundings of the country.
Whether there will be a different outlook in the future it is hard to say. One
cannot prophesy with any degree of
certainty what conditions will prevail in
the coming years. They may be entirely
changed. At the same time, I am afraid
that I cannot agree with the hope expressed by Rev. Professor Corkey that
no more, houses should be erected in the
city of Belfast. While I am strongly in
favour of open spaces, I think the Minister
should consider the advisability of building houses, or allowing houses to be built
on some of the more suitable blitzed sites
in Belfast, say three houses where
formerly five stood. Many workers do
not care to live far from their work.
They do not want to reside in the
country, or even in the suburbs, and
these people are, entitled to consideration.
I know there are legal difficulties in the
way, but these should be insurmountable. If it were within its province, I
think the Trust could dertake with
beneficial results a great slum clearance
campaign in Belfast, and perhaps also in
Derry. I know the Lord Mayor will not
admit there are any slums in Belfast.
am not suggesting that our cities, especially Belfast, are any worse in that respect
than some of the larger cities across the
water, but there are undoubtedly dwellings, so called, which should be taken
down and suitable workers' houses erected
in their places. That would be a progre
sive step and help to ensure social
conditions conducive to health and
happiness.
I trust we shall not have many block
flats such as we see in Glasgow. In my
opinion, nothing beats a separate house
for a family, and in this connection, I

Is that not preventable by insisting that
the houses to be erected shall be designed
and supervised by a competent architect?
The Government should satisfy itself that
the plans and the specifications are sound
and see that the work is properly carried
out. For private enterprise the subsidy
is infinitely preferable to an annual payment.
One hears a lot about the high cost of
land for building purposes.
In the large
cities where land is scarce it is naturally
very high in price, but in the country
towns that is not the case. When an individual wants to build a house in a
country town there is generally not much
difficulty in procuring a site to his liking.
Of course when an authority sets out upon
a scheme its methods of obtaining land
are not so simple. Take land in the
neighbourhood of a country town at the
highish figure of £200 per acre and build
10 houses on this acre, the land will cost
about the same per house as the water
closet-£20.
If there is to be a ceiling fixed for the
price of land should there not also be a
ceiling fixed for the other essentials?
Bricklayers should be encouraged to get
back to their old standard of bricklaying.
The present level is 600 bricks per day on
straight-forward work. I believe that is
condemned by those interested in building.
That has an appreciable effect in connection with the increased cost of building.

houses were built by the Soldiers' and

Mr. BARCLAY: I rise to support the
Second Reading of this Bill. I believe
that the Trust will supply the workingclass people with better and more substantial houses than was done 20 years
ago by private enterprise. I am not
going to occupy the time of the House
very long, but I should like to make one
or two remarks to the Minister. When
houses are being erected by private enterprise and by the Trust there should be
proper supervision in order to ensure that
everything connected with them is done.
in a proper way. Under the last subsidy
that did not take place. Men were
appointed to supervise the building of the
houses who were incapable of doing so.
They had
no practical knowledge of
building at all. They knew it in theory,
but they had no practice. Some of the
floors of the houses that were built under
the previous subsidy are to-day-this has
also happened before now-down in the
cellars. Coming up in the train a man
informed me that the floor of the house
in which he lives went down with him.
His legs went down three or four feet
below the floor and he found himself
standing in 18 inches of water. The
house in question was built on a level with
the road. The walls were built and were
filled all round with earth. There was
no proper drainage system to take the
water away from the side of the walls,
with the result that it has been lying
there since the house was erected. I saw
some of the floors. If there had been a
proper man supervising the work the
drains would have been properly constructed. As it was they were not
jointed with cement. The Ministry
should see to it that capable men are
given the job of supervising the building
of houses for the workers. I have
pleasure in supporting the Second Reading of this Bill.

juncture and try to create for me a
favourable atmosphere in this House.

in entire agreement. He said it would
be a pleasure to me after the passage I

haven of rest. That would be quite all
right up to that point, but I am afraid
if I agreed with some of the suggestions
he made in the latter part of his speech
that haven of rest might be turned into
something about which it would not be
parliamentary to say anything in this
House. (Laughter.)
I agree with Rev. Professor Corkey's
remarks about young married couples. I
am quite sure that Northern Ireland inlaws are as good as those of any other
part of the British Commonwealth of
Nations, but I think it is very desirable
that where possible young married couples
should start out in homes of their own,
even if they are humble homes. And
when we consider that in Belfast alone
over 9,000 marriages took place in a year
we can appreciate the need we have in
Northern Ireland for houses for our
people. That is one of the points I want
to put forward specially to
specially to Senator

to deal later, in their proper order, with

The Rev. Professor Corkey went on to
quote from the report of the Medical
Superintendent Officer of Health
Belfast and stated that the figures for
tuberculosis were twice as high in crowded
In our Ministry we are going to
do everything we possibly can to see that
the unfortunate sufferers from this disease
get the best possible treatment in
sanatoria. At the same time we do
appreciate the point that there is no use
in recklessly spending money in erecting
these sanatoria. I do not want to be
talking about millions. When people talk
about millions they scare me. We do,
however, require to erect sanatoria over
the country to treat this dreaded disease?
After all it is not any use building these
sanatoria and providing treatment for
many cases where cures can be effected if
the unfortunate sufferers have got to
come back into the overcrowded areas
from which they were taken. It is all
due to dirt and overcrowding in many
cases. When I say dirt I do not want

Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Grant]
to be misunderstood.
It is not the fault
of the unfortunate people; it is the
environment in which they live and have
been brought up. They have not had
the opportunity, as Senator Browne said,
of exercising that cleanliness that is next
to godliness. I hope my right hon.

my right hon. Friend-will remember not

a luxury.
I am glad that the Rev. Professor
Corkey gave the Housing Trust his blessing. I should like to refer now to some
points which he said were in his opinion
necessary to improve the Bill. First,
he thought it was a defect that the Bill
did not require those providing houses
to bring their new building schemes into
relation with town planning schemes.
This matter was raised in another place
by a suggested amendment on Committee
stage. If I may I would like to repeat
the answer which I gave and which was
accepted by the mover of the Amendment.
I was able to assure the Members of that
other House, however, that an amendment
was quite unnecessary as the Planning
Acts already provided that no housing
scheme could be undertaken which had
not the approval of the planning authority.
In many cases the local authority sponsoring the housing scheme would itself be
the planning authority. If, however,
the scheme were being put forward by a
rural council in whose area the county
council exercised powers the approval of
the county council as planning authority
would be needed. I hope that answer
will satisfy my right hon. Friend.
The right hon. and learned Senator also
raised the question of our not going step
by step with Great Britain by having a
ceiling on the price of land. This question of land is not a point on which I
want to enter into controversy. This is
a point which affects the whole question
of the acquisition of land for public
purposes. It is a matter which is at present under active consideration and will
be dealt with on a general basis by
legislation.
I should like at this point, if I may,
to strike a personal note and remind
Members of the House that this is
virtually a new Ministry. It is only
about six months in operation. It is not
we
easy to set up a new Ministry, especially
as I have not had much experience in
this sort of thing. We had to start this
Senator
Ministry, as an hon.
of this
House who has since passed away said,
when we had neither money nor marbles
nor chalk to mark a ring. We have not
been idle. There are many things that
Members of this House may think
should have undertaken, but we really
have not had the time or opportunity to
deal with them. I was perfectly sure
in my mind that the first job we had to
tackle was the all-important one of
housing. There are many other questions
arising out of housing which the hon.
Gentleman behind me raised yesterday,
and which have to be given very careful
consideration. I refer, for example, to
water schemes.
I am very anxious to see the land
needed for housing purposes in the
possession of local authorities and the
Trust, but the initiative in acquiring the
land must come from them and not from
me. That is as it should be, because the
local authorities and the Trust are the
people who will be responsible for building houses, and while it is very important that the right type of houses
should be built, it is equally important
that the houses should be built on the
right type of site, so that our ideas as to
providing good houses in healthy districts
will be fulfilled. Personally, I will do
all I can to encourage the advance preparation of sites for post-war housing
purposes.
The right hon. and learned Senator
asked that I should take some steps to
have subsidies graded to meet the needs
of individual local authorities. I recognise that some local authorities are in
a much better position than others to
finance a substantial part of their housing requirements, and grading will be
achieved in practice by the attempts
which I shall make to ensure that each
local authority makes a fair and proper,
but not unreasonable, financial contribution to the cost of housing in its area.
As was rightly said by a Member of
the House yesterday, while a penny rate
in Belfast means a considerable amount
of
money in rates, the same rate in
another district would not raise £25 or
£30. That does not mean that the
people in that particular district are

hon. Friend that not a single house should

wants to live in York Street, and the
Shankill Road man wants to live on the
Shankill Road. Bearing that in mind we
have to see that they get decent, comfortable houses to live in in York Street and
the Shankill Road. I have not referred
to other roads, but it may satisfy Senator
McAllister if I include the Falls Road.
No matter where the roads are we must
have decent houses for the people to live
in, and avoid overcrowding as far as we
possibly can. For that reason I cannot
agree that we should not build any more
houses in the city of Belfast.
The Rev. Professor Corkey also
mentioned the question of open spaces,
and while I agree with him that we should
have open spaces, we have to remember
that at the same time we must have houses
for the people to live in; if there were no
houses there would be no children to play
in the open spaces.
We cannot turn the
city of Belfast into a general recreation
ground. You could not call the families
and the children to the country, because
they would have to come back from the
country to play in these grounds. The
Government would not get any credit for
introducing legislation of that character.

The right hon. and learned Senator
also drew attention to the insertion of the
Fair Wages Clause in the Bill so that I
might have an opportunity of explaining
what success I had had in negotiations
with labour. During the passage of the
Bill through the other House I had resolutions from the trade unions associated
with the building industry and many
deputations from the federations
cerned, indicating that they were wholeheartedly in favour of the Bill and
pledging themselves to do everything they
possibly could to assist the Government
in this effort. I asked them what contribution they were prepared to make.
"It is all right to say you are in sympathy
with the thing, but what are you going
to do to help?" I have their definite
assurance-and I cannot expect anything
more that they will do everything they
possibly can to assist the Government in
their effort to procure houses.
But it does not finish with that, because I pointed out to them that these
houses were for their own particular type
of people, people who could not afford to
pay high rents. I said the only way to
keep rents low was by cheap production.
I did not mean they were to be shoddy or
jerrybuilt houses, but that the cost should
be brought down as far as it reasonably
could be under present circumstances.
They have given that assurance and I have
no reason to doubt that they will carry it
out.

this point will be covered by regulations
and by tenancy agreements. It is not
possible as the right hon. Gentleman
knows to put everything into Acts of
Parliament.

to be given to ex-Service men as set out

near my heart. It has every right to
do so, for I am personally interested in

under Clause 23 I would specify the giving of a preference to ex-Service men.
My power to do that is contained in
Clause 23 (1) which says:
the Ministry may prescribe the class of
persons to whom reasonable preference
should be given in the selection of
tenants or occupiers of such accommodation.
The reason that I did not put a direct
preference to ex-Service men in the Bill
as originally drafted was that I felt that
matters of this kind were quite capable
of being left to regulations.
It is perfectly true that on the first
Order Paper in the Committee stage in
the other House there was an Amendment
which distinctly put forward to the
Government that preference should be
given to ex-Service men. It did not say
anything more than that. I was
worried about this Amendment, because
if it was in the Bill it would make it very
difficult for the regulations to be made
covering that part. It only said ex-
Service men. It did not say anything
about the men of the Mercantile Marine
or the Merchant Navy who, I think, are
also entitled to preference. I was
worried about it and consulted with hon.
and right hon. Members in the other
House whose names were backing this
Amendment. After a good deal of
trouble-not that there was any
trouble between us as to what we wanted
it was to get the right words—we got
this agreed Amendment and the Government were perfectly delighted to accept it.
Then to be told that we only accepted it
at the point of the bayonet! Perhaps
the right hon. Member's idea of a
bayonet and mine are different. On this
particular question, at any rate, I think
the Government were entitled to some
credit for the action they took in having
this Amendment drawn up and accepted
unanimously by the whole House.

hon. Senator should make the type of remark which he has made about prospective members of this proposed body.
They are going to have a very important
task to carry out. The gentlemen whom
I hope to persuade to undertake the duty
will do so with the full knowledge that
their work is going to have the searchlight of public opinion very much directed
upon it; it is going to be a task of great
difficulty and magnitude.
Their services are to be given without
fee or reward, and the task must obviously
be entrusted only to persons who have
both energy and ability and a deep sense
of the need for public service. I suggest
that the hon. Senator and those other
Members who may wish to talk in the
same strain, should defer their criticisms
until the members of the Trust have been
selected and appointed.

Some of those men are going to make very

interest, and I am very glad to have the

Trust.

to build houses on those sites.

In regard to Clause 19, I do not agree
that this is the introduction of party
politics. I know Senator McAllister is a
great party man, and he knows that I am
a great party man, but this question of
housing, as I have already said, is not
one of party politics. The houses that
are to be built under this measure by the
local authorities and by the Trust are
houses that will be built by the money
of either the taxpayers or the ratepayers.
Under those circumstances I hold, and I
am certain I am right, that we have no
right to ask the taxpayers and the ratepayers of this country to build houses for
people who belong to another country. I
am not going to talk about Southern
Ireland or anything of the sort, but it is
our duty to provide houses for our own
people as our people are paying for these
houses.

Mr. GRANT: I suppose I should be
just as soft a person as anyone else to fix
it on. (Laughter.) The hon. Member
went on to point out that there was no
machinery for fixing rents. The machinery is in the hands of the local authorities already, and the Trust are going to
work in co-operation with the local
authorities. There will be no question
of one authority charging a different rent.
from another authority for the same type
of house. One of the reasons that there
is no definite statement made in the Bill
in regard to the fixing of rents is that
we do not know ourselves what the rents
will be. The subsidy which
giving for the 40 years' period, and for
which the local authorities will be paying
their contribution, is only, as my right
hon. Friend the Leader of the House said
in his Second Reading speech, a token
subsidy. The houses that are built up to
1947 will get the benefit of that subsidy,
and either at that time or before it, if
they find it possible to go into the question of what these houses cost, it is
absolutely certain that the Government
will increase the amount already mentioned in the Bill. On that the rent will
be based, but I want to make it quite
clear that this Bill has only been brought
in to provide houses to be let at a rent
that can be paid by people who cannot
afford to pay a higher rent and who cannot, either by hire purchase or any other
means, buy their own house.
I do not want it to be taken that I am
against people buying their own house. I.
think it is a very laudable desire that
persons should own their own house, but
I do not want them to buy a house without a bathroom. I want them to have a
good house, but we have not yet arrived
at the time when the housing shortage
has been overtaken and when we are in a
position to allow houses to be sold,
although the sooner that time arrives.
the better. At the moment my desire
and the desire of everyone else is to find
houses for the poor people who are living
under conditions that are an absolute
disgrace to humanity. Many cases have
been instanced of people who are living
under intolerable conditions. How can
we have a healthy community under such
conditions. We are not at present in, a
position to determine what the rents of
these houses will be, but they will
certainly be fixed at a figure these people
will be able to pay.
Senator Browne said the solution of the
housing problem would be brought about
by private enterprise. No one disagrees
with him on that point, though I have
been told from many quarters that I am
opposed to private enterprise.
I am
nothing of the sort.
I do realise that we
will never completely solve this housing
problem until we get private enterprise
started on the job, but at the moment
housing costs are so high that it is practically impossible for the Imperial
Chancellor to determine what subsidy
should be given to private enterprise. It
is not to be expected that people are
going to invest their money in house
building unless they get a fairly substantial subsidy from the Government. But
if that cannot be done it does not mean
that we have got to sit down and tell the
people who are living under these deplorable conditions that we can do nothing
for them until private enterprise comes
into the market. We have a moral
responsibility towards these people, and
it is for this reason that this Bill is
brought in.
I would also like to remind hon. Members that between the years 1919 and
1923 there were only 1,000 houses built
in Northern Ireland, even with the subsidy. We cannot look forward to that
occurring again. We must see to it that
we get on with the job, at least as soon
as the war in Europe is over, and I am
hoping that even before then we will get
some building done. I think we should
see to it that what happened after the
last war is certainly not going to happen
after this war.
I do not know whether or not I rightly
understood Senator Browne to say he was
glad to know the Trust would build houses
and shops and lay out recreation grounds,
but if he did I want to point out to him

Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Second Reading
4 JANUARY 1945
that the Trust is not going to do anything
of the sort. The Trust is going to build
houses for the people, but what we have
in mind is that if the Trust goes outside
and takes a large area of ground on which
it sets up a sort of communal centre under
modern conditions, and if, after building
houses, the Trust has sufficient land to
provide sites for shops and churches and
recreation grounds they can lease that
land to people who would be willing to
take it for those purposes. But under
no circumstances is the Trust going to
undertake any of these things.

working for wages, or working at a
trade without employing others are
not subject to the £8 a week condition.
The object is to bring them up as near as
possible to the existing legislation regarding unemployment and health insurance. Someone may ask, "Why do you
not insert those words in the Bill?"
The reason is that there is so much
talked about social insurance and White
Papers that we do not know whether
these things will be carried on under the
same name, and we thought it better to
protect ourselves by referring to it in the
way we have in this Bill.
I was rather interested to hear Senator
Major Boyle say you cannot build houses
without bricks. I was in his ancient,
historic and apostolic city not long ago.
I saw quite a lot of houses that were not
built with bricks. They were built with
stone, and if we cannot get the materials
that are ordinarily used to construct
houses the Trust will experiment with
different types of materials and houses.
After all, we might have all the bricks
that are necessary, but, as the hon. and
gallant Senator rightly said, the availability of labour for laying the bricks.
might be a drawback to going ahead.
You cannot expect the local authorities
to spend the ratepayers' money on experimenting, and therefore the Trust will do
that to a limited extent. I did not agree
with Senator Rev. Professor Corkey when
he quoted Sir William Beveridge and
asked us to think in millions, but not to
waste the money. Then he quoted Sir
William Beveridge as saying that a man
should dig holes and fill them up again.
Surely that is waste.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: Sir William
Beveridge says it is not.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: We do not
all agree with the economic theories of
Sir William Beveridge.

Mr. GRANT: I have not a brick in
my possession. Senator Boyle went on
to say that he was not clear why we should
wait for a formula from the other side
for building purposes. I do not know
exactly what he means by formula in this
case, because if he means getting private
enterprise into the field to build houses
a formula would be a subsidy from the
Chancellor of the Exchequer, and we
certainly could not, as was made very
clear yesterday by the right hon. Senator,
Mr. Leslie, take anything from the
Imperial contribution. We have no
power and no desire to do it. He did
amaze me by saying if we were going to
get these houses built at an economic
price that bricklayers would have to pass
the level of laying 600 bricks. It certainly
is information to me that bricklayers are
laying 600 bricks a day. I would not be
so despondent about the situation if I
thought that that was really the actual
figure at the moment. I certainly would
feel a lot happier.
He
He rightly went on to praise what had
been done by the Soldiers' and Sailors'
Land Trust after the last war and the
type of houses that were built.
realised that that was a reserved service
-an Imperial question-but I want to
join with him in hoping that another
scheme of that nature will be carried out.
Senator Barclay raised the all-important
question of the proper supervision of
building. Of course I believe that a job
can only be done properly if there is the
right type of craftsmen to do it, and I
know my Department will do its utmost
to carry this out. I should hate to fall
down on this question. I was rather
amazed by Senator Dobbs likening me to
a fairy. I have been called many things,
but this is the first time I have ever been
called a fairy. (Laughter.) He referred to my waving a wand and building
houses. I cannot see myself flitting about
waving a wand and producing houses.
(Laughter.)
I do thoroughly agree with him about
the question of building houses near to
the isolated cottages all over the country.
That is just one of the objects I have in
mind in connection with this Bill, because
it is hard to get essential services up to
those people who like their nearest neighbour to be about a mile away. I cannot
understand people like that. If we can
get the right spirit, which I believe we
will in the local authorities, the position
will be that where there are a number of
cottages together the number will be increased by the building of the proper type
of houses, so that all essential services
will be available to all those people. I
think that is the desire of every one of us.
This debate has clearly indicated that
there is no doubt about our need of
houses. Everybody is united on that,
but there has been considerable difference
of opinion regarding the means to the end.
All along, I myself have been convinced
that the legislation which is at present
before us is the best way in which, under
present circumstances, we can secure, the
speedy erection of houses. The subsidies
offered bring our housing finance into line
with that of Scotland. This is the best
we can do at the moment.
I am very hopeful about the part the
Housing Trust can play in helping those
local authorities which require and wish
to have assistance. Backed by the unanimous support of my colleagues in the
Cabinet, my determination in this matter
has, therefore, been based on a sound
belief that in the end the public will be
satisfied with the results With this
legislation we are saying to our people,
here is something worth-while on which
we can make a start. We do not want
to waste any time. Further measures
will be brought forward as soon as ever
possible. I hope the House will give this
Bill its Second Reading.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in page
3, after line 26, to insert as a new subclause :
For the purpose of this section the
expression "new house" means a
building, constructed or adapted for
use as a dwelling-house, provided under
a scheme submitted to, and approved
by, the Ministry after the passing of
this Act and completed to the satisfaction of the said Ministry.
As I think the Committee will recognise the Amendment on the Order
Paper is an example of what is sometimes spoken of as putting something on
the Paper in order, perhaps, to get information. The point is, the Committee
may remember, that I did ask certain
questions about this Clause when the
matter was before the House
on the
Second Reading, and I did not get an
answer, because, as I understood afterwards, I spoke so rapidly that I outpaced the pencil of the right hon.
Gentleman, the Minister of Health and
Local Government, who was taking notes.
Therefore, perhaps, that is the main
reason why this particular form of words
has been put on the Order Paper this
afternoon.
I think it is worth while to look at the
Amendment in detail to find out whether,
as it stands on the Order Paper, it does
fill the bill and cover what the public who
has to work this Bill, or suffer under it
or enjoy it, may want to know. Let us
break the Amendment up a little and
look at it. First of all previous Acts of
Parliament of this description-I am
thinking particularly of the Act of 1944have given us some sort of idea of when a
building or whatever it is, is to start. Your
building has to be under a scheme to get
the grant sanctioned under "the Act."
Words of that sort are generally put in,
but in the Bill before us there is nothing
about that. We are not told when the
building is to start. It is to be done in
pursuance of a scheme, and it is right to
ask which scheme, otherwise we may have,
as I hope we shall, thirty or forty schemes
floating about together. I think we ought
to be assured that before the local authorities get down to ask for a grant everybody will know what the scheme is. That
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Committee
11 JANUARY 1945
is what I called the other day the
terminus a quo.
It seems to me that the local authority
should choose the scheme it is going on
and say so, otherwise will have someyou
thing in the nature of a switch; you will
have a local authority putting up its
scheme and then trying to switch over to
another. I think people would not know
where they are, and there is the possibility of the local authority itself being
put in that position. That is the first
point. We do not know at the moment,
of course, how many schemes there are.
Possibly the Ministry knows, but I do
not think anybody else knows.
The second point is in connection with
this word "new." The word "new" as
applied to housing is new in this connection and I want to know what the Minister
means by a new house. Does he mean,
as I imagine he does, a house which has
never previously been occupied ? That
would be the ordinary acceptation of the
term. Does it also include an adapted
house? It might be that the local authority has bought a house from somebody
and either adapted it or taken it on as
it is and then come forward and say,
"Here is a house which we have provided." There is nothing to stop that.
Although I have put the word "adapted'
in this Amendment for consideration I
do not think it is the Government's proposal that a house that has only been
adapted should rank as a new house.
They should tell us whether it does or
does not.
The Committee will remember that,
under the Trust part of the scheme, there
is distinct power given to adapt a house,
but in the first part, which deals with
local authorities, there is no specific reference to adaptation, although there are
some powers in some of the building Acts
at the back moving in that direction. I
think we ought to be told definitely, when
the Government come to reply to the
Amendment, whether they do
adapted houses or freshly built houses.
mean
The expression about these houses is
not that they shall be built by the local

and the two things are not quite the same.
I think we want to know about those provided houses, and whether a house is to

not.

as quite a lot of us have had at one

The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I have some difficulty
in dealing with this matter, because the
right hon. Gentleman went a very long
way on other subjects which do not
appear to have much bearing on the
Amendment. First of all I will deal with
the point as to the scheme or schemes.
It is the Government's intention when
and if this Bill becomes an Act that all
schemes will be under the Act except, of
course, such houses as have already been
allocated to local authorities under the
Act of 1944. Under that Act the
number of houses is limited and they have
practically all been allocated. Once this
Bill becomes law all further schemes will
come under it.
As to the second point in regard to the
definition of a new house, the Government are not anxious to insert a definition of a new house in this Bill. If they
do not insert a definition, then the words
"a new house" will bear their natural
meaning. Having said that, it is clear
that an adapted house is not a new house.
was
The third point was in regard to the
period during which a grant would be
paid, and whether the Ministry would
satisfy themselves that the house
satisfactorily constructed. In the first
place the Ministry will satisfy themselves
that the house is satisfactorily constructed.
It may be that in very many cases the
grant will not be paid until the house is
completed, but there may be cases where
it would be desirable to pay a portion of
the grant during construction. I understand that the Ministry will satisfy themselves that the part of the building to
which that portion of the grant applies
has been satisfactorily carried out before
the money is paid. I think that answers
all the right hon. Gentleman's points.
Mr. LESLIE: What about the shell.
of the building?
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg your
pardon. In the case of self-contained
flats each self-contained flat will get a
grant, but the whole building when
completed would not attract
an extra
grant. There will not be anything extra
for the shell of a building. In regard to
the bathroom, it is simply a
case of
whether the design in the case of a local
authority is approved by the Ministry
or not. There may conceivably be cases
especially in rural districts where there is
no available running water in which a
bathroom will not be insisted upon, but it
will be very rare that the designs do not
contain a bathroom. I would remind the
right hon. Gentleman that he must not be
misled by the subsidy figures in the Bill.
They are only token figures. I fear that
the eventual figures to be written in will
be substantially larger when we know.
the expense incurred. I think I have
given an explanation of all the points.
raised.

Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move, after the word "shall" in the third
line, to insert the words "in consultation
with the Planning Commission."
This is a short Amendment and should
commend itself to the Committee.
It
will be remembered that in 1942 the
Government appointed a Planning
Advisory Board and Planning Commission for the North of Ireland, and the
purpose of this Amendment is to give
that Planning Commission the opportunity to do the work for which it was
appointed two years ago. Now, what was
that work? I find that in 1942 a Member
of the then Cabinet, speaking of this
Commission, said:
It is their business to assist and
advise in the preparation of local plans
and to secure the co-ordination of
these, so that ultimately one consistent,
coherent plan for the whole Province
can be produced.
This Amendment will give to the Planning Commission an opportunity to
intervene at an early stage when the
local authorities are making out their
plans for the lay-out of the new houses.
A further fact is that our Government
already has legislated along these lines,
giving to the Planning Commission somewhat similar, though more limited,
functions in an Act which was passed by
this House in the early months of 1942.
There it is stated that the local authorities shall act after consultation with the
Planning Commission. Now this short
Amendment of mine extends that function
to the Planning Commission and gives the
local authorities a chance of calling them
in at an early stage, and I suggest that
the calling in of the Planning Commission at an early stage would result in
obviating disagreement or conflict about
plans. We have a body actually at work
along these lines already, and that is the
Planning Commission, and as far back as
1942 we were told by the same Member
of the Government, speaking about this
Commission, that
the Planning Commission has made.
good progress in the more technical side
of the work, which, as I have explained, is its peculiar concern.
It seems to me plain common sense that,
when you have a Planning Commission
already in existence doing this technical
work here, there and everywhere all over
the Province in order to frame a coordinated plan, this Commission should
have an opportunity of intervening
at an early stage in the work of the
local authority which is engaged in
making out a scheme for its own area. It
seems to me only sensible. This is not a
drastic Amendment. I merely ask that
this Commission should be consulted. I
do not ask that the local authority should
make out a town plan. I do not ask
the Planning Commission to make out a
town plan for that area. I do not ask
them even to develop the area. All
I ask is that they be consulted. The
local authority will have the last word,
subject, of course, to the approval of the
Ministry.
It was said by the Minister in reference
to this suggestion of mine, when replying
to the opening debate on this Bill, that
all schemes would be subject to the
planning authority. But what is the
planning authority? The planning
authority is simply the local authority,
whether it be an urban authority or a
rural authority, and unfortunately I
think it is true to say that the planning
authority is not always an authority on
planning--
-
Major PERCEVAL MAXWELL:
Hear, hear.
Rev. Professor CORKEY- -as our
past experience of building, especially
between the two wars has shown. For
these reasons I think the local authority
should consult the Planning Commission.
before they send forward their plans to
the Ministry to have them finally
approved, and accordingly I move this
Amendment.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT : The Government objection to this Amendment is
entirely on practical grounds. The Planning Commission's job is to consider
planning in the wide aspect for the whole
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Committee
11 JANUARY 1945
Province or large areas, and the personnel of the Commission are to a very
large extent the technical officers of the
different local authorities. Now the idea
of the Ministry in dealing with this matter
is that the local authorities should be
allowed to put forward these schemes
which in many cases will be more or less
of a routine nature and the Ministry
should consult the Planning Commission
where points of difficulty arise or where
the local plan cuts across the main plan
of the Province. In other words their
intention is, apart from the wide
functions I have spoken of, to
them in a sense as an appeal court, or, if
you like, a panel of experts who can be
consulted.
use
If the Amendment is passed every
single plan will have to go to the Commission and they will have to spend time
on it. We are afraid if you do that the
whole machinery may break down and
plans will be held up continually unless
the Planning Commission were in constant
session. They cannot do that because
most of them will be busy in their local
areas doing the actual planning. It is not
through any wish to handicap planning as
planning but simply that we. believe that
this is the only practical way of carrying
it out that I am obliged to resist this
Amendment.

of the Imperial House of Commons.
do not think I need say very much more

I

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: This is a very
interesting Amendment. Dealing with
the first matter of excluding Members of
the Imperial House of Commons, there'
does not appear to be any special reason
why Members of the Imperial House of
Commons should be excluded. It is extremely improbable that we should wish
to appoint one of them because they are
very busy men, but between saying that
and excluding them by statute there is a
great deal of difference. It is not usual
in this Parliament to take any legislative
notice of Members of the Imperial House,
and there is just a question as to the
constitutional propriety of placing them
under a disqualification, but I would not
press that point very hard.
The object of excluding Members of
both Houses of this Parliament was in the
case of Members of the House of Commons that they would be dealing in
Parliament with the financial affairs of
the Trust, and in the case of Members of
both Houses that the Government attach
great importance to both Houses having
complete freedom to criticise the administration of the Trust. It would be
embarrassing to do that when a Member
of one of the Houses was a member of the
Trust.
There is also the question of Ministerial responsibility. As long as no
Member of either House can be a member of the Trust the position is quite
clear. It rests with the Minister of
Health and Local Government who can
answer questions in the House. Supposing there was another Member of either
House who in a sense could speak for the
Trust it would be quite embarrassing and
in some degree a diminution of the
Minister's responsibility. For all those
reasons, I am afraid I must resist the
Amendment on the grounds that both
Houses should be free to criticise-the
House of Commons particularly being free
to criticise financial matters-and that
the Minister should bear sole responsi-

of the Trust. In the case of the Members of the Imperial House there seems

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Here again
is one of those questions where practical
difficulties may arise in the future. At
the present moment the Government have
no intention whatever of appointing to
the Trust anyone who is in any way, as
one might say, part of the Government
machine. That, of course, includes civil
servants. At the same time you cannot
foresee whether in future it might not be
necessary to have them on it.
To take
a case in point, I am advised that it
would be extremely difficult to wind up
the Trust without for a short period
putting one or more civil servants on to
it. I understand that that is a difficulty
which has arisen in other parts of the
United Kingdom when companies and
trusts of this kind have been wound up.
I do not pretend to understand the point
myself, but I am accepting it as being so.
For that reason alone, I think it is undesirable to make a far-reaching rule, but
I would ask the Committee to accept the
assurance of the Government that as far
as they can see things at present there is
no intention of putting civil servants or
ex-civil servants on to the Trust.
The hon. and gallant Senator has said
there might be some civil servants with
particular qualifications whom it might be
desirable to put on later. The Amendment would not prevent that, but it
would prevent an active civil servant, and
cases might arise in which it would be
almost a necessity to have one, though
the Government see no immediate chance
of that position arising. They are prepared to give an assurance that there is
no intention at present of appointing a
civil servant, but they do not want their
hands to be tied. It may be of interest
to the Committee to know that in actual
fact the Scottish Housing Association at
different periods of its career has always
had some members or ex-members of the
staff of the Scottish Department of
Health on the Board, and at one time I
believe there was actually a majority of
such members. While it is not a precedent that we intend to follow, it shows
that it was necessary to have them on a
very successful body of the same kind.
This is, as I said on the Second Reading, to a very large extent experimental,
and we do not want to have our hands
tied too tightly when we are making experiments or we may drop the beaker and
spill the acid all over ourselves. The
reason for the Amendment is fully realised
by the Government and they have every
intention of living up, as it were, to the
spirit of the Amendment.

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Because the

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move to leave
out sub-clause (4). This is really a more
important Amendment. The proposal,
as I see it, is to give this new Trust
certain powers of compulsory acquisition.
These are the powers which hitherto have
only been enjoyed either by Parliament
itself, or by some Corporation acting
under the direct authority of Parliament
or by a local authority, shall we say a rural
district council, which has compulsory
powers to acquire a plot for a labourer's
cottage. Those compulsory powers on
the part of a rural district council are
exercised less and less every year, for
they nearly always get the plots by agreement. It seems to me that it is essential
for the present, at all events, that this
new Trust should not have what I must
describe as an extraordinary power. It
is a tremendous power to give.
Under it this Trust is acting, as the
Committee will remember and as we have
just been reminded, practically under the
direction of the Ministry. It is almost a
sub-Department of the Ministry, because
the Ministry's hand is over it at every
stage. Acting under the Ministry it can
go anywhere apparently and take anything, and the only form of appeal that
the person whose property is to be
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Committee
11 JANUARY 1945
acquired seems to have is to the Ministry
itself. That is a position I really cannot
stand for. Look at it again for a minute.
One of the suggestions underlying this
Trust is that it can go into the country
and acquire a relatively large tract of
and for the establishment of model
villages. If you are going to establish a
model village in some county you may
be running over half-a-dozen farms, and
you know it is a strong measure to have
compulsory powers to acquire those farms,
especially when the body in question is
not directly appointed by Parliament,
and is not elected by any local government authority.
Again, you may have the same difficulty
here that you had in England. There
have been complaints in England again
and again that in the acquisition of land
for various purposes the very best agricultural land has been taken when there
was probably some other land that would
have done as well. Against such proceedings here there would be no appeal.
Similar complaints have been made in
our own area as regards the acquisition of
land for distinctly military purposes, and
though the prosecution of the war has had
to take first place, some of the land so
taken has been first class agricultural
land, which could have been used to very
great advantage in the production of
food. In other cases, the interest of the
agricultural section of the population has
been supported by the Ministry of Agriculture, and sometimes the military requirements have even been put, not in
the second place exactly, but have been
diverted into a side track and land found
for them elsewhere.
Again you have no appeal of any sort.
I know it is easy to say that the Trust
will be concerned with compulsion only
where it cannot get the land in any other
way. As to that, I think we should say,
"Let us wait until it happens." If the
Trust, or the Minister who is going to
appoint the Trust, cannot proceed without compulsion I can only say that that
argues a very considerable fear on the
part of the Ministry that the methods of
the Trust will not be of that considerate
character which is essential if it is to
work with success. I am afraid it is the
ordinary departmental suggestion that
power should be given to wave a big stick
at the first moment; in fact, to draw the
sword before you blow the horn, instead
of the other way about. You will not
get public goodwill or public confidence.
that way.
It is very much better to leave this subclause out now, and then if that difficulty
does, in fact, arise let the Trust through
the Government come back to the House
and tell its story and ask for further permission. At the moment I cannot possibly
stand for this. We have got to remember,
if we pass this, we give the farmers of the
country-and I am speaking more of the
country for the moment than the towncause for disquiet. No man will know
whether his land is going to be taken or
not. That can only result in disquiet,
and in some cases I think a deterrent to
the agricultural effort. A man is not
going to spend money on improving his
land or trying to grow crops if without
any warning or without any proper appeal
he is liable to have his land pounced upon
by a Trust, for which probably he has no
great respect. As I say, the only thing
is to cut it out altogether, and in the
future we will find out in practice
whether the Trust is a strong enough
body to be entrusted with power of this
kind, and if so the Minister can come
back to Parliament and ask for it. At
the moment I am opposed to the granting
of these powers as set out in the subclause, and I beg to move that the entire
sub-clause be left out of the Bill.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid
the Government regard this sub-clause as
absolutely vital to the Bill. I think the
right hon. Gentleman in his speech was
making in the main a case against the
compulsory acquisition of land, in which
I must say I personally in many ways
agree with him, but we are now unfortunately dealing with one of the cases
where there must be compulsory powers.
What are these compulsory powers in this
case? They are not the powers of the
Trust, but of the Minister, and the
Minister is responsible to Parliament. In
most cases, though there might be an
occasional case when neither House is
sitting, it will be possible to bring a
question of acquisition up in the House,
as has been done on other occasions both
here and in England, before a Vesting
Order is made, and it can always be
brought up and the Vesting Order revoked.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Not by the party
concerned.
