Committee
THE SENATE
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: No, but by
his representative in Parliament
That should be good enough for him.
Some compulsory powers, unfortunately,
are necessary, otherwise there may be delay or an extreme case of exploitation of
the Government by an unreasonable enhancement of the price. The Trust will
select the site in all cases, and possibly
the site will be got by agreement. In
99 cases out of 100 it will be got by
agreement. If the Trust fails to secure
agreement, the question of the sites goes
to the Minister, and the Minister is responsible to Parliament. It is he who
makes the Vesting Order, not the Trust.
It is he who exercises compulsion, not
the Trust. Really, you cannot have anything more democratic than the power
vested in the Minister, who is always
answerable to Parliament. It is much
more democratic than leaving it to a local
authority, which is elected on a narrower
franchise.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I cannot accept
the explanation given by the right hon.
Gentleman to justify this very drastic
sub-clause. I agree with the right hon.
and learned Senator that it is contrary to
every idea of constitutional law. I would
say this to the Leader of the House-that
if you want to gain confidence for this
Bill and for the Government, the only
way to do it is to leave it to the courts
to decide. any question that is in dispute.
I do not understand what the Leader of
the House means by saying that where
land is taken compulsorily any grievance
can be brought before Parliament,
because the Minister is responsible to
Parliament. It is the owner of the. land
who is concerned, because he suffers the
loss, and he has no assurance that he can
bring his grievance before Parliament.
Therefore, I think the explanation given
by the right hon. Gentleman will not hold
water for one moment.
This is a sub-clause that cannot be.
justified, and the Committee should resist
it with all its strength. Any man whose
land is taken from him compulsorily
should have the right to appeal to an
independent court in which he has
confidence..
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I am
not quite sure where the Trust may
operate, but if it is to operate in Belfast
there is no doubt that it is very necessary
to give them this power. There is so
little land in Belfast to acquire for the
purpose of building, and those who have
land are very anxious to retain it. If
the housing of the people is priority No.
1, as I maintain it should be, nothing
should be allowed to stand in the way of
building houses, and nothing should stand
in the way of acquiring land, even if it
has to be acquired compulsorily. I think
it is very necessary that this sub-clause
should be in the Bill, especially in the
case of a city like. Belfast, or even in the
case of smaller towns outside Belfast.
For these reason, I would be in favour
of retaining this sub-clause.
Mr. LESLIE: Perhaps one might ask
the Leader of the House. whether the
Government would be prepared to accept
an Amendment limiting the operation of
this sub-clause to the city of Belfast.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid
we could not accept any limitation to this
sub-clause. I would ask the Committee
to realise that this sub-clause is essential
to the working of the Bill. All the objections that I have heard are objections
not to the. Trust, which, after all, will
only select the land, but to the Vesting
Order procedure, which the House has
again and again inserted in other measures. The Minister makes the order,
not the Trust, just as he does under other
Acts. The power to say "yes or "no,"
and the power to compel does not rest
with the Trust, which merely selects the
land to be acquired. The Government
regard this power as being absolutely
essential to the successful working of the
Bill.

Rt. Hon. Sir Roland Nugent

desirable that something should be put

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause 2 (a), page 12, line 4, to leave out
the word "compulsorily."
When I first put down this Amendment to leave out the word "compulsorily" it was in the hope that the
Committee would have expunged the
whole of sub-clause 4 of Clause 14,
but that hope was not realised. The
Committee preferred for the sake of
one per cent. in cases in which it was
applicable to maintain this power. All
the same my objection to this still continues. It seems to me it is all very well
to put into the Housing Fund and take
out of it money required to pay for land
compulsorily acquired, but all the while
there is nothing said about land acquired
otherwise than compulsorily. That is
why I want the word left out.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Sir Roland Nugent): As the right
hon. Gentleman has mentioned, this was
to a large extent consequential on an
Amendment which the Committee rejected on the last occasion. We do
attach importance to maintaining the
word "compulsorily" here, because it is
to make quite clear that there is a Fund
to which claims for compensation in
respect of land acquired by the Trust
under the Vesting Order procedure
can be attached. That is the
reason I am resisting the Amendment.
I think it is a point of reasonable substance to maintain the word.
Mr. LESLIE: My point is not met.

acquired by agreement or compulsorily
that would meet my point of view.

put one forward. I think myself, subject
to correction by those who know better,
that those words cover the matter.
Mr. LESLIE: On those grounds I ask

the matter may be looked into.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause (1), page 12, line 16, to leave out
the word "greatest" and insert the words
"very great."

This is certainly a matter of a trivial
character. There are two objections to
the use of the word "greatest. Firstly,
I do not think it is classical English,
because you cannot have greatest in more
than one area at the same time. That is
trivial, but again if you leave it as it
stands you put on the unfortunate
Minister the onus of deciding where the
need is greatest. He has to stand the
racket of a possible attack from an area
which he has not selected. It is leaving
him open to be shot at by some area which
complains that another area has been preferred. I think you would get over all
that by accepting the Amendment.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: This is a
very nice point, but we have to look at
the matter from the point of view where
the need for houses is greatest. The need
at present is very great everywhere, and
the object of the words in the Bill is to
ensure that the Ministry and the Trust
will direct their attention to those areas
where the need is even greater than in
others. I agree that the word "greatest"
is not perfect English, but the idea is the
selection of the worst places first. I think
the idea is worth preserving in the Bill.
Mr. LESLIE: I think we will leave it
at that. It does not really matter.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

CLAUSE 19 (Residence qualification
for tenants).
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie:
At the end of the Clause, insert in page
14, after line 12, "Provided that for the
purposes of paragraph (a) of this section
absence from the United Kingdom on the
part of a person normally resident therein and due to the present war shall be
reckoned as residence in the United
Kingdom."
Mr. LESLIE : In view of another
Amendment on the Order Paper in the
name of the Leader of the House there
would be no object in moving this Amendment.
Amendment not moved.
Mr. LENNON: I beg to move to leave
out the Clause. Clause 19 directs that
the Trust shall not accept any person as
tenant of any housing accommodation
provided by them under this Act unless
such
shall have been resident
person
within the United Kingdom for a period
of seven years, or shall have been born
in Northern Ireland. This is a Clause
which in its present form is a violation
of natural justice.
as
This country geographically is one
country and it remains so, and the race
inhabiting it is the Irish race. The Irish
race has a right to live in and reside in
any part of Ireland which it might select,
and this Clause, if passed in its present
form, is a Clause which directs compulsorily the body known as the Trust.
being created by this Bill-directs them
in the most mandatory language that
they must not accept any person
tenant in accommodation provided by
them under this Bill who has not been
resident within the United Kingdom for
seven years. That is to say, if we interpret it as such it is a specific direction
to the Trust not to accept any person as
tenant of any such housing accommodation who shall for seven years preceding
the date of the tenancy have been
resident in the other portion of Ireland
constituted of the Twenty-six counties.
It has been said of previous Acts when
they were passing through both this
House and another place that there are
reasons behind this which are financial.
I cannot accept that as being the explanation of the insertion of this extraordinarily bad Clause in a Bill which
otherwise I might have been prepared to
welcome. If it were financial, then one
would have expected that the direction
should have been to the Trust not to
accept any person as tenant of any such
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Lennon]
housing accommodation who could not
pay the rent. That is not the direction.
The direction is that where the tenant
or proposed tenant is as solvent as it is
possible for any person to be, that person is not to be taken as tenant or proposed as tenant if he has not been
resident. The resident may be financially
in such straits as to be completely unable
to pay the rent.
The answer which has been previously
given when similar Clauses were inserted
in other Bills that the reason for them
was financial is obviously fallacious. I
can therefore only come to the conclusion
that the reason behind it is ignorance.
I use the word advisedly, and I use it
deliberately, because the Irish race being
a race, as a whole, it includes those resident in the Six Counties as well as those
resident in the Twenty-six, and as well
as those members of that race spread
abroad through the entire globe. Here
in the Six Counties an insult is being
directed at the Irish race by a fractional
portion of the Irish race itself. It has been
usual for many centuries that this race
should be insulted by another race, but
now the insult comes from an infinitesimal fraction of the Irish race
itself. The net result of this Clause, if
it is to be kept in this Bill, is that the
Trust is commanded to refuse to accept
an Irish man born in Ireland who has
lived in Ireland, simply because that person has not lived within the ambit of the
Six Counties, and is to prefer, so far as
one can see from the interpretation of the
Clause as it now stands, a person who may
be a member of any race under the sun,
but who has lived in the United Kingdom
for a period of seven years.
•
One would naturally have thought
that the designers and framers of this
Bill might have been concerned with
perhaps the health of the population and
that a prospective tenant might be asked
to show the Trust that he and his household were free from contamination by
some virus of disease which would affect
the property afterwards or affect those
living in it. The Trust are not SO
directed. The Trust are directed to
see that they are free from any virus
or germ that might attach to them
by reason of the fact of their having not
been resident in the United Kingdom for
seven years or not born in some place in
Northern Ireland. What are they afraid
of? Are they afraid of the germ of freedom? Will a person resident in the
Twenty-six Counties bring with him something utterly repugnant to our ideas of
life? One can only imagine that this
Bill has been mutilated-has been
destroyed-by the insertion of this Clause,
for the usual reason, blind fanatical
hatred of everything which for centuries
gone past has been the pleasure and pride
of those who were the forefathers of the
framers of this Clause. I protest most
strongly against this Clause being inserted
in this Bill and I beg to move its rejection.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid
I must resist this Amendment. I will
not follow the hon. Gentleman who
moved the rejection into the mysticism of
race, but I would ask the Committee to
accept my assurance that this Clause is
not based on racial hatred or fanaticism.
It is simply a practical matter. There is
a shortage of houses and somebody has to
get a house first. These houses will be
provided by a very heavy Government
subsidy, and it does not seem to the
Government unreasonable that those who
have been resident in the North of Ireland and paid taxes and rates, or those
who have been resident in the United
Kingdom and have borne to some extent
the burden and heat of the war-which
has caused the shortage of houses-should
have first choice. It does not seem to me
to be fanatical but plain common sense.
Anyhow we are looking after our
folk first and I am not going to apologise
for that.
Own
There is one point which it was intended
to deal with in a more general Act but
which the Government now consider
should be dealt with in this Bill. It is
the case of a man who although not
previously resident in Northern Ireland
or born in Northern Ireland has been
serving in His Majesty's Forces or in the
Mercantile Marine. In other words
one of those who have borne the burden
and heat of the war. Under existing
Housing Acts that man could not be
considered as resident in the United
Kingdom if he was serving abroad. It
would be obviously unfair that a man
seated comfortably at Aldershot should
have the advantage while a man who
spent four years in Burma should not.
That being so I am moving at a later
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Committee
16 JANUARY 1915
stage a new Clause which will give these
men the right on account of service.
Beyond that I am afraid I could not
make any concession. I would ask the
Committee to accept the assurance that
there is no political or sectarian feeling.
It is simply that a selection has to be
made. We do feel that our own folk
should have first claim.
Mr. LESLIE: It seems to me that
The hon.
there is a third point of view.
Senator in front of me (Mr. Lennon) has
put forward one, and the right hon.
Gentleman the Leader of the House put
forward another. It seems to me there
is a third point of view which has not yet
been put forward. I must admit at once
that to leave out this Clause now would
be rather difficult in view of the fact that
a similar Clause appears in the Act of
1939, and also the right hon. Gentleman
has assured us that he is going to accept
a suggestion made that soldiers and sailors
should not lose the benefit of this Bill
through service abroad. There still remains the point which I made in 1939.
The wording of the Clause is too drastic
altogether. There is no loophole left at
all whatever the circumstances. There is
a loophole for soldiers and sailors serving
in the present war. That could not be
in the Act of 1939. The wording is so
I instrong that there is no way out.
stanced, speaking in 1939, what might
have been the condition of Lisburn after

the following New Clause-
NEW CLAUSE (Compensation for
land acquired other than by agreement).
(1) When at any time within the
period of five years from the date of
the commencement of this Act, land is
acquired by a local authority or by the
Trust, otherwise than by agreement,
for any of the purposes for which land
may be so acquired under this Act, the
compensation payable to such persons
as may be entitled to compensation
shall be ascertained by reference to
prices current at the thirty-first day of
March, 1939:
Provided, however, that when the
person to whom compensation under
this rule is payable is an Owneroccupier, he shall be entitled to receive,
as a supplement to such compensation,
a further sum equal to either of the
following sums, whichever is the lesser :
(a) thirty per cent. of such compensation, or
(b) such sum as would bring the compensation up to the amount that
would have been payable as compensation if this Clause had not
been enacted.
(2) Compensation for disturbance
shall not in any case be assessed at any
Mr. J. McHugh
greater amount than that at which it
would have fallen to be assessed if this

Brought up and read a First time.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: The new
Clause which is under my name was referred to by me on the Second Reading
of the Bill.
I may say that the Clause,
so far as its principles are concerned,
follows precisely along the lines of a corresponding Clause in a Bill passed by the
Imperial Parliament a month or two ago.
I have not attempted to elaborate the
Clause in the way in which it is elaborated
in the English Bill. I did not think it
worth my while to do so until I would be
sure of what the attitude of the Government was, but if the Government are prepared to accept my Amendment in
principle I do not think there would be
any great difficulty in elaborating it in
the way which would be desirable in order
that it might take its place finally in this
Bill.
May I say that I received this morning.
a very interesting document from the
Planning Commission to which I referred
on the last day, and that I have read that
document with great pleasure? It is one
of the most heartening productions that
I have perused for a good while. It may
be many years, perhaps half a century,
before all their ideals are realised, but it
is a grand thing to think that we have
men in authority prepared to think in
this way about the future development
of our city. I hope that this document
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
will be read, marked, learned, and inwardly digested by those who have the
good of the city of Belfast at heart. I
have referred to it because I think that
in that document are facts which justify
me still further in bringing forward this
Amendment, the effect of which would be
to put a ceiling to the prices required from
either the local authorities or the Trust
for land which they will need for these
workers' houses that are to be built.
We are told in this document that there
will have to be a displacement or overflow from Belfast of some 75,000 people.
They will have to be housed outside the
city on lines suggested by my right hon.
Friend, the Lord
Lord Mayor, who sits
opposite. This great number would represent 28,000 families, so that 28,000
families now housed in Belfast will
eventually have to be housed on the land
surrounding the city if a reasonable
standard of housing accommodation is to
be found for the people. If these houses
are to be placed properly, as the
Planning Commission think they ought to
be, no fewer than 3,000 acres of land will
be required. It is easy for anyone to
imagine the ramp that is possible, to put
it in that slangy way, if effective steps
are to be taken to house 28,000 people in
new houses in the neighbourhood of
Belfast. Undoubtedly suitable land will become scarcer and scarcer, and you cannot
defeat the law of supply and demand. As
the supply becomes less the price will rise
regardless of what arbiters you may have
to decide the matter.
I should like to assure the Minister concerned and the Leader of the House that
I know they are not unsympathetic in
regard to the principle indicated in this
Amendment of mine; indeed, the Minister said in reply to the debate on the
Second reading of the Bill that the matter
was under consideration and the legislation would be introduced in a short time
to deal with the way in which land would
be acquired otherwise than by agreement, when that land was to be used
for public purposes.
I should like to reiterate briefly the
facts which I think would justify
Government legislation along those lines
despite the difficulties. The first principle
is that this is a social service and that we
in Northern Ireland are pledged up to the
hilt to keep in step with Great Britain in
cur social services. When
you have
housing subsidies of almost 50 per cent.
of the cost, no one can deny that housing
has become one of the most important
social services.
The Parliament in Great Britain has
decided that the price paid for land ought
not, except in exceptional circumstances,
to exceed the normal or standard price in
the year 1939. That is the principle of
my Amendment. Then there is another
fact. This suggested legislation putting
a ceiling price for land is in line with the
Rent Restriction Acts which have proved
to be necessary under war conditionsduring the last war, during the period
between the wars and during this war.
These Rent Restriction Acts were found
to be necessary owing to the shortage of
houses and to the fact that, as a result
of that shortage, if rents were not restricted the people who occupy these
houses would, in some cases, not necessarily in all, be open to exploitation.
The effect of this Amendment of mine
would be to prevent people who will
occupy the houses that will be built in
the future from being, to some extent,
exploited in the same way because of the
high prices that will have to be paid for
the land, a high price which would undoubtedly be reflected in the rents,
whether the land was taken on lease or
purchased outright. Then, further, this
legislation, or legislation along those lines,
would be in line with many other items
of legislation by which the prices of
various commodities which are scarce
during the war have been fixed.
The man who speculates in land in the
neighbourhood of Belfast and sits until
the price rises, has not to expend a great
deal of labour in order to realise the
increased value of the land. A man who
sets out to produce tomatoes during the
war has to put a great deal of thought,
labour and capital into his work, and
when he has produced his goods for the
market he finds that the price is fixed.
Indeed, that is so in regard to nearly
everything that a farmer produces at the
present time. A maximum price is fixed
for nearly everything a farmer puts on
the market at the present time, and it
seems to me not unreasonable to ask that
speculators in land, which is a peculiar
commodity, and which cannot be expanded by production, should be pre-
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Committee
16 JANUARY 1945

which is essential for the building of
houses. The principal fact is that if
something of this sort is not done, the
effect will be one or other of two things.
First, either the rents of these houses
will have to be increased, or, secondly,
they will have to be placed more densely
per acre than is desirable.
If a local authority is able to buy land

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: First of all,
I should like to say that the object of
this Clause, the prevention of the exploitation of the public or community by
what is commonly called land speculation,
has the entire sympathy of the Government, but there are various weighty
reasons why the Government do not wish
this Clause to be incorporated in this Bill.
The first reason is that this measure deals
only with the acquisition of land for
houses. There are many other public
purposes for which land may be acquired
compulsorily, and in many cases the
actual area is considerably larger. That
being so the Government consider that
this question of compensation in connection with land acquired compulsorily
should be dealt with in a more comprehensive measure.
As the right hon.
Gentleman, the Minister of Health and
Local Government, said on the. Second
Reading, the matter is now under consideration by the Government..
The second reason why the Government
think it undesirable to include a Clause
of this nature in the Bill is that they are
advised that it is very doubtful whether
the Clause, as worded, would come within the power of this Parliament in view
of the requirements of the Government
of Ireland Act, Section 5. It would
obviously be undesirable to pass a Clause
which would subsequently have to be
ruled ultra vires. Those are the two
main reasons why we resist the insertion
of the Clause. I do not think it would
be fair to leave the Committee under the
impression that it is easy to devise legislation which would frustrate the land
speculator and, at the same time, not
inflict hardship on the owner of land who
is going ahead and farming his land.
without any idea of speculation.
The Members of the Committee will
remember that this Clause had an extremely stormy passage in the Imperial
House of Commons. It would be even
more difficult to put into effect in this
country where we have such a very large
number of holders of small farms. It is
my experience that it is very much more
difficult to avoid doing injustice and causing hardship when dealing with the small
owner than when dealing with a large
owner. That being so the Government
wish to have plenty of time to think over
this matter, and they will act with the
greatest care before they bring forward
legislation on the matter. This is a question which needs to be treated sympathetically, but as objectively and unemotionally as possible. As I have said
it is a highly controversial matter and
the less heat we generate over it the
better.
There is one further point I should like
to make. Although it is undesirable that
anybody should be allowed to make
exorbitant profits in connection with
land acquired for the building of houses,
Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
it should be remembered that the cost of
the land is a very small part of the cost
of a house. If an acre of land costs £100
and ten houses are erected on it the cost
per house for the land works out at two
pence per week on the rent of each house.
Although an exorbitant profit should not
be made, the cost of the land would have
to rise to a figure which it had never
previously reached before it would affect
the cost of a house to any great extent.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I wish to
thank the Leader of the House for his
very sympathetic attitude towards this
matter, and also for the indication which
he gave that the point is being considered and will be dealt with. My
difficulty, however, and my reason
for
pressing the matter, is that during the
time the question is being considered
land will be acquired at some price or
other and a good number of houses
erected. It is quite true that this might
have been a more appropriate point for a
Planning Bill than for a Housing Bill,
as in England. The trouble is that the
Government here have produced their
Housing Bill first, and how are they going
to deal with the land after it has been
taken and the houses built on it? I
think that this new Clause should be put
to the House.
Question, "That the Clause be read a

Mr. LESLIE: There is a curious little
point in this Clause-I have not any
Amendment down on the Order Paperabout the taking over of the road. The
road is to be taken over and thereafter
maintained by the local authority. There
is a definition in the Bill, as the Committee is aware, of the expression "local
authority," but that definition does not
include the county council.
It seems to me to be very possible and
very probable that the new road to be
taken over, and thereafter maintained by
the local authority may be one of the
roads called a class I or class II road, and
at present, so far as I know throughout
the North of Ireland, class I and class
II roads are maintained by the county
councils and not by the rural district
councils in whose area they happen to be.
I think I mentioned before I have no
Amendment on the Order Paper, but am
speaking on Clause 22 generally. That
is a difficulty which, it seems to me, might
very well be cleared up on the Report
stage. The question is, is the expression
"local authority" where it occurs at the
end of the Clause to be treated as including "county council" or not, because it
seems to me that the new roads to be
taken over may well be class I or class
II roads and maintainable by the county
council as the local authority. That is
all I want to say on the Clause.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I rather
think that the right hon. Gentleman, as
not infrequently happens, has got a very
good point there of which I have not
thought. At the same time I should not
like to say so definitely without further
reflection on it. If he would permit me
to deal with it on the Report stage I think
that would meet the case.
Mr. LESLIE: I think that is the right
thing to do-to let the Clause go through
now, and consider it on Report stage.

number of persons who may be

reasonable preference should be
given in the selection of tenants
or occupiers of such accommodation;

Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move at the end of page 15, line 22 (“any
letting thereof") to add the words: "provided however that in any case where the
persons occupying such accommodation
are all members of the same family they
shall not be removed from the dwelling
merely on the ground that their number
exceeds the number prescribed, unless the
local authority, or the Trust, as the case
may be, offers them other and ampler
accommodation."
This Amendment refers to a particular
case. The Ministry are here taking
power to prescribe the number of people
who may be allowed to occupy one of these
new houses, and I foresee the possibility
of difficulty arising where it might be
hard for the Ministry to enforce the law
and exceedingly hard for the family that
may be affected. I quite agree that,
where too great a number of people are
put into a house as lodgers or by subletting, the Ministry should have the
right to limit the number of people in that
case and perhaps even eject the whole
crowd of them, if the number is excessive;
but suppose the number that exceeds the
prescribed maximum is due to natural
causes, as I might describe it what then?
Suppose they are members of the one
famiy and they are in a house which is
supposed to accommodate six persons and
there are six children and two parents,
making the total eight-what is to happen
in a case like that? I think special consideration should be given to a case of
that sort.
I do not think that the Ministry should
take power unto themselves to limit the
number of a family in that way. That
is what it would come to. Surely in
these days of a falling population anything like that would be a great misfortune. I would suggest that this Clause
be clarified in the way that I have suggested. If the Government would say
to the Committee, the Regulations would
be framed in such a way that a large
family would not be ejected unless alternative accommodation was found for them
I would be quite satisfied. I think it
right to call attention to this because
great hardship might be created by the
prescribed maximum number for a house
of a particular size. I beg to move.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT:
I quite
appreciate the right hon. Gentleman's
point of view, but the Government think
that it is really covered by the last words
of paragraph (a), "any letting thereof."
During the course of a tenancy there is
no question of turning anyone out. Only
at the termination of a tenancy would it
be possible to say, in the interests of what
I might call decent house and estate
management, this house is absolutely
overcrowded. I do not imagine that this
will ever be enforced harshly, but I think
the power should be there. If it were
not possible to provide that at the end
of a tenancy, one might say that then
decent house and estate management would
become, almost impossible, and it would be
possible. merely by the increase of families
to have the most appalling conditions of
overcrowding.

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think I
must have failed to make this clear to
the Committee. Under paragraph (a)
the Ministry cannot put anyone out while
the tenancy continues, whether they are a
young married couple, happily and busily
engaged in increasing their family, or for
any other reason. It is only on the
termination of the tenancy that it can
be said that the house is overcrowded.
The draughtsman and everyone concerned
have so interpreted this paragraph, and
I think it is reasonable to accept that
interpretation. As I have said, I am
sure this paragraph would not be interpreted harshly, but there must be power
to deal with it. I think I might deal

confess that I do not understand the

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Leaving
aside for the moment whether the paragraph is happily worded or not, maybe I
can give the reason for it being there.
The reason at any rate in the early stages
is the acute shortage of houses, and it
will be necessary to exercise a preference
within a preference so to speak. For instance, it will be necessary probably to give
a preference to a married ex-Service man
with children over an ex-Service bachelor,
and, in general, to try to strike some sort
of order of priority which will give houses
to those in the greatest need of them.
That need will probably gradually disappear as houses become more plentiful,
but that is the object of this paragraph.
It would ensure that a house would go to a
man with a large family instead of a man
with a small family, and to a man who has
no house rather than to a man who has a
house and merely wants a better one.
It is very difficult to find a form of
wording which will give the necessary
power for reasonable discrimination as to
the type of tenant. Maybe we have not
hit the happiest wording. I am prepared
to reconsider the wording, but must keep
in the principle. There must be in the
early stages a certain amount of regulation.
I
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
too am very unhappy about the wording

the thing does not need. I think in this
case it is quite clear the words are unnecessary. If the houses are built by the
Housing Trust then the houses belong to
that Trust, and this body can exercise
whatever authority it wishes. The Housing Trust is completely in the hands of
the Ministry. Everything that is done
Rt. Hon. J. G. Leslie
Mr. J. G. Lennon

NEW CLAUSE (Relaxation of residence qualifications for purposes of certain housing enactments).
27. For the purposes of section
twenty of the Housing Act (Northern
Ireland), 1939 (which requires a residence qualification for tenants of houses
provided by a local authority under the
Housing Acts or the Labourers Acts),
sub-section (5) of section two of the
Housing (Requisitioning of Premises)
Act (Northern Ireland), 1944, (which
requires a residence qualification for
persons permitted to occupy premises
requisitioned under that Act), and
section nineteen of this Act, any period
during which a person has served outside the United Kingdom, in time of
war emergency, in the armed forces of
the Crown, or the Merchant Navy, or
in the Mercantile Marine, shall be
treated as a period during which he has
been resident in the United Kingdom.
Brought up, and read a First time.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That the Clause be read a Second
time."
This is a new Clause to which
reference was made in the debate on
Clause 19. Its effect is to provide that
throughout the Housing Act war service
in the armed forces, the Merchant Navy
or the Mercantile Marine outside the
bounds of the United Kingdom shall be
treated as residence in the United Kingdom for the purposes of qualifying for
accommodation under our housing legislation. Again I thank the right hon.
Gentleman for calling attention to this
matter. It was the intention of the
Government to cover it in a general
measure later, but on reflection they think
it desirable to insert it now.
Clause read a Second time and added to
the Bill.

Mr. LESLIE: The point, of course, is
that you are going to raise the qualification for the possession of the tenancy of
one of these houses provided by rates and
taxes, and you are going to let in a person with £8 a week, a person with £416
a year. That is a great advance on anything that has been done before. We
began with 30s a week, then we went to
45s a week, and now it is proposed to go
to 160s a week. I really cannot feel
that you are justified in putting a person
with an income of £400 a year on the
rates and taxes as regards his house.
is too much altogether.
It
Housing (No. 2) Bill-

16 JANUARY 1945
the
The argument has been used that you
accordance with
are acting in
Insurance Act, under which a person who
gets a salary of four hundred and something a year is compulsorily insured, but
if you are going to take that as an argument and you are going to be tethered
to the insurance anchor, and something
in the nature of the Beveridge Scheme
comes along, under which everybody is
insured, everybody will be entitled to a
house at the expense of the rates and
taxes. I cannot stand for that, it is
putting too much on the State and on the
rates. That is one thing.
Another point is that at present you
are presumably out in search of houses
for certain classes of people. There is
said to be a shortage of houses, so the
method proposed to be adopted is to
increase the number of classes of people
who are demanding the houses. If you
are going to raise the level from 45s to
£8, you are going to multiply enormously
the number of people applying for houses
and there, I think, you are excluding the
class you really want to help. You are
going to get in a worse form one of the
evils attending the labourers' cottages
schemes, where, we found again and again
that the cottages provided for agricultural
labourers were in fact used by people
other than agricultural labourers. That
was so then, and this would aggravate it
again.
You are introducing an additional class,
and I do not think that that would be
advantageous, because the point is that
you want houses at once, and in some
quantity. You want quantity rather
than quality, and you will not get that
by raising the standard of the applicants
so high as £8 a week. I have suggested
£6. I think it is rather too high, but
still, in view of the difference in prices
and wages and everything else since. we
last dealt with the matter and fixed 45s,
it might be possible to go as far as £6,
but certainly, I think, no further. We
cannot rely on wages and prices remaining
as they are at present; we hope they will
not. At all events, £400 a year is too
much.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think perhaps it would be best just to run through
the persons who are qualified for houses.
under the various housing schemes. First
of all, without any income qualification,
there are all persons working for wages,
and then all self-employed persons who
do not employ others except members of
their own families. In those two categories no question of wage. level, income,
or family income arises. Then you come
to the rest of the world. If you put in
no income, everybody is entitled to be
qualified for subsidised houses, and it is
just a question where you put the qualifying amount.
When this matter was being discussed
in the Commons the Bill, as originally
drafted, provided the old 45s a week, and
in deference to a very strong request in
the House of Commons, the Government
increased that figure to £8. Therefore,
it is now Government policy that it should
be £8. The figure of £8 was taken
largely because, as the right hon. and
learned Gentleman has said, it is the
figure in some recent Insurance. Acts.
The matter really can be argued from
that point of view.
People who do not work in the sense of
the first two categories I mentioned are
very often expected by the State to look
after themselves on a lower income level
than the workers, but I think there is no
particular logic behind that. It really
comes to this: you must select some
figure. The figure of £8 really does relate to something in Acts which have
been passed, whilst £6 relates to nothing
whatever; in fact, it does not seem to me
to hang on to anything between the £3
in the English Housing Acts and the £8
in the Insurance Acts, which we have
taken here.
There is one point which I think it is
worth while mentioning to the Committee.
With the present cost of housing the
economic rent of a new house without a
subsidy, as the Lord Mayor of Belfast has
told us, is £2 or over £2 a week. It is
only brought down by subsidy. It is
generally held that the very highest proportion of income a man should be expected to pay on rent is one-eighth. So.
as long as costs stay as high as they are
the £8 man will come within this limit.
Under a Clause which the Committee has
already passed there will be, as I say, a
preference within the qualifying classes as
long as there is a housing shortage.
do not imagine that the £8 man, unless
he has an enormous family or other good
reason, will in the early stages qualify for
a house as against a poorer man. The

Housing (No. 2) Bill-
Report of Amendments
23 JANUARY 1945
Dealing with the debate in the Committee
stage this is an attempt on my part to
provide a possible loophole to meet the
cases which, although not expected at the
moment, might not impossibly arise.
As it stands, the Clause is very definite
indeed. A person who has not got this
seven years' continuous residence as explained on the Amendment, passed in the
Committee stage, is definitely excluded.
Inserting the words I suggest will not of
itself give him the opportunity of getting
a house, but it does leave a possible loophole of which advantage could be taken
in suitable cases if they arose.
It is not hard to imagine the sort of
cases which may arise. I instanced on
the Second Reading, and again in Committee, the incursion of the Huguenots
into Northern Ireland at the end of the
seventeenth century. Something of that
sort may occur again. You may lose the
key men mentioned by the hon. and
gallant Member opposite in the Committee stage. It does seem to me desirable that a loophole should be provided,
although I must admit it is a little undesirable to give the power of consent to
the Ministry of Health and Local Government. It may, perhaps, put the Minister
in a somewhat difficult position, but I do
not think we can help that.
After all,
Ministers accept that position.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid
I must resist this Amendment by the
right hon. Gentleman. It would give a
power of discretion to the Minister of
Health which I think no man would wish
to have. Also, we do feel that this is one
of the cases where it must be definite.
We must either not have the Clause at
all or we must make it definite. I should
remind the House that this Clause applies
only to houses built with the aid of subsidies under this Act. There still will
be some three or four hundred thousand
houses available. I am afraid I must
resist the Amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think anybody
is disposed to press on the Ministry a
power they do not want. I ask leave to
withdraw the Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

It is regretted that no concession can
be made regarding the ploughing of
lea which would have the effect of
reducing the tillage quota. While it is
desirable that the ploughing of lea,
particularly where the land is heavy,
should be done as early as possible
when conditions permit, the Ministry
feels confident,
in view of past
experience, that satisfactory crops can
be produced on lea land ploughed much
later than the present date. In Northern Ireland excellent corn crops have
always been raised from lea land. In
many districts farmers do not commence
to plough lea until well into the
new year, and during the war
years the Ministry has been urging
farmers to plough leas earlier in
view of the increased plough-

30 JANUARY 1945
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 30th January, 1945.

Laid before the House (pursuant to

EDUCATION.
RECONSTRUCTION PROPOSALS.
Motion by Sir Roland Nugent.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I beg to move
That this House welcomes the intention of the Government to introduce
legislation for the improvement of
the educational system in Northern
Ireland.
The House would wish me, I am sure,
to extend a welcome to my right hon.
Friend the Minister of Education on his
first visit to the Senate. He has come to
listen to our debate on the Motion, take
note of the suggestions for improvements
that will be put forward by this House,
and let us have the benefit of 'his views
on matters which may appear to any of
us to be obscure, or doubtful.
In making a speech of this nature,
when a very long and eloquent speech
has been made in another place, I think
there is bound to be. at the best little
variation of the theme, and I would ask
the indulgence of the House if I only
outline in skeleton fashion a few of the
points. I think the Motion which I am
moving is, however. one of very great
importance. It is the first step towards
the creation of a new educational system
for the children of our Province, a system
which we all hope will produce for future
generations men and women educated not
only in the narrow and formal sense of
the word, but also educated as ready,
willing and competent citizens playing
their full part in the life of our country
in the future. The Motion is based, of
course, upon the White Paper on Educational Reconstruction which my right
hon. Friend has had prepared.
I have
no doubt that hon. Senators have read
it with care, and they will not disagree
with me when I say that it is admirably
drawn up it is simple and lucid, it
portrays the past with clarity, and it
presents its arguments for the future
with moderation. It is a document
which we shall all study with the greatest
care during the next year or so.
in
The proposals in the White Paper
are summarised in paragraph 97. It
will not be necessary, I think, that
I should discuss them seriatim. Many
of them will arouse no criticism,
dealing, as they do,
do, with existing
educational practice and administration.
I shall concern myself chiefly with the
more novel features the manner
which it is proposed to create compulsory
secondary education without violent
disturbance to our system as it stands at
present the changes that are foreshadowed in technical and further education, the financial arrangements that are
proposed for the voluntary managers of
schools and for the local education
authorities, the amalgamation into
county committees of the regional committees for Antrim, Tyrone and Londonderry-and, of course, religious instruction. At the conclusion of the debate
my right hon. Friend will deal with those
matters which call for reply, and he will
be at the disposal of hon. Senators to
elucidate any points on those proposals
which I am passing by, but which, nevertheless, may be of special interest to some
of the Members of this House.
The great fundamental change proposed
in the White Paper is the abolition of
elementary education and the creation in its
place of two stages of compulsory education-primary up to the age of 11 plus,
and secondary up to the age of 15. Hon.
Senators do not need to be told by me
that this regrading of education has been
going on in Great Britain in a modified
form since 1926, and that it is now made
statutory by the English Education Act
of 1944. It is a change that can be
effected easily enough in the large cities.
with their dense populations. In the
rural districts the problem is more difficult, for there the schools are small and
scattered, with usually not more than one
or two teachers. And yet it is the small
local rural school that most needs a
development and advance in education.
Education-
Reconstruction Proposals
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
There we have, in one or two rooms,
children of all ages from 6 to 14 taught
by one or two teachers without any proper
grading, without any facilities for domestic
economy for the girls, without a woodwork
room for the boys, no gymnasium, no
specialist teacher and, to be perfectly frank,
very little but the bare essentials. It is a
wonder to me that the rural schools under
these conditions can be as successful as
they are. I think it is a great credit to
the devoted work of the teachers that
they have produced the results they have
under these disadvantages. But, I think,
it must be admitted that many country
children, when they reach the age of 11
or 12, are merely putting in their time
at the school, and that the country
teacher is thankful if they keep quiet in
the class and allow him to concentrate on
the younger sections of his community.
To leave the rural schools as they are and
to impose on them an additional year of
compulsory schooling would be, to my
mind, a complete waste of time and
money.
The proposal that the Government
favour is that the child should go to the
primary school until he has reached the
age of 11 plus, and that he should then go
to a secondary school until he reaches the
leaving age of 15. Eleven plus, of
course, is a convenient of saying
way
"between the ages of 11 and 12," and
when we say that the age of transfer
should be 11 plus, we do not mean that
this should be a rigid rule. Provision
will be made for those pupils who
develop early or late. Their transfer will
be accelerated or retarded accordingly.
The secondary school to which they will
go will cater for them in a four-year
course. The first two years will be a
general course, with proper grading of
the pupils, and the last two will be more
specialised. For example, a large
secondary school of this type might well
have classes in literary subjects, in commercial subjects and a practical course.
In all four years great stress will be laid
on physical training and on subjects such
as woodwork, domestic economy, civics
and training for citizenship, and I do not
think that any hon. Member will dispute
that what is proposed will constitute a
great advance in the education of those
children—and I suppose they are the vast
majority-who are not academically
minded, but who can be educated, or
rather can educate themselves, much
more readily if the approach is made
through practical subjects.
There are two points on this division
into primary and secondary education
which have aroused interest-the question
whether 11 plus is the right age for the
"break" or transfer to the secondary
school, and the point as to whether the
travelling by bus that will be involved for
the rural child-say 5 or 10 miles each
way-will have a detrimental effect on
the rural dweller and accentuate what is
called the "drift from the countryside."
So far as this latter point is concerned,
I should have thought that the creation
of these secondary schools in our villages
and towns would tend to arrest the drift,
for we all know that one of the motives
that actuate the countryman in coming to
the city is a desire to give his children
better education than they can get in the
small rural school. I asked my right
hon. Friend about it; his reply is that if
travelling by bus spoils the country child
he must already be thoroughly spoilt, for
at present 10,000 or more of them travel
every day by bus coming in from the
countryside to the larger towns where
there are secondary schools, day technical
schools and large, well-equipped elementary schools.
The other point-whether 11 plus is the
right age for the break-is interesting.
I note that this was the age recommended
by the Hadow Committee which issued
its famous Report in 1926, and the
English authorities have adopted it in
their new Education Act. It is also the
age at which there is a traditional change
in Northern Ireland in the school programme. Standard IV is finished, and
standard V introduces the pupil to new
subjects such as domestic economy, woodwork and mathematics. But there is
another and, to my mind, even more
important reason why the "break" should
be made at 11 plus rather than at 12 plus
-it enables us to make the new secondary school a real school with a four-year
course properly graded, and, again, it
provides a sufficient number of pupils to
make the school a proper size with
adequate staff and adequate accommodation for the practical subjects. This is
very important, especially in the rural
districts, where numbers will be a vital
consideration. If we were to defer transfer until 12 plus we should lose the
Education-
Reconstruction Proposals
30 JANUARY 1945
advantage of the age group for one year
and we should have to draw on much
wider areas throughout the country to
furnish us with the numbers necessary to
make up a school of reasonable size. I
need hardly point out that that again
would complicate the travelling problem.
Well, then, that is the picture of the
new secondary school-and here I may
say frankly that I intensely dislike the
term "junior secondary," as does my
right hon. Friend. We hope some Member of the House will introduce a better
term. So anxious are we to find a better
term that my right hon. Friend might
almost offer a prize to any Member who
might help him in this difficult matter.
(Laughter.)
It is made clear in the White Paper
that the two classes of secondary schoolthe new type giving free compulsory
education normally up to the age of 15,
and the old established type giving its
education up to 17 or 18-will be equal in
status so far as the Ministry of Education
can make them, and there should be no
question of any inferiority or superiority
complex. I cannot help thinking there
is a little too much talk about snobbery
in this matter. One who accuses another
of being a snob is generally the most
appalling snob himself.
There is also provision made for boys
of 13 to pass from the secondary school to
the day technical school where they will
be expected to stay until the age of 16
in order that they may complete a sound
specialised course lasting three years;
this, to my mind, is specially important,
for we shall need the hightest skill
possible developed in our tradesmen for
the years to come. That skill to my
mind will have to be based on sounder
foundations of general technical and
scientific knowledge than has been the
case in the past. Processes and methods
of manufacture are changing rapidly,
and there must be sound foundations of
general technical knowledge if the boy,
when he becomes a man, is to move
easily to new processes and new forms of
skilled work.
Coming back to 11 plus and to the
child who has an ability and a desire
to secure secondary education which
will bring him-or her-to the age of
18, and perhaps further to the University
For
the Training College, but whose
parents cannot afford to pay the fees
-
required by the existing secondary schools.
-what is proposed here? It is proposed to make use of the existing
secondary schools which have served
Ulster well and which give an academic
and cultural education equal to any
given elsewhere. They are, of course,
nearly all under voluntary boards of
governors. It is proposed to require these
schools to admit up to 80 per cent. of
who have
pupils who
have passed a general
qualifying not competitive examination conducted by the Ministry, and
to require the education authorities to
pay the fees and any other allowances
necessary for those children whose
parents cannot pay. That includes not
only the fees but whatever is required
for the maintenance of the child at school.
The old competitive element will pass
away, a general means test will be devised for all education authorities, there
will be grading of the means test, starting with the parent who can pay nothing
and shading down to the parent whose
child has passed the examination but
whose means are such that he can pay
all charges himself. Under this system,
I hope, we shall have no more cases of
the child qualifying for academic secondary education and showing that his
parent cannot pay but nevertheless being
denied the education for which he is
suited.
New secondary schools will be required
throughout the country and these will be
built by either education authorities or
by voluntary managers. I leave the
education authorities for the present and
shall deal with the voluntary manager,
Protestant and Roman Catholic. The
Government fully realise that he will
require increased financial assistance for
the building and the maintenance of his
schools. At present he receives 50 per
cent. for heating, cleaning and lighting
from the education authority, and 50 per
cent. from the State for building and extension. We propose that the education
authority should now take over full
responsibility for the heating, cleaning,
lighting and internal repairs of
voluntary school and that the State should
increase its building grant to 65 per cent.
the
In return the voluntary manager should
agree to appoint a four-and-two committee

I pass over many matters of great
significance in the proposals of the Government, and come now to the new financial
arrangements between the education
authorities and the Exchequer. Hon.
Senators know, of course, that at present
the State bears a very great proportion
of the expenditure on our educational
services, due to the fact that the salaries
of all elementary teachers about
£1,500,000 a year-is defrayed from the
Vote of the Ministry of Education. They
know also that the Ministry's grant to
education authorities is two-thirds of
their approved expenditure up to the
limit of the produce of a 1s rate. Thus
when the limit has been reached, no
further grant is given by the Ministry.
This is wrong in principle. An education authority that is going to receive no
State assistance is not at all likely to embark on educational projects. Indeed it
might be very hard on the ratepayers if
they did, no matter how desirable they
may be.
It is also clear that in the great
scheme of reconstruction which is envisaged, requiring as it will many more
teachers with revised salary scales, the
State cannot be expected to carry an
ever-increasing burden for salaries, and a
part-however small-of this burden
must now be assumed by the local
authorities.
Education--
Reconstruction Proposals
30 JANUARY 1945
In the drafting of a new scheme of
financial relations these two considerations must be borne in mind, but there
is another, even more important that no
child shall be prejudiced in his opportunities for education merely by reason
of the fact that he lives in an area which
is a poor rate-producing area; an area
where, perhaps, the cost of building a
new school would represent an increase in
the local rate so considerable as to make
the education committee concerned delay
the erection of the school or perhaps even
postpone it indefinitely. I am assured
that there have been many cases where,
through no fault of the local authority,
a child in its area has had far less chance
than a child of similar ability in a richer
area. That, to my mind, is wrong, and,
in looking at the future of our country,
it is a mistake.
On these three considerations has been
built a new scheme, one which, I submit,
is sound from both the educational and
the financial point of view. It is proposed that a general education rate be
levied over Northern Ireland as a whole,
out of which will be met 35 per cent. of
the following charges:-(a) The increase
over the cost in a standard year of the
cost of the salaries of teachers in primary
and junior secondary schools; (b) 35 per
cent. of the cost of new and improved
school accommodation provided by the
authorities; and (c) 35 per cent. of the
cost of scholarships to secondary and
technical schools. In other words, these
charges will be defrayed, as to 65 per
cent. by the Exchequer, and as to the
balance of 35 per cent. by the new general
education rate. All other expenditure
by education authorities will be met from
their own local rates struck for the
purpose and from an Exchequer grant of
65 per cent. To sum up, the State will
carry the annual
annual charge
charge of about
£1,500,000 in respect of what may be
called existing teachers' salaries, and will
contribute a grant of 65 per cent. in
respect of all approved expenditure by
the authorities, some of this expenditure
being met from the general education rate
and the rest from the local rate. Further,
there will be no ceiling to the Exchequer
grant, and no hindrance to progressive
development.
I have dealt only with the bare framework of the scheme of reconstruction.
Hon. Senators will have read for themselves in the White Paper the details
which I have not time to touch on nowthe extension of the school-leaving age,
the provision of nursery schools, the
reduction in the size of classes, the provision of free books, the expansion of
medical and dental schemes and schemes

children, and other matters. All these
new schemes will throw an immense
amount of work on the education committees and their officials, and the
Government suggest that if that work is
to be carried out efficiently, the thirteen
regional education committees at present
in operation in the counties of Antrim,
Tyrone and Londonderry must be
amalgamated into three county committees.
These small committees, many of which
by the way have done excellent work
under the Education Act of 1923, will
not be able to carry the burden that will
be placed upon them under the new
schemes. Most of their officers are parttime, and it will be agreed that the time
is coming when the full-time officerexecutive officer, school medical officer,
school architect, or school attendance
officer must be provided. The time when
a part-time officer can carry on the work
is vanishing. There is another reason
which, I think, demands the amalgamation of these committees on a county
basis. The new secondary schools to be.
built will serve districts with a radius of
5-10 miles, and if they are to be sited
properly and economically, without
unnecessary overlapping, it will be no
longer possible to have in the one county.
three or even five independent education
committees, each conducting its own
accommodation survey, and planning the
erection of its own secondary schools
without reference to the plans of its
neighbours. I think this is an absolutely
conclusive argument for the establishment of education committees on a county
basis, such as obtains at present for
Down, Armagh and Fermanagh.
The last matter on which I have to
speak is religious instruction. This
matter has excited the greatest interest
in Northern Ireland generally, and it
seems to me that perhaps historical and
legal considerations have been allowed to
some extent to obscure the importance of
the advances which the Government pro-
Education-
Reconstruction Proposals
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
pose, and that there is a danger of
allowing ourselves to lose our sense of
proportion on this matter.
The Government propose that for
every school in Northern Ireland which
is conducted under the regulations of the.
Ministry-primary, day technical, junior
secondary, and senior secondary-it shall
be a statutory requirement that the
school day shall begin with collective
worship on the part of the pupils, and
that religious instruction shall be given.
The advance that is proposed is clearly
shown, when I explain that under the
law as it is at present there is no requirement whatever for collective worship and
there is no obligation for the giving of
religious instruction in any schools, other
than elementary schools, under the education authorities. All the voluntary
elementary schools, all the day technical
schools, and all the secondary schools,
whether under voluntary boards
governors or not, are at present exempt
from any obligation to give religious
instruction. That is a very great advance,
since there will in future be collective
worship and religious instruction in every
school in the country.
of
We are also proposing that in future
the liberty of conscience of the teacher in
the local authority school shall be respected, and that if he requests that, for
reasons of conscience, he be excused from
giving religious instruction, he should be
SO excused and that the education
authority should be given power, instead,
to pay for the services of a person specially
appointed to give the instruction. I do
not wish to enter into the legal aspects
of this question beyond saying that where
eminent lawyers differ on a constitutional
matter there must obviously be great
doubt as to the powers of this Parliament,
and that if it should, when constitutionally tested, prove that we have not such
powers, it seems extremely improbable
that the Imperial Parliament, which has
protected the liberty of conscience of the
teacher by the fullest and most meticulous.
safeguards in their own Education Act,
would be disposed to accord a subordinate
Parliament powers to do that which they
have with such care abstained from doing
themselves.
I wish, however, to call the attention
of the House to one point which seems to
me to be of great importance and on
which I feel very deeply. It seems to me
that we who sit in Parliament have a very
special responsibility for maintaining the
liberties of the subject. Of those liberties
infinitely the most important is liberty of
conscience. We have seen in the past few
years on the Continent of Europe how
terribly easy it is for liberties which
seemed securely founded, to vanish like
yesterday's snow.
We should, I think, be very careful to
avoid any act which may imperil this
most precious of all liberties, even if what
we did would only affect one or two
persons in all this Province. I for one
should lament very much if that hole was
made in the dyke. After all, our fathers
had to fight for that liberty of conscience.
Many of them died in the field, and in
prison, and on the scaffold. At a great
price they bought that freedom, but we
were born free. Let us look to it that
we do not heedlessly imperil that precious
heritage.

Now the Ministry of Education apparently

This proposal deserves careful examination, not only because it is a reversal of
that agreed settlement arrived at at that

also of the policy announced by Lord
Craigavon in 1939 that no legislation of

the period of the war. It is true that
the Education Bill in England was
enacted during the war, but in England
the parties concerned were in agreement.
In Ulster, however, in regard to this
Education Bill the parties are at sixes

proposal of the Government, and, more

It may be asked, and very properly so,
if the Churches are in agreement with the
opinions expressed by the Leader of the
House and the Minister of Education in
regard to the necessity of safeguarding
the liberty of conscience of teachers,
where is the difference? What is all the
dispute about? Well, there is a very
great difference, and subtle minds cannot
obscure the difference. I do not pretend
that the Leader of the House tried to
obscure that difference, but it could be
done. It ought to be brought out clearly,
and that is what I shall try to do. Briefly,
the difference is this. The Ministry of
Education apparently thinks that education committees should be precluded by
Statute from entering into any understanding with the teacher at the time of
his appointment that he will give religious.
instruction in a systematic and regular
way, whether that teacher is willing or
unwilling, whether that teacher has a
conscientious conviction or has no scruples
whatever about the matter. The Ministry
appear to hold that on account of the
alleged prohibitions in the 1920 Act these
education committees should be debarred
from raising the question of religious
instruction or Bible instruction at all
when they are appointing a teacher.
Their view would seem to be that,
without any agreement, or any understanding, or any undertaking with a
teacher, whether he was willing to give
religious instruction or not, the entire
responsibility for religious instruction in
the schools should be left to the intermittent and haphazard whims and
fancies of the teacher as to when, as to
how, and as to how often he will give this
instruction, and as to whether in fact he
will give this instruction at all. In short,
it seems to be the opinion of the Ministry
that this aspect of the work of the school
should not be made part of the duty of
any teacher, whether that teacher has
conscientious objections to it or not. The
alternative view, and the view of the
Churches and the regional education
committees, is that since Bible instruction
is to be provided by the regional committees by Statutes of the Realm, these
regional committees ought logically and
naturally and normally to be allowed to
appoint teachers to their positions in
particular schools in adequate numbers
to give that instruction which the committees are required to provide in these
schools, and
be
that they should
empowered, as part of that duty, to
make arrangements with willing teachers.
and qualified teachers that this work
would be done by them in a faithful,
loyal, and systematic way, and in
accordance with the agreed programme
as part of their normal duties.
That seems to be the sole and only
claim of the Church so far as this particular item in the programme of the

to
What are the arguments by means of
which the Ministry try to justify their
desire to entrust religious instruction to
the mere haphazard whims and fancies
of the teachers? The Ministry justifies
its policy on two grounds. First of all
they say that to make Bible instruction
a duty even of the willing teacher is
contrary to the terms of the 1920 Act.
Secondly-and by the way I think this
was implied, perhaps unwittingly, by the
Leader of the House they seem
think that if a teacher at the time of his
appointment makes a contract to do this
work, that is compulsion, and the whole
spirit of the instruction he will subsequently give will be vitiated. I shall
deal with that alleged prohibition of the
1920 Act in a moment, but I wish now
to concentrate attention on the alleged
impropriety of a teacher giving an undertaking to carry out what he has been
appointed to do. This idea of such an
arrangement vitiating the spirit of the
instruction reminds me of the theory of
the early Quakers that one dare not speak
about religion except under the influence
of a special religious urge. A teacher,
May I say with all respect that this
objection seems to me to be just nonsense.
Everyone knows that, human nature being
what it is, few duties of a regular and
systematic nature would be faithfully and
constantly carried out without the aid of
a positive agreement, and such agreements are almost invariably made between
employers and employees. One might
just as reasonably argue that the spirit
of the service of teachers in regard to all
their other duties would be vitiated by
the fact that they gave undertakings in
regard to these other duties which they
carry out in the school. So much for
this theory of the Ministry of Education
which lies behind their apparent determination to tear up and destroy root and
branch in a period of war-time this
settlement which was arrived at by Lord
Craigavon and his Government to the
satisfaction of all the parties concerned.
What are the reasons why it should be
resisted? First of all I believe it would
inevitably tend to depreciate in the eyes
of teachers and pupils alike the importance of Bible instruction in the schools.
If this subject is to be treated as one of
which regional committees can take no
note, as one which must not be put on
any teacher's programme, as one which
must not be regarded as the duty of any
particular teacher to give instruction in,
I am convinced that in a very short time
it will come to be regarded as a subject
of very small importance in the work of
the school. A second objection, to this
proposal to leave this matter to the intermittent, haphazard decision of individual
teachers is that it overlooks entirely the
fickleness that occasionally besets even
the best of us. It is not even realistic.
We ought to take into account not merely
the conscientious scruples of the few but
the weaknesses of the many. There is
something of Rip Van Winkle in all of us.
If we were not under a definite contract to carry out faithfully the routine
duties we have to perform very often
these duties would not be carried out at
Education-

30 JANUARY 1945
all. But there are special reasons why
in the case of teachers there might rise up
in their minds very good excuses why they
should shirk this responsibility which was
not laid upon them as part of their duty
and as part of their contract in response
to their expressed willingness to undertake it. They would have very good
excuses. Their hours are long; there are
many subjects on the programme; and if
Bible instruction is not in that programme
it will be the first of those subjects to go.
I challenge anyone to deny that fact, and
I could cite facts in support of that were
there time at my disposal, and were this
the place to introduce such facts.
A further reason for resisting these
proposals is that they are not in accordance with the views and the wishes of
the Government's own regional committees. It is interesting to recall the
fact that prior to the adoption of the 1925
Act, and also of the 1930 Act, the representatives of the Protestant Churches and
the Orange Order were constantly told
that their desires could not be complied
with, because all the regional committees
were against them. Now what has
happened? These same regional committees, after 15 years' experience of this
Act, are on the side of the Churches and
against the views of the Government.
Yet their views are now wholly ignored.
No notice is taken of the protests of these
committees. The Ministry seems determined to march on an unpopular course
just as if it were prepared, if necessary,
to operate the whole of the educational
proposals from Stormont without any cooperation at all from the regional committees.
May I be allowed to quote a couple of
sentences from the statement issued by
the Association of Northern Ireland
Education Committees. This Association is the official body, constituted under
Act of Parliament, representing all
county education committees, county
borough education committees, regional
education committees, and technical
instruction committees in Northern
Ireland. In other words, it represents
the managers of all provided and transferred public elementary schools, technical
schools, and secondary schools in Northern Ireland. This statement says:-
The Association expresses profound
disappointment and resentment that
in the White Paper proposals, as published, practically no notice has been
taken of its views and recommendations,
particularly on the subject of (a) the
proposal to set up junior secondary
schools, and (b) religious education.
What are their views regarding religious
instruction? This body of representatives of the Ministry's own regional
committees expresses its view—
that the present system of religious
instruction in provided and transferred
schools should continue, and that in
secondary and technical schools to
which scholarships are awarded by the
local education authority religious instruction should not fall below the
standard at present provided and given
in provided and transferred schools.
It is considered most unfortunate that
an agreed settlement of this highlycontentious matter was not negotiated
privately between the various interests
concerned before publication of the
White Paper.
It was indeed unfortunate, particularly
in view of the assurance given by Lord
Craigavon, the head of the Unionist
Government at that time, that no contentious legislation would be introduced

during the period of the war.
objection to these proposals of the Government is that they are not on all fours

I need hardly point out that in the

The Minister has been badly briefed at

terms of the English Act, in what are
called controlled schools, which correspond to our transferred and provided
schools, and which are entirely under the

of positions which will be open only to
teachers who are willing to enter into a

than any contract the churches here have
suggested. These controlled schools in
Education-

THE SENATE
were
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
England were church schools, and the
founders of the school
naturally
interested in the tradition of the school
and were hopeful it would be maintained.
In order to meet the wishes of Church
representatives in England provision was
made in the Act for the appointment in
these schools of a considerable number of
"reserved" teachers, whose duty it would
be, as part of the terms of their engagement, to give religious instruction not
merely in accordance with an agreed programme, but in accordance with the
religious tenets of the founders of the
schools.
It is quite true that the type of
religious instruction required of these
teachers is denominational, but that does
not affect the principle that there will be
thousands of appointments in the schools
in England to which can be appointed
only teachers, who are prepared to enter
into a contract to give religious instruction, and who will not have what the
Minister calls the most complete freedom
in this matter. All we ask for in Ulster
is that the regional committees be empowered to make analogous arrangements.
I do not say an identical arrangement,
for, thank God, we have got far beyond
mere denominational teaching in the
schools so far as the Protestant religions
are concerned. We ask the Government
to make analogous arrangements so that
there are an adequate number of willing
teachers in transferred schools where the
parents wish that Bible instruction be
given.
A further reason for resisting these
proposals that are put forward by the
Ministry is that the alternative suggested
for meeting the situation that would be
created if, in a number of schools, the
teachers objected to giving this instruction to the children is, in my opinion and
in the opinion of others with whom I have
consulted, entirely inadequate and unworkable and, indeed, objectionable.
The Minister proposes that education
authorities should be empowered in that
predicament to appoint and pay outside
persons to go into the schools and do this
work. It is not difficult to set out many
serious objections to such a suggestion.
First of all, this person would, in fact, be
a teacher, no matter by what name he
or she be called, and it is only by a
mental subterfuge that anyone could
argue that the difficulty created by the
1920 Act could be. got round by any such
device.
If the regional committee discovered
that it is ultra vires to make an agreement with a teacher in the school to give
this instruction, it would obviously be.
equally ultra vires for the committee to
make a similar agreement with any outside person and pay him or her for doing
that same work in the school. The
second objection is that these special
persons, whom the Minister thinks might
go into the schools, would perhaps be
more difficult to find than he thinks.
He suggests that Sunday school teachers
might find their way into the schools,
and also ministers. I would remind the
Minister of Education that Sunday
school teachers are nearly always very
busy people who are occupied during the
whole day in their ordinary vocation, and
could not, therefore, attend in the
schools during school hours to give this
religious instruction.
In addition to that, the Minister does
not seem to have any idea how illusive
these Sunday school teachers can be..
even on Sundays, and even when they
make an agreement that they will carry
out this work. I think it would be
equally utterly impossible to find enough
clergy to do anything more than a mere
fraction of the work so splendidly done
along these lines by the teachers themselves in the. schools. In some areas of
Belfast it would require double the
number of clergymen there are to spend
their time for anything up to four or
five hours every day doing this school
work. Where are the Ministry going to
get these men, and who is going to pay
them? I suppose they would get
get a

modicum from the
Education.
The third objection to this is that it
unfairly discriminates amongst teachers
in the matter of payment. If an outside
person is going to be paid for this work.
why could not the inside man be paid
for the job? Teachers naturally will say,
"If the outsiders are paid for this work,
why should I continue to do it for nothing," and in a short time. the whole of
this work will be rolled on to the

committee and the country. But the

proposal to
