What type of vitiated atmosphere would be. created in schools by
that placarding and advertising of religious differences before the minds of little
children? What would be the value of
Bible instruction given in a school, the
whole atmosphere of which was vitiated
in that way?
That brings me to the conclusion of the
criticism of these proposals of the Government so far as what I might call moral
considerations are concerned. Let me in
concluding refer briefly to the so-called.
legal argument put forward against this
settlement arrived at by the Government
of Lord Craigavon in 1930. The 1930 Act
is, we are told, ultra vires-that these
things simply cannot be done. Bible instruction cannot be made the duty of any
teacher in a transferred or regional committee school, because it would be illegal.
I want very briefly to sum up my own
opinion in this matter, and in doing so I
recognise the fact that I am not a lawyer.
First of all the 1930 Act is the law of the
land, and will remain the law in a legal
sense until it is repealed by Parliament
or declared to be null and void by a competent court before which all parties will
have the right to be heard. This law
cannot be vetoed by any person, no matter
how exalted he may be, even if he is
Attorney-General. Though it is incorporated in a new Bill it cannot be vetoed
in any new Act seeing that it is already
the law of the land.
May I say that it was approved and
acquiesced in by the highest legal authorities in 1930, both here and at Westminster, and that being so I have as good
a right as any other man to say that it is
good law. My second proposition is that
taking into account the whole situation to
which the 1920 Act was intended to
apply, the prohibition in it against
establishing or endowing any religion.
was obviously intended to prevent
the establishment or endowing of any
particular organised form of religion as
against any other similar form. That is
the obvious meaning of the words in the
setting in which the Act was based, and to
which it was intended to apply. It was
never meant, I submit, to debar the Government from recognising the religious
realities that are recognised by all religious in this land, nor was it ever intended
to debar the Government from encouraging the perpetuation of the fundamental
principles and beliefs that are common to
all religions and all organised forms of religion practised by the Ulster people. The
agreed programme of Bible instruction
which, by the 1920 Act, is to be taught
in the schools is not the programme drawn
up in the interests of any particular
Church or any particular religion. It is
a programme that has been adopted and
approved by the regional committees, who
are representative of all religions. What
religion, then, is endowed or established
by Bible instruction in the schools? I
cannot discover it.
Why, if the 1920 Act is to be interpreted in such a stringent way as to
rule out the possibility of simple Bible
instruction we will have to do away with
many other things to which we have
become accustomed. We shall have, for
example, to dismiss our honoured
chaplains who come to this House to lead
us in prayer.
I think that would be a
pity. I do not see that their visits do
us any harm. We shall have to cease
to make any pretence at having chaplains
in the Stranmillis Training College, or the
pretence of giving help and aid to the
teachers in training there on the
means and methods of religious instruction. Stranmillis Training College under
this stringent reading of the 1920 Act will
have become what I am afraid it was
originally intended to be a Godless institution. The education authorities
will have to cease to think about collective
acts of worship at the beginning of the
school day. They will also clearly be
illegal as tending to establish a particular
religion. Nor will it be legal in the
future, no matter how many Churches
are blitzed in Belfast, for the regional
committee to lend their buildings even
for temporary religious purposes. I need
not give examples of further absurdities
of trying to interpret a sensible Act of
Parliament in a nonsensical way.
My final proposition is this.
If I am
wrong, and Bible instruction is eventually
shown to be contrary to the terms of the
1920 Act, then a very serious situation
Education
Housing (No. 2) Bill
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
will have been created in Ulster, and the
trouble that exists at present will be
nothing compared with the agitation that
will raise its head. A great deal of
valuable property has been transferred
by the Churches to the Government on
the basis of church deeds which guarantee
that Bible instruction would be continued
in those schools. If the terms of those
deeds cannot be implemented because of
this strange, stringent, modern interpretation of the 1920 Act, what becomes of
the claim of the Government to the
possession of these schools? Surely the
Protestant Churches in equity would
have a strong case for demanding that
these schools should be returned to them
in order that they may be used for the
purpose for which they were builtsecular and separate religious instruction.
In that unhappy event, should it ever
take place, the educational system of
Northern Ireland would be thrown back
25 years, and the country would be disturbed by a serious division in its ranks.
There are many other matters, and in
particular the constitution and powers of
school management committees and the
training of teachers, in which
Churches are deeply interested, but I have
not time to refer to these matters now.
the
My last word is this. In my opinion
the great majority of the people of Ulster
are wholly opposed to any Measure which
would tend to belittle the importance of
religious instruction as a normal part of
the work of the schools where the parents
desire that such instruction should be
given. I regret delaying the House at
such length, but I felt it incumbent upon
me to express myself freely and frankly
in regard to these matters, as I have
always done and will continue to do.

The Tillage Order
30 JANUARY 1945
ADJOURNMENT.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I beg to
move, "That this House do now adjourn
until Tuesday next.'
""
Major DOBBS: At what time?
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: 2-30.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I promised
Senator Leslie that I would get an answer
from the Ministry of Agriculture on a
point he raised on the Adjournment of
the House the last time we sat. The
answer that I have received is as follows :
With a view to inducing farmers to
undertake early ploughing the Tillage
Order has for the past few years provided that one-quarter of the tillage
quota must be ploughed before 1st
January. The Ministry is aware that
owing to the difficulties experienced in
saving the harvest of 1944 and to the
subsequent adverse weather conditions.
farmers have been able to make only
limited progress with ploughing. Consequently it is not proposed to take
action against any occupier who failed
to have one-quarter of his 1945 tillage
quota ploughed before 1st January,
1945. It should, however, be clearly
understood that the obligation imposed
by the Tillage Order on each occupier
to plough and crop the full quota still
remains.
Mr. LESLIE: But he cannot.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Adjourned accordingly at Ten
Minutes after Four o'clock
until Tuesday next.

THE SENATE.

Thursday, 8th February, 1945.

EDUCATION.
RECONSTRUCTION PROPOSALS.
(Second Day.)
Order read for resuming adjourned
debate on Question (30th January),
"That this House welcomes the intention
of the Government to introduce legislation for the improvement of the educational system of Northern Ireland.".
(Sir Roland Nugent.)
Question again proposed.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I hope in a very
few moments to prove very conclusively
that the regime of the Northern Government for the last 20 years, so far as
education is concerned, has been retrograde and reactionary, and a total failure.
I think there are two tests for the success
or failure of education, namely, the
spiritual and the material. I will take
the spiritual first. Under the old system
of the Commissioners of National Education all the schools, Protestant and
Catholic, had very full and complete
religious instruction. All Catholic
schools were entitled to teach Catholic
doctrine, and all Protestant schools were
not limited to simple Bible teaching, but
were entitled to teach the whole
Protestant doctrine to Protestant children
in Protestant schools.
Without dwelling on that point I
should just like to say I approve of that,
and I do not speak for myself alone. I
quote the actions and deeds of my Catholic
fellow countrymen, and they have only
done what I would have expected them
to do. The Protestant population of
Eire is not one-third of the population as
we are here. It is a minority of only 7
per cent., and in Eire every Protestant
school is entitled to and receives the same
equal educational advantages as Catholic
schools do. This matter was raised in the
other House by Mr. Campbell, and the
politicians, with the exception of Mr.
Andrews, very wisely evaded a reply.
Mr. Andrews made an unconsidered
statement, which if he had to make it
now would not be made at all. His only
argument and only reference to the
general treatment by the Catholic Government of Eire of the Protestant minority
was that Protestants were compelled to
learn Irish.
Let me just deal with that for a
moment. In Eire there are a good many
Catholics who do not believe in compulsion so far as Irish is concerned. I
myself do not believe in compulsion in
anything. But what is the grievance of
Mr. Andrews? Whether a Catholic
believes in it or not his children have to
learn Irish the same as Protestant
children. And on the bigger question
one of the best Irishmen of this generation, Dr. Douglas Hyde, the chief protagonist of the Irish language and the
founder of the Gaelic League, was
appointed by his Catholic fellow countrymen-and a very wise appointment it
was to the highest office in the land. I
should like to know from Mr. Andrews
if he is prepared to go on any platform in
Wales, Liberal, Conservative, Catholic or
Protestant, and condemn the people of
Wales for preserving their language. I
can imagine the reception he would get.
As I said, under the old system Catholics and Protestants were entitled to
receive full religious instruction in their
schools. Let me come down to bed-rock.
What is the position to-day? Under the
old system every school, Protestant and
Catholic-I am sorry to have to make
these references. I hate, as an Irishman,
to be called upon to do it-every school,
Protestant and Catholic, receives a twothirds minimum grant for equipment of
the school. What is the position here today? Has this legislation of the Northern
Ireland Government been progressive?
Instead of giving two-thirds they give 50
per cent.
I should like the Minister of
Education in his reply not to make
a general statement but to give some concrete facts on which he bases his proposal
to give only 50 per cent. to voluntary
schools and 65 to four-and-two committees.
I should like the Minister when he comes
to reply not to reply by means of a
general statement-we can all do thatbut to give us some concrete facts as to
why he denies that extra 15 per cent. to
voluntary schools.
The MINISTER OF EDUCATION
(Lieut.-Colonel Hall-Thompson): I do not
deny the extra 15 per cent. I give an
additional 15 per cent. to the four-and-two
•

bring the Minister of Education down to

he does that and gives only 50 per cent. to

come down to the absurdity of the whole

give 75 per cent. to youth welfare schemes.
In other words the idea of education in
the Ministry of Northern Ireland is to give
and
75 per cent. for skittles, darts

that a fair interpretation of the position?
and
I do not intend to take up any unnecessary time. I have tried to address myself
to the germane facts so far as they affect
those whom I represent. I do say there
are many things in this White Paper commendable and acceptable and of which I
heartily approve. For instance-I am

in the school age. I do not profess to be

therefore I approve of it. There are

one-third. After all, if you pass legislation that deprives one-third of the population of the benefits of it, can that
legislation be a success? Is not that a
question which answers itself?
con-
Let me say in conclusion that I
was very much impressed and touched
by the peroration of the right hon.
Gentleman the Leader of the House.
I was tremendously impressed by his

science which is the greatest freedom of

Mr. LESLIE: I must confess that the
White Paper, of which we are to-day continuing the discussion, seems to me to be
a wonderful piece of work. It is very
well thought out, it is well expressed, and
it evinces a very sound knowledge of the
great and complicated issues with which
it deals. I take it upon myself to say
that the White Paper reflects the very
greatest credit on those who are responsible for its production and presentation to this House: We must, I think,
throughout our discussion keep it constantly in mind that what we are discussing is a White Paper; it is not a Bill.
The White Paper is really something in
the nature of a treatise which is to be
used at a later date as a foundation of
a Bill-a Bill which in its turn will
have to be submitted to Parliament and
be subject to scrutiny throughout all the
normal Parliamentary stages. What
that Bill will be is not finally decided yet.
It may be that what is said here and in
another place may have some bearing on
the details. That is for the future. It
is not for to-day.
But, as I have said, the White Paper
is a very fine piece of work, and I am
very glad that the presentation of this
Paper to this House synchronises with

right hon. Gentleman, the present

I am sorry to say very few of them are

that we should erect or plant a little

that great wood, because if we did there
was a danger that we should obscure our
vision, and, in fact, not be able to see
the wood in its magnificent verdure, be-

THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
discussing the White Paper-that the

The second line of thought does not go
on the same lines as what I have called
the canons. These are things to be desired
-matters which are perhaps more capable of being dealt with by administration
than by inclusion in the actual Act of
Parliament. I will come to those again,
but they are not of the same importance
as what I call, again for the want of a
better word, these canons. We cannot
separate the two things. We cannot, I
think, separate altogether that which is
required of us and that which we ourselves would like to do. I have six of
these maxims or canons if the House
would tolerate me while I refer to them.
First of all-and I think it is right to
put it in the first place you may take it
as an established fact that these Six
Counties are controlled by Parliament,
not by any other body, nor by any individual or band of individuals, either
great or small, outside the walls of Parliament. That, I think, is the first and
most necessary foundation.
Secondly, that control which I have
just spoken of is itself subject to control.
The proceedings of this Parliament are
controlled by Statute. Our Charter in
that respect is more like the Charter of
the United States, which is expressed in
a written Constitution, than like a Charter
such as that great Charter which we have
in Great Britain, which rested upon
tradition and statute law earlier than that
in the written document. That controlling statute is undoubtedly the Act
of 1920, of which a particular part is in
everybody's mind at present and to which
I must refer, namely, Sections 5 and 8.
I regret the necessity, but there they are
and we cannot help it. It follows from
the binding force of those two sections
that we
can attempt no compulsion on
anybody as regards any matter of conscience. Neither parents nor children
nor teachers can be compelled. Any
attempt at compulsion is, in my judgment
-and I am not alone in my opinion-a
contravention of that 1920 Act and also
futile. No use must be made of education for anything in the nature of
proselytising a child belonging to this or
that form of faith.
The third of these canons-and this
canon is not laid down in any statute of
which I know-is that every child in the
Province must be given an opportunity
of climbing the educational ladder from
the lowest rung to the highest. That, I
think, we accept as an axiom. I do not
think it is laid down in so many words
in any statute. This ladder of which I
have spoken has no terrestial limit.
It is,
I think, abundantly clear that this canon
is forced on us by our own people.
I say
forced, but I do not say that they are
not pushing against an open door. That
is not the point at the moment.
Our people undoubtedly are determined that our children shall be taught
that Book which the majority of them
do regard as the Word of God, and that
they shall receive that instruction from
teachers who themselves admit that great
Book as their rule of life who are prepared to follow it themselves, and are,
therefore, willing to teach it to the
children under their care in order that
the children in their turn may form their
characters upon it. That is, I think, a
canon which this House, even if it wished
to-and I do not think it does wish to
I could not obscure. The people of the
Province simply would not have it.
Passing from that to another serious
subject, one of these canons is that public
money must go hand in hand with public
control, or rather perhaps the other way
about-that where public money is spent
public control must go with it.
If you
are spending public money you must give
the public that provides that money the
right, to some extent at all events, to control the method in which that money is
spent. When we are dealing with public
money we come to the last of the canons,
and that is that the expenditure we incur and the money we spend must be
spent in strict accordance with the true
basis of that which is called the Colwyn
Award. We cannot justify expenditure
in this country which is, so to speak, out
of proportion with regard to our population and so on with that of the United
Kingdom. The reason is obvious. The
money that is spent comes to a great extent from taxes which are levied not by
this Parliament but by the Imperial
Parliament, and it is the allocation and
administration of the money so received
that came under review when the Colwyn
Award was discussed and arrived at. I

shall return to some of these canons in a

I pass to other matters that I call
desiderata which are more susceptible of
management by Regulation than by express wording in an Act of Parliament.
The curious will find a long list of 24
matters of this kind dealt with in paragraph 97 of the White Paper before us.
I am going to comment on two or three
of them, but I am sure the House will be
glad to know that I do not propose to go
through the whole 24. Those that I do.
think are perhaps of more importance
than others are, like the. canons, six in
number. First of all, it is desirable from
our point of view that parental control and supervision should be most
scrupulously maintained. Secondly, in
any scheme we may bring forward there
is every need of the utmost elasticity.
That is because of the very different
conditions in different parts of our area
among different sections of our population. We have got to make sure that the
Bill to be founded on this White Paper
provides this elasticity, and I may say
that, perusing the White Paper fairly
carefully, I find every evidence that
elasticity has not been lost sight of. I
think it has been very carefully borne
in mind.
Again, the problem is the better education of the child. I do want it to be
remembered that the word "education"
is improperly pronounced. The word is
education. You want to bring out of
the child that which is in it; you do not
want to stuff knowledge down its mental
throat. We want to avoid anything in
the nature of cramming, a word which, I
hope, is passing into desuetude. There
must not be anything approaching cramming. There, must not be any excessive
amount of what is called home work, and
there must be an endeavour to bring out
that breadth of view which is inherent in
the unspoiled child. I want to add something which should be put into the minds
of children, and that is the duty of kindness to animals. I rather want that in
the Bill when it comes before us. It is
a matter in which something has already
been done, because there was a circular
issued not long ago dealing with that
subject.
Then, again, it is desirable, in fact
necessary, that the education of the child
should be conducted in comfortable surroundings. That is, that school premises
must be of a proper size, and many of
them are not. They must be properly
ventilated and lighted. Neither light nor
ventilation is all we could desire in many
of our country schools, and we have
always to keep in mind the difference
between town and country. Things can
be done in a large town, and even in a
small town, which cannot, under present
arrangements, be carried out in either a
country village or in adjacent townlands,
in one
or other of which one of these
schools is situated. Further, we must not
proceed too fast. We must remember
that Rome was not built in a day, and we
must not rush any new arrangements we
are bringing in at a pace, with which the
locality cannot keep up. Again, I do not
see that the White Paper offers any
reasonable ground for anxiety as to an
intention to proceed with undue haste.
It seems to me clear, from more than one
passage, that it is not expected that the
whole thing will be accomplished, metaphorically speaking, in five minutes.
mean
The last of these things to be desired
is that the new schools should have
attractive names. The proposal is to
have nursery schools, primary schools,
junior and senior secondary schools, and
technical schools. There is certainly no
objection to the term nursery school, but
I do not particularly care for the word
primary. The object of primary seems to
me to serve as introductory to secondary,
and secondary is a term which I hate.
When you talk of a thing being secondary
there is always a doubt as to whether you
secondary below or secondary
higher up. I think that is the main
objection to secondary, and the use of the
word secondary seems to imply the use of
the word primary. I cannot but think
that I would rather see our schools described first as nursery, and, secondly,
why not keep the old word elementary?
Why introduce primary, except as introductory to secondary? Now you come to
the next school. What is wrong with
general school, or preparatory schoolgeneral for choice-followed by advanced
school or technical school where circumstances require. Those are suggestions
which seem possibly worth while considering.

To reduce the lower limit of compulsory attendance from 6 to 5-
That, I think, is the existing lawbut giving education committees power
to raise it, with the approval of the
Ministry of Education, to 5 or 6,
either in part or the whole of their
areas.
I do not like that proposal and I never
did. Your committee is dealing with a
child of six to the best of its knowledge
and belief, but that committee is not the
parent of the child. You cannot deal
satisfactorily with that matter by considering it in terms of locality, even in
terms of part locality; the item to be
considered is in every case the individual
child. That is one of the passages where
this question of elasticity comes in. I
think there must be some good provision
for the protection of the child who, for
some reason or other, is not at that early
age fit to travel the distance to his school.
Again, that difficulty will arise more in
the country than in the towns, and it is
not going to be solved by dealing with the
matter in those terms.
The next paragraph talks of raising the
school age to 15 without exemptions and to
take power to raise it subsequently to 16.
I have no objection to the raising of the
school age to 16, provided it is done, not
as an act of administration, but after
reference ad hoc to Parliament, and with
the same elasticity. It may be done with
the best intentions in the world, but it
may not be in the best interests of the
child to raise the age to 16. The parents
may be the best judges-very often they
are not-and that is a case where the
utmost elasticity is necessary if the enterprise. is not to end in failure.

make it compulsory between the ages of

with
medical inspection and the compulsory

This is a very dangerous subject, very

Sub-paragraph (xxiv) is very controversial. This is the revocation of the arrangement whereby a county may act through
more than one regional education committee. We are told in the White Paper
that there are three counties which act
through one committee and three which
do not. My recollection of what happened in 1923 is that the fight was rather
to prevent the counties merging themselves into one committee. The theory at
that time was that a regional committee
in quantity was the best method. Now
opinion is changing. I confess that I
have not any strong opinion on
subject. Speaking generally, I am very
much inclined to favour one committee,
but my difficulty-and I am an Antrim.
man-is that Antrim is of a rather inconvenient shape to be run by one committee. It is very long and distances
are considerable.
the
Antrim is one of the counties which up
to the present maintain the three regional
committee system. There are advantages,
as pointed out in the White Paper, and
there are disadvantages, but the question
is whether the maintenance of local
interest in theory has any valid effect in
practice. I am very much inclined to be
afraid that your ordinary regional educational committee does not take much
interest in education except in the
appointing of a teacher. I have observed
myself on occasions that the appointment
of a teacher would be very much better
in the hands of a central board. If you
have got committees you have got to
staff the central board, and if you have
only one committee you have got to staff
that committee. I confess it is a matter
upon which I have not a very strong
opinion, and I should not feel disposed to
controvert, or attempt to controvert, any
definite proposal on that subject.

Finally, we may fairly say that the
White Paper on the whole does answer
the tests I endeavoured to suggest to the
House should be applied. I think it is
likely to produce success in the future,
so I commend it to the House. Before
sitting down I should like to inquire from
the right hon. Gentleman about the Bill.
I should like to know something about
the mechanism by which it is going to be
brought about. Is it intended to scrap
all the Education Acts at present in
existence? If that is so, it is a question
whether after the resulting Bill has received a Second Reading in both Houses
it should not be referred to a Joint Committee, as is done, as we know, with very
considerable advantage in the case of
Consolidation Bills.
I think that that is a course that might
be considered, because I am assuming
that when this Bill comes in it will evoke
plenty of criticism in both Houses, but
taking it in the main I assume it will not
be regarded as a party question. It is
not a party question, it is a national
question, and that Joint Committee might
be found to be of considerable service.
That is a point which might not be settled
to-day, but it will receive consideration
in the future. For the second time
within a brief period I thank the House
for its consideration and forbearance.

of hourly talk. With regard to the remarks made by Senator Cunningham

without their elders, I think he is quite

Education--
Reconstruction Proposals
THE SENATE
[Mr. Stevenson]
I am on the roads every day. My experience is that the tiny tots always go

on bicycles or go home quickly. Day
after day on the roads I meet these little
ones aged 6, 8 and 9 years, and they are
always by themselves. I do not think
there is any danger there in breaking up
the children at 11 plus.
The great drawback to the 11 plus in
the opinion of a great many people in the

it is almost an axiom now that there
should be a break-that so many children
are backward at 11 and do not develop.
I think it is rather hard that they should

If the numbers are not too large it

who develop at 12 or 13 years of age
should have every opportunity of going
in for higher education. With regard to
the means test, generally speaking it is
not liked, and I think it should be done
away with. I know that education committees find it a very difficult question to
deal with. It leads to a certain amount
of evasion on the part of the parents when

In addition, well-to-do parents are paying income tax and rates for the education
of their children in any case, and I think
it is a question that might very well be

entirely for the kitchen staff. Meals
should be cooked properly and put on the
table.
I think it would be a very great asset

the quality of the meal, watch the
behaviour of the children, and teach them
simple table manners. (Hon. Members:
Hear, hear.) All children have got to be
so taught, and I think it would be a very
great help. I read a very interesting
article a short time ago on public schools.
It was written by a man who was not
a public school man and who had no love
for the old school tie. He said that in
studying the question he was bound to
come to the conclusion, apart from all
questions of class distinction or snobbery,
that there was something in public school
education that fitted children to become
candidates for high positions. Amongst
other things he attributed that to the
close touch between the masters and
children. In that connection he referred
to the masters having the children in
class, meeting them at sport, and being
with them at table. I think it would be
a decided asset to the forthcoming Education Bill if that could in some way be
brought into being.

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
should like to congratulate the right hon.
and gallant Gentleman, the Minister of
Education, on achieving a real advance
in the education of this country. Of
course, the White Paper cannot please
everyone. In fact, it is a compromise,
and so it may not in a sense fully please
everyone. But this fact is a mere indication of the immense difficulties of the
subject rather than an indication of inadequate treatment by the Government. I
believe the White Paper will prove to be
carefully thought out and an honest, and,
I believe, an effective attempt to solve
these difficulties. We in our generation
have been through perhaps the hardest
times of any generation since the Middle
Ages. We have been through two major
wars, we have seen a world devastated,
Education-
Reconstruction Proposals
8 FEBRUARY 1945
and we have been through the worst trade
collapse for a century. I think that if
there is one thing we are all agreed on it
is the complete and absolute failure of
the systems of government and administration relying on the self-sufficiency of
man.
After such a devastating time one cannot be surprised that a great many people
of intellect in this country are slightly
agnostic, and that that is not only the
result of the last war. There was for
some years before the last war a surging
up of what was called liberal philosophy
in Germany, and at the same time there
was a most remarkable advance in
scientific development due to freedom
of thought. I believe this so-called
agnosticism which people talk so much
about is merely reaction stimulated by
these transient factors, and that, like all
other reactions, it will die, and there are
signs already that it is dying. If we take
children of that tender age from their
parents and put them for a whole day to
school we must supply them with some
alternative for the mother's teaching. It
is no good saying that many mothers are
too busy or too ignorant to teach religion.
That is all the more reason why the school
should teach it. But there are a majority
of mothers who, if the children were at
home, would not teach them religion, and
I think the schools must take their place.
For that reason I am glad that the right
hon. and gallant Gentleman has made
such a strong point of the teaching of
religion to children at school.
But it is not enough just to give them
Bible teaching; what we want for the
future is an adequate philosophy of life..
When you have these immense strides
which science is making in material
development and technology, unless you
have a proper and reasonable philosophy
of life you are not a fit person to take
charge of these matters, and even those
who are agnostically minded amongst the
intelligentsia realise that agnosticism is
a negative creed and must be replaced by
something much more constructive.
believe school teachers are in the van in
thinking that it is their duty, whatever
their own feelings and abilities may be,
to give to the children something better
than they themselves possess, and not to
harness their minds to their own feelings,
or to obstruct or obscure the development
I
of a proper philosophy of life in the minds
of the children.
This White Paper has given rise to a
strange paradox. A teacher may contract out of teaching the Bible because
he is too heathen, or because he feels
himself incapable of teaching it, although
a teacher cannot contract out of teaching
the Dialogues of Plato because, he is too
Christian to teach a heathen philosophy.
I think the Government will have to be
very careful in dealing with this matter
and not make contracting out too wide,
because it seems to me quite clear that
under the Act of 1920 a teacher must
have a religious reason for wishing to
contract out, and I think that the word
"religion" should not be given too wide
a scope. It is essential that religion
should be taught in the. right way. At
the same time, I think the Government
will run into very serious difficulties if
they allow this contracting out to be too
widespread. It is also a curious paradox
that, if it were not for Christianity the
teacher would have no conscience to
stimulate him to contract out. I remember a story of G. K. Chesterton, in which
he described a discussion between Cosmos
and the Sceptic. Cosmos says:
"How
can you use the instruments provided by
me. to deny my existence? Permit me
to reduce you to nothing, and then we
can discuss it." That is really the
answer. If it were not for Christianity
the teacher would have no conscience.
He would probably now be a slave, at the
dictation of some Oriental despot.
I think the Churches have had a very
difficult time, and one should realise that
if it were not for the Churches the liberal
philosophers who did their best to destroy
places of Christianity would have succeeded, or very nearly succeeded, and I
do think it is not for the Churches to
accept very willingly any compromise
which in their view will damage the
teaching of Christianity. They must
watch every compromise most carefully,
and I would suggest to them that it is not
the path of true statesmanship to reject
this White Paper, either on the ground
that they wish to force the Government
to take up a position which is untenable,
or which the Government have
advised by their own advisers is untenable. If they do not accept this White
Paper. I think they will be putting the
clock back, though they may hope for
been
Education-
Reconstruction Proposals
THE SENATE
[Major Perceval-Maxwell]
better things in the future and strive in
every way to get them.
The real objection to the White Paper,
from a religious point of view, is that it
may not work.
I think everyone realises
that it may not work. I personally think
it will. If it does not the Government
will be called upon to do something else,
but it is wrong for anyone to suggest,
until it is proved up to the hilt, that
teachers as a body, or to a large extent,
are not willing to work it. Personally I
think they are. I think they have a high
sense of their duty as teachers which will
enable. them to put forward their own
views, to the exclusion of views which
they feel are not best for the children to
have.
This question of religious education has
rather tended to throw into the shadow
some very important aspects of this White
Paper. The first is the question of the
- 11 plus age. I know that in saying this
I am going against the Hadow Report,
but that was not a report on education in
Northern Ireland. Many children live
in out of the way areas, and until they
go to school they have had no chance of
associating with their fellows. I think
that in Northern Ireland 12 plus would
be a very much better age and would
enable us to know whether the children
should go to a junior or senior secondary
school.
At the same time I think the age of 11
plus is likely to destroy many country
schools, and that means that many children of five and six will have to walk long
distances to their elementary school, or
will have to be brought in transport. It
may be possible to bring children in
transport to the secondary school, but it
will be quite impracticable to supply
transport for children attending the
elementary schools of this country. It
will be very difficult to supply the necessary elementary schools if children have
to go from widely separated areas.
think 12 plus would be a more satisfactory
age for going to the secondary school,
even though the Government might afterwards go back to 11 plus. I do not believe that at the age of 11 plus, or even
12 plus, an examination would be a satisfactory test. I think something in the
nature of an aptitude test would have to
be devised by the Ministry to decide
whether children should go to the junior
or senior secondary school.
I
The other thing I wish to deal with is
the question of children who are deformed
in mind, a sad subject, but one which
one cannot ignore on a debate such as
this. The actual number of children
whose mental deficiency has been
diagnosed is very small, amounting to
a few hundred, but if you compare the
population of Northern Ireland with the
population of England, unit for unit, it
is probable, in fact I think it can be
satisfactorily proved, that the number of
mental deficients in Northern Ireland,
instead of being a few hundred, amounts
to some thousands, and there is a possibility that the actual number of mental
deficients in Northern Ireland exceeds,
per unit of the population, the number
in other parts of Great Britain. That
is a very serious position and one
which cannot be cured by any Education
Act.
I have maintained, and maintain still,
that the only way of dealing with this
subject is to do it on a general basis, as
a matter of general public health. The
trouble we have in this country is that
there is no school of mental health. The
university provides a course, and there is
a lectureship, but it is not satisfactory,
and if a doctor wishes to get a diploma of
mental health he has to go to Dublin or
some other university. There is no system
of training in experimental psychology,
and until there is you cannot expect
medical practitioners in this country to
have an adequate knowledge of normal
mental health, and so they cannot be
relied on to have an adequate knowledge
of diagnosing abnormal mental health,
nor can they satisfactorily distinguish between just mental ill-health and those
moral and mental disorders which are set
up in youth, due to bad training and bad
homes, which are psychopathic and which
are curable, and which must be cured in
the child's home.
Until we have a satisfactory mental
deficiency Bill, and until the Ministry
of Health wake up to the serious nature
of the mental ill-health that surrounds
the children we will never have a satisfactory system of cure at all. We will
never have a satisfactory system for meetting this terrible scourge of child crime
which the judges are always condemning,
and which a committee, under Sir Robert
Lynn as chairman, considered many years
ago without any effective action having
been taken since.
Education-
Reconstruction Proposals
8 FEBRUARY 1945
The only other thing about which I
wish to speak is the question of the
nomenclature of the schools. The question of secondary schools is one that
should be dealt with. I think that calling them junior and secondary schools is
a very bad thing. It may damage their
reputation. The word "secondary" is
bad Latin and worse English. It is quite
satisfactory to distinguish between preliminary and secondary education, but it
is unsatisfactory to talk about a secondary
school. I remember the great surprise I
received when my tutor at Eton informed
me that I was attending a secondary
school, a thing I had never realised. It
is perfectly absurd to talk about one
section as being secondary schools and
exclude the others.
The words "secondary education" apply
to the collegiate schools of the Middle
Ages, the grammar schools of the Elizabethan period and later, and to all schools
which provide pre-university education.
I hope the right hon. Gentleman will
give his attention to this matter and see
to it that a more satisfactory name is given
in order to distinguish between these two
schools. One is for those who pay or can
win a scholarship, and the other is for
children of a county. That being so the
word "county" should figure in the name
of the school. If you wish to use good
Latin you can call it a college; if you wish
to use good Greek you can call it an
academy, and if you wish to use good
Saxon you can call it a school. I suggest
that the right hon. Gentleman should investigate the names that used to be given
to the ancient seats of learning in Downpatrick and Armagh in the fifth and sixth
centuries, places to which young men from
all over Europe came to be educated. If
the right hon. Gentleman does not want
my suggestions about Latin, Greek or
Saxon he can go to some one more learned
and get them. (Laughter.)
Lieut.-Colonel
HALL-THOMPSON:
In the first place, may I thank the House

very nice

In reply to Major Perceval-Maxwell I
say we do not like the terms "junior
secondary" and "senior secondary.'
They were put into the White Paper
pending the receipt of suggestions. I am
glad to say we have received many suggestions-none of them is better than those
suggested in this House to-day-and we
will give very careful consideration to
them. I have no doubt but that we will
make some change in the names of the
schools, but what that change will be I
am not at the moment prepared to say.
So far as mental defectives are concerned I can assure the hon. and gallant
Senator that the Education Committee
are alive to the necessity of dealing with
this very important matter. I am not
unmindful of the recommendations made
by the Health Committee, which sat for
12 months. In due time the Minister of
Health and Local Government and myself
will try to put forward something which
we hope will help to solve this very difficult question. We shall have to consider
whether you can treat them better in an
institution than you can in their own
homes. The parents are most reluctant
to part with such children. Again, if
you took them away during the day time
would the training you could give them
during the day not be upset by the surroundings they found themselves in at
night? These are problems which will
have to be solved before we can arrive at
a definite conclusion. It would be premature for me to put forward any
suggestion
moment.

that it is a White Paper put forward for

giving the matter further consideration.

THE SENATE

[Lieut.-Colonel Hall-Thompson]
will call the junior
We want that trans-
The age of transfer
may be slightly under 11; it may be
slightly over 12. What we are aiming at
is that the children to be transferred
should have completed the fourth standard, and then, instead of going into the
fifth standard, that they should go to the
junior secondary schools. That decision
was not arrived at rashly. The Hadow
Committee-it sat in 1926-after due
consideration of the matter decided that
11 plus was the right age. When the
Butler White Paper and the Bill were
being considered the matter was gone into
thoroughly and the same conclusion
arrived at. Our Committee of Investigation thrashed this matter out. They
were not unanimously of the opinion that
11 plus was the proper age, but after
much debate and consideration of the
matter they agreed that 11 plus was the
most suitable age for Northern Ireland,
whether or not it was the proper age on
the other side of the Channel.
We feel that if you keep a child at the
public elementary school for a further
year it is to a large extent only wasting
its time. Unfortunately, in the country
districts-I am thinking more of the
country districts than I am of the city,
because the city can do things that the
county cannot do the teachers, for the
present at any rate, have very large
classes and in most cases it is impossible
for them to give adequate attention to
all the children. The result is that they
have to concentrate most of their attention on the younger children who need
looking after from a disciplinary point
of view, and they have to leave the older
children to do things that will occupy
their time rather than educate them.
That means that after a child has been a
year in the fourth standard it cannot
receive the personal attention that is
necessary.
Again, we want the children to have a
four-year course at the junior secondary
school. I think most educationists agree
that a four-year course is necessary. The
first two years may be occupied by a
general course, and the second two years
may have a more practical bias.
In
addition, our population is so sparse,
especially in the, country, that if you cut.
off one vear from the junior secondary
I am
school course you would be cutting off
20,000 pupils. The cutting off of 20,000
pupils would mean fewer schools.
assuming, in the meantime, that these
junior secondary schools will have accommodation for a minimum of 200 pupils
and a maximum of 400. If you took all
those thousands of pupils away it would
mean that the schools would be few and
far between in the country districts, and
the distances to be travelled by the
children would be too great.

hoped that the local education authorities

Lieut.-Colonel HALL-THOMPSON:
While we are discussing the question of

five and six years of age whether the age
would be five or six? There will be elasticity in this matter in the same way as

children leaving the primary and going to
the junior secondary school. A considerable amount of discretion will be allowed
to the local authority. We know that
where there are older boys or girls in the
family it is quite. an easy matter for them
to take a child of five to school with them.
We also know that, if the parents live in
a backward part of the country and there
is no older boy or girl going to school,
to ask a child of five to attend school
might be a hardship. It will be left to
Education-
Reconstruction Proposals
8 FEBRUARY 1945
the discretion of the local authority as
to whether such a child should be allowed
to remain at home until it is five-and-ahalf or six years of age. In such matters
commonsense must be used, and there
must be give and take on both sides. I
have no doubt that with goodwill on both
sides these little difficulties will be
overcome.
Mr. Stevenson dealt with the 11 plus,
and he also mentioned those who develop
slowly. It is our intention to try to meet

so, if at the age of 13 a child is recommended by the headmaster or the master,
as the case may be, as being willing,
anxious and fit to take advantage of the
senior secondary education such child will
be given an opportunity of doing so.

Lieut.-Colonel HALL THOMPSON :
Let me give an illustration. It is very
much easier to make special laws when
you have only 7 per cent. to legislate for
than when you have 33 per cent. If a
man comes to me and asks me for something and I know there are not 99 others
standing outside the door seeking to get
the same concession, I am quite willing
to give it to him, but if I know I am
going to set a precedent, and I know
there are 99 others waiting for it, I shall
go more cautiously. I shall be in a
position to set a different precedent to a
small percentage than it is possible to do
when there is a large percentage.
I want to emphasise this point. There
is no question of the religion of the individual. It is a question of a democratic
principle. If any denomination insist in
running their schools themselves in spite
of the fact that we pay the salaries of the
teachers, etcetera, and that we make contributions from either the rates or the
State, or both, then we say we should
have some voice in the administration of
that school. It is on a principle of

Senator Leslie asked, I think, about the

In due course it will
be extended to 16. Power is being taken
now to extend it without a new Act of
Parliament, but Parliament will have to
sanction it. None of these things will be
put through without the approval of
Parliament, and there will be a full opportunity for considering them in due course,

With reference to university scholarships, we want uniformity of practice, and
we also want to act in line with the rest
of the United Kingdom. Then there is
compulsory medical inspection. As I
Education-
Reconstruction Proposals
8 FEBRUARY 1945
said earlier, I sat on the Select Committee
on Health Services. There were 73 meetings and I attended all 73 of them. The
evidence given before us led the Committee unanimously to decide that inspection should be compulsory. We had
much evidence to show that in many districts the day the medical officer was
coming was known. On that day a child
would be absent with a sore throat, headache, or some other childish complaint.
That makes certain people a little
suspicious that there was some reason why
that child, or that child's parents, did not
want him or her examined by the medical
officer. And perhaps that child was just
the one that needed medical inspection
more than any other. That, there should
be compulsion was the unanimous finding
of that Committee.
I might say, so far as regional committees are concerned, that Antrim has
not three but five. I am putting it very
mildly when I say that we think it is in
the best interests of education as a whole
that it should be administered, as in three
other counties, on a county basis, because
this is going to be a big scheme, and it
is going to require educational experts to
deal with it. It is going to require
uniformity of practice throughout the
whole county. It is going to need wholetime officers, not retired schoolmasters,
who would give only a few hours a week
or a day to the job. In future you will
want a secretary, call him a director or
whatever you like, for the whole county.
He must be an experienced, first-class
man, and he will be on a whole-time basis.
That can only be dealt with by one big
county committee, not by five little ones.
Of course they can have their school
management committee, who will keep up
what is described as the local touch.

Education-
Reconstruction Proposals
THE SENATE
[Lieut.-Colonel Hall-Thompson]
that there shall be religious instruction
or Bible instruction, that is, the type of
religious instruction, undenominational as
in the past, but not only in every provided and transferred school, but in every
type of school, in the day technical
schools, the junior secondary schools, the
senior secondary schools, in the voluntary
public elementary schools and in the provided and transferred schools. Now you
say, can you do all that, and the
Attorney-General will tell you the last
Attorney-General would have told you
the same that under Section 5 of the
1920 Act you cannot endow, you cannot
show preference to or discriminate
against.
If we are going to pay for religious
instruction in schools-as we are going to
do. That is our proposal-are we going
to propose to endow? That is a matter
for decision. If we are going to say to
the teacher, "You must give religious
instruction in the State schools, the provided and transferred schools," we are
going to discriminate against those who
will not. My proposal is to go ahead and
put into the Bill what we think ought to
be there, and that will not include compulsion, and then the Bill will be submitted to the Attorney-General for his
approval and certificate. And, incidentally, Senor Leslie raised the point this
morning as to whether all previous Bills
will be scrapped. They will. We must
start de novo.
Mr. LESLIE: I think you must.
""
Lieut.-Colonel HALL-THOMPSON:
We must start with the new Bill, and if
the Attorney-General-it is his duty;
he has got to decide-can certify that the
Bill is not contravening the 1920 Act;
if he says, "I can certify it is plain sailing"; well and good. If he says,
as he says at the moment, "I cannot certify, because you are contravening section 5 of the 1920 Act
by endowing the Christian religion,'
then we say "Right. We will read our
Bill a First time so that there will be
something definite in our hands, and then
we take the Bill over to His Majesty's
Privy Council on the other side and get
their decision as to whether it is ultra
vires or not.' That is the position as it
exists at the present moment. I said we
were not prepared to include compulsion
on the teacher. Let me tell you my
""
opinion. It is this. If you insist on
compulsion of the teacher you can change
your Government, but you will not even
then be able to get compulsion on the
teacher, because you will not get any
person on the other side who is an
authority to say, "We agree to or approve
of compulsion on the teacher." They
will not allow you to discriminate against
the teacher. That would be discriminating against if you put compulsion on the
teacher. I know compulsion was there in
the 1930 Act, but that does not permit
us to put it into the 1945 Bill.

also been said that we are adopting a
certain Clause that gives exemption to a
teacher on a very wide scale. That again
is not correct. Clause 4 of the English
Act, which is quoted in that pamphlet,
was submitted by me to the heads of the
churches away back in July last when
many other things were under discussion.
The invariable practice was
that if we were going to discuss certain
subjects with committees we drew up
an agenda. In due course I presented
them with the English Clause, but it was
quickly realised by them, and by me, that
the English Clause of exemption for the
teacher was too provocative and too
drastic for Northern Ireland. Therefore,
have substituted another Clause. This
Clause has been tentatively approved by
the Cabinet. I will read it to you:
If
a teacher in a primary or
secondary school under the manage.
ment of a local authority requests that
on grounds of conscience he be wholly
or partly excused from conducting or
attending collective worship in the
school, or from giving religious instruction in the school, then, until the
request is withdrawn, the teacher shall
be excused accordingly.
Mr. J. CUNNINGHAM: That is the
conscience Clause for an exceptional case
of a teacher who may object to being
obliged to give religious instruction. We
agree to that. There is general agreement
on that.
Lieut.-Colonel HALL
Do you agree to it?

Provided that the education authority

HALL-THOMPSON:
Yes. I am naturally not questioning the

of the Clause in the 1923 Act. That prohibition of giving religious instruction in
the schools was objected to, and the 1925
Act removed that prohibition. As the
right hon. Senator said, the removal of
it meant nothing. It did not say you
shall do it or you shall not do it. It
just left the position as it was except that
that Clause was removed. So much for
the 1925 Act. The 1930 Act went a great
deal further and stated:
If Bible instruction is not provided
by the education authority in any
public elementary school, being a provided school or transferred school, it
shall be the duty of the education
authority to provide Bible instruction,
should the parents of not less than 10
children who are in regular attendance
at such school make application to the
education authority for that purpose in
the prescribed manner-
Here is another prohibition.
. . . Provided that the obligation under
this sub-section shall not arise or continue where there is another public
elementary school in which Bible instruction is given and which in the
opinion of the Ministry such children
can conveniently attend.
Now what are we doing? We are saying there shall be a collective act of worship, and there shall be Bible or other type
of religious instruction in every school in
Ulster. Surely that is going further than
the 1930 Act? You can only have it
under the 1930 Act if 10 parents ask for
it and if there is no suitable school giving
Where the education authority makes
arrangements for Bible instruction in a
provided or transferred school it shall
be the duty of the teachers of the school
to give such instruction, if so required
by the education authority:
What do we say there? We say that
a teacher in a provided or transferred
school-because this does not apply to the
voluntary schools as they can do as they
like and can continue to give it as they
like provided they give religicus instruction-must give such instruction, but if
he or she asks to be excused on grounds
of conscience he or she shall be excused.
I am sorry that I have not put it clearer,
but that is it and it is as clear as I can
put it.

that will be allowed.

HALL-THOMPSON :
That is so obvious that I did not think it

conscience for the parents as well as the

course, that will not be touched at all.
We have no desire to touch it, but if we

and a child who do not want to receive
Bible instruction.
I should like to quote from the speech

House on the first day of this debate, when
I was pleased to hear him say:
:-
Neither I nor the churches have or ever
will have any sympathy with a policy
which would impinge upon the liberty
of conscience or which would do violence
to the scruples of conscience of any
teacher.
Shortly afterwards he said:
Let me quote a sentence of a resolution
passed by a meeting of representatives
Education
Adjournment
8 FEBRUARY 1945
of the Protestant Churches a few weeks
ago. The resolution declared-
The Churches wish to make it clear
that they do not desire violence to be

of Bible instruction.

""
If this is their view, then we are in
entire agreement. What I have found
up to the present is that, as I speak to
individual members of the churches, they
invariably say, I do not believe in
bringing pressure to bear on the teachers.
Perhaps that is not the way to put it,
but they say, "I do not believe in religious
instruction being given by teachers who
do not do so willingly, or who do not
believe what they are teaching, and I do
not want a boy or girl of mine to be
taught what the teachers do not believe
in.' Collectively they say, "We must
have in the 1945 Act everything we had
in the 1930 Act." If they insist on that
they insist on the compulsory clause regarding teachers.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I should like
to intervene to say that, in the light of
this new conscience clause which has been
read by the Minister, I do not think there
is very much between the Ministry and
the Churches at the moment. At the

opinion on the wording of that conscience
clause without carefully examining it,
because, so far as my recollection goes,
this is the first time it has been brought
to my attention. There is no desire on
the part of anyone to compel anybody to
give religious instruction against his conscientious convictions. The point at
issue is, will the local authorities have
power to make an agreement, when
appointing a teacher who is willing to
give it, that if appointed that teacher will
actually give it?
Lieut.-Colonel HALL - THOMPSON :
The local authorities can do anything
when making an appointment, provided
they do not contravene Section 5 of the
1920 Act. Let me say it is a great pity
that the right hon. and reverend Gentleman did not insert this conscience clause
in his circular instead of the obsolete one
that was scrapped six months ago. If he
had done that I might not have taken up
such a strong attitude as I did on this
particular subject. Let me say this to
the right hon. Gentleman. He has great
influence with the Churches, and if they
will take this Clause as a basis for further
discussion I will be very willing to meet
them and discuss the matter with them.
In conclusion, may I thank this House for
the courtesy it has extended to me, and
say I hope we will all join together in
doing what is in the best interests of the
children of Ulster and of Ulster itself.

Prorogation of Parliament
THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 13th February, 1945.
The Senate met at five minutes after
Twelve of the Clock
noon,
Mr.
SPEAKER in the Chair.
PROROGATION OF PARLIAMENT.
Mr. SPEAKER: I have it in command
from His Grace the Governor to read
forthwith in this Chamber to the Senators
and Commons of Northern Ireland therein
assembled His Grace's Proclamation proroguing this present Session of Parliament.
Resolved: "That the Gentleman Usher
of the Black Rod be commanded to
inform the Commons that the Proclamation proroguing the present Session of
Parliament is to be read forthwith, and
inviting the Commons to attend in
order to hear the same."(Sir Joseph
Davison.)
The Commons, being at the Bar with
their Speaker, the Proclamation was read
to both Houses of Parliament by the
Speaker of the Senate (in pursuance of
His Grace's command), as followeth :-
BY HIS GRACE THE GOVERNOR OF
NORTHERN IRELAND
A PROCLAMATION
:-
For Proroguing the present Parliament
of Northern Ireland.
WHEREAS it is provided by section
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance of
certain enactments amending the said Act,
that the Governor of Northern Ireland
shall, in His Majesty's name, summon,
prorogue and dissolve the Parliament of
Northern Ireland:
AND WHEREAS it is desirable that
this present Parliament of Northern
Ireland should be prorogued to the
twentieth day of February, one thousand
nine hundred and forty-five:
NOW I, JAMES ALBERT EDWARD,
DUKE OF ABERCORN, KNIGHT OF
THE MOST NOBLE ORDER OF THE
GARTER, KNIGHT OF THE MOST
ILLUSTRIOUS ORDER OF SAINT
PATRICK, Governor of Northern Ireland, by this Proclamation do publish
and declare His Majesty's Pleasure that
the said Parliament be, as from the
reading of this Proclamation in the
Parliament of Northern Ireland, prorogued to Tuesday the twentieth day of
February, one thousand nine hundred
and forty-five.
AND I DO hereby further declare His
Majesty's Royal Will and Pleasure to be
that the said Parliament shall on the said
twentieth day of February, one thousand
nine hundred and forty-five, assemble and
be holden at Stormont for the despatch
of divers urgent and important affairs,
and the Senators and Members of the
House of Commons of Northern Ireland are hereby required to give their
attendance accordingly.
Given this Thirteenth day of February,
one thousand nine hundred and fortyfive.
(Signed by his own hand),
ABERCORN.
GOD SAVE THE KING.
End of the Seventh Session (opened

Tuesday, 20th February, 1945.
The Eighth Session of the Fifth Parliament of Northern Ireland appointed to
meet on 20th February, 1945, in the
Ninth year of the Reign of King George
VI., was opened by the Governor (His
Grace the Duke of Abercorn, K.G.,
K.P.) in person.
The House met in the Commons
Chamber at Five Minutes before Twelve
of the Clock noon, Mr. SPEAKER (the
Right Hon. Viscount Bangor, O.B.E.,
D.L.) in the Chair.
KING'S SPEECH.
His Grace the Governor being seated`
on the Throne, commanded the acting
Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod
(Brigadier-General H. G. Young, C.I.E.,
D.S.O.) to let the Commons know "It
is His Grace's pleasure that they attend
him immediately in this House.'
""
The Commons being at the Bar with
their Speaker, his Grace delivered the
following speech from the Throne:-
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
The people of Northern Ireland
encouraged by a year of glorious achievement in the struggle against the common
foe, view with increasing confidence the
approach of final victory and the establishment of a just and lasting peace.
They are proud to have made their own
distinctive contribution to the armed
might and military exploits of the
United Nations.
During the past few months, after an
early promise of bountiful crops, Our
farmers experienced the most unfavourable harvest weather with which they
have had to contend for many
years. Despite disheartening conditions,
farmers and their workers, with the help
of those recruited under the harvesting
labour schemes, have made strenuous
and untiring efforts to save the crops.
As a result, the proportion of crop
damaged or destroyed, while in some
cases considerable and bearing heavily
on individual farmers, represents
relatively small loss to the nation's food
supply.
a
For the coming season, farmers are
asked to maintain their efforts to increase food production, and my Government is confident that Northern Ireland
farmers will continue to contribute in
full measure to the needs of the United
Kingdom.
The Imperial Government has already
taken the first steps towards the
resumption of peace-time industrial
activity. I am glad to learn that our
textile machinery and linen industries
have participated in the concessions so
far made. I look forward hopefully to
an extension of the reconversion policy
both in these and other fields, although
the production of munitions of war
must continue to take precedence for
some time to come.
A
King's Speech
Papers Presented
THE SENATE
My Ministers accept, as one of their
primary aims the maintenance of the
highest possible level of employment in
Northern Ireland after the war, in conformity with the principles enunciated
by the Imperial Government in their
White Paper on Employment Policy.
The heavy incidence of unemployment
in the years following the last war
showed the need for a wider range of
manufactures, and the importance of
securing the establishment of new industries is fully appreciated by my
Government. Further legislation to
achieve this end will be brought forward
in due course.
One of the principal Measures which
will be laid before you is an Education
Bill designed to reform our whole
educational system as soon as conditions
permit. A comprehensive outline of the
new proposals has been published in a
White Paper and debated in both
Houses of Parliament. Before the
necessary legislation is introduced, my
Ministers will give careful consideration
to the valuable and constructive suggestions which have been made.
You will be invited to approve
proposals to make further provision
with respect to Government loans to
Local Authorities, and to authorise, at
moderate rates of interest, loans for
agricultural and fishery development.
It was not practicable to proceed in
the last Session with the Disabled
Persons (Employment) Bill, which is
designed to assist persons handicapped
by disablement to secure employment,
and the Measure will be brought forward for your consideration during the
forthcoming Session.
Legislation will also be introduced for
the establishment of Wages Councils
and for the regulation of remuneration
and conditions of employment in industry.
Measures will be submitted for your
approval, corresponding to those which
the Imperial Government propose to
promote, providing for an enlarged and
unified scheme of National Insurance,
a new scheme of Industrial Injury
Insurance and a system of family
allowances.
Members of the House of Commons.
The Estimates for the Public Services
will be laid before you in due course.
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
I am glad to learn that proposals for
the improvement of the public health
services are receiving detailed consideration, and that arrangements have been
made to deal with the emergency situation arising from the regrettable increase
in pulmonary tuberculosis during the
war years. It is a source of gratification
to me that miniature mass radiography
will shortly be made available for the
better detection of the disease in its
earlier phases.
It is the intention of my Government
to encourage the provision of large
numbers of new houses and the repair of
old houses, and to introduce such
further measures as may from time to
time be found necessary to achieve this
object.
Attention will be given to the
improvement of water supplies and
sewerage and to the general planning of
our towns and countryside, so that an
increasingly high standard of amenities
may be enjoyed by the whole community.
The Reinstatement in Civil Employment Act, which was recently passed at
Westminster, applies to Northern
Ireland and will be of benefit to men
who gave whole-time service during the
present war in the Armed Forces of the
Crown, as well as to women who gave
similar service in cognate organisations.
Included in the arrangements for the
resettlement of persons who have been
engaged on war service is a scheme for
assisting those whose education
apprenticeship has been interrupted,
and my Ministers are acting in collaboration with the Imperial Government
in the administration of this scheme in
Northern Ireland.
or
I pray that the blessing of Almighty
God may rest upon your deliberations.
Then his Grace was pleased to retire,
and the Commons withdrew.
House adjourned during pleasure.
House resumed in the Senate Chamber.

DEBATE ON THE ADDRESS.
SPEAKER having read
Mr.
King's Speech,
the
Major DOBBS (in morning dress): I
beg to move:
That a humble Address be presented
to His Grace the Governor of Northern
Ireland as followeth:
Your Grace,
We, His Majesty's most dutiful
and loyal subjects, the Commons of
Northern Ireland, in Parliament
assembled, beg leave to offer
humble thanks to your Grace for the
Speech which your Grace has addressed
to both Houses of Parliament.
our
In rising to move this humble Address
of thanks for the Gracious Speech, may I
be allowed to say how very much I
appreciate the very high compliment that
has been paid to me, and through me to
the county of Antrim, our premier
county. We all, I am sure, heartily
agree that it is only fitting that pride of
place in the Gracious Speech should be
given to the glorious achievements that
have taken place against the common
foe. The fact that the people of this
small but loyal Province have made a
distinctive contribution thereto
matter of great satisfaction to us all.
Looking back to the years 1940 and 1941,
it
incredible that, with the
stupendous successes of the then irresistible
German armies before them, our nation
never faltered, and its people never
seemed to realise, even then, that there
could be any result, in the near
distant future, but complete victory for
our efforts.
seems
is a
or
Looking back, we have been deeply
moved by the sufferings of the city of
Belfast.
But her ordeal has enabled her
to prove to her sister cities across the
water that she, like them, can take it.
We are indeed proud of Belfast and her
scars, which she will bear for many years,
are a great memorial to a great city.
Farmers will view with calm satisfaction
the fact that the very disheartening conditions under which they have been
working during the past few months are
openly recognised, as well as the fact that
the great majority of farmers have
worked strenuously in the national
interest, in many cases despite heavy
financial loss. I am afraid that
we farmers have a very serious year in
front of us. The water-logged state of
the land in parts of the country and the
consequently complete
of
absence
ploughing up to the present timebeen
though ploughing should have
nearly finished by now in a normal year--
means very late sowing, late ripening,
and consequently a precarious harvest in
the coming autumn for most parts of the
Province. However, I am sure farmers
here will still use every endeavour to
maintain food production, and it is at
any rate reassuring to them to realise
that the Minister of Agriculture
recognises that certain lands are better
employed in fattening cattle than in
trying to raise grain crops.
It is comforting to learn that the
textile industries are being gradually, by
the help of certain concessions, turned
over to peace-time work. The aim to
secure the maintenance of the highest
possible level of employment after the
war will be warmly welcomed in this
House. In this connection we are glad
to note that emphasis is placed on the importance of securing new industries for
the Province. This matter will, of
course, be in the hands of the Minister
of Commerce. May I offer our cordial
congratulations and felicitations to Sir
Roland Nugent, the new Minister.
(Hon. Members: Hear, hear.) I am sure
the appointment will be regarded as a
most excellent one by every Member on
both sides of this House, and will be
heartily endorsed throughout the country.
He certainly bears our heart-felt wishes
with him in his arduous duties. It would
be appropriate if I say now how glad.
to see our Prime Minister
returned fit and well after his severe
illness. He was an excellent Minister of
Commerce, yet it was universally felt that
we
were
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
as Prime Minister and Minister of Commerce he was over-taxing his strength.
I am sure we all here welcome the present
arrangement as best serving the national
interest.
To return to employment. I should
like to see in country districts the formation of small co-operative societies for the
making of furniture. I think our country
lads and lasses are sufficiently artistic and
handy to make a success of this industry.
Good, well designed, and well made
furniture would keep this potentially big
market from being swamped by mass produced inferior stuff. Incidentally, the
growing of box-wood and other timber
suitable for chair and furniture making
should be encouraged.
We come now to the Education Bill
foreshadowed in the Gracious Speech,
and note that it will be designed to reform
our whole educational system. This
House much appreciated the facility
given to it in the last Session to debate
the White Paper, and it notes with satisfaction that the Government intends to
give careful consideration to the suggestions then expressed. It will be a most
important Bill, and I am sure it is the
wish of all that adequate time will be
taken to make it into a great statesmanlike measure. The Leader of the House
envisaged the probability of full-time
schools on technical subjects being opened
for the 15 and 16 year olds. Many boys
of the present generation are mad about
wireless, motor cars and so on. The
teaching in a popular manner of the simple
A B C of electricity, metallurgy, power
transmission, et cetera, might well so
interest many of these lads that they
would become enthusiastic to try and
master these subjects, and they would
eventually give the new industries, and
the old industries also, a most important
source from which to obtain an intelligent
personnel for their factories. It is certain
that if we are to keep or better our place
in the manufacturing world-and it
applies to farming also we must
become a much more scientifically and
mechanically minded people. This all
goes to emphasise the great importance
of this Education Bill.
Hon. Members have heard with
pleasure the pronouncement that miniature mass radiography will shortly be
available for the detection of pulmonary
tuberculosis in its early stages. Everything that can be done to successfully
combat this dreadful disease is of the
greatest importance to the health of the
Province, and will have the warm support
of this House.
The provision of large numbers of new
houses will appeal to everyone here as a
matter of paramount importance, and I am
glad to note that the repair of old houses
will also be encouraged. I should like
to express the hope that the large number
of small farm cottages dotted up and
down the country will also be seriously
considered, and that encouragement in
some form or another will be shown to
enable them to be renovated and
larged so that the families of agricultural
labourers and others will not be
intensively crowded, often into one
sleeping room as at present.
en-
I am very glad to see that special
mention is made of the intention to increase the standard level of amenities
over the whole community. The enlightened planning of our towns and of
our countryside calls for the most serious
hard-headed thinking by those to whom
this work is entrusted. I hope careful and
studious consideration will be given to
the improvement, and I would also add
provision, of water supplies and sewerage,
and also of electricity and roads especially
in country districts. This will add
immensely to the health and enjoyment
of the people of the Province.
The Gracious Speech begins with an
acknowledgment of the distinctive contribution of Northern Ireland to the
armed might of the United Nations, and
it is very fitting that it should end with
the pronouncement that the Reinstatement in Civil Employment Act applies
also to Northern Ireland. We welcome
this pronouncement most heartily as safeguarding the positions of the men and
women from Northern Ireland who
served in the Armed Forces, and the
positions also of the women in similar
cognate organisations.
My Lord, I am sure that everyone
after giving careful consideration to the
Gracious Speech will realise that it is a
Speech breathing the spirit of hope to
the men, women and children of Northern Ireland. It is not a contentious
policy, but a policy with one great aimto bring happiness, health and prosperity
to this Province. (Hon. Members: Hear,
hear).
Debate on the Address

20 FEBRUARY 1945
Debate on the Address

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM (in
morning dress): I feel very pleased to be
again asked to second the Address in
reply to the King's Speech at the opening
of another session of this Parliament. I
cannot think that the honour is for me
personally; rather is it intended for those
loyal, reliable working men connected
with the Ulster Unionist Labour Association whom I have been privileged to represent for so many years in the House.
I am very thankful that I have been
blessed with good health to serve them so
long, faithfully and well and, I hope, to
their satisfaction. I would like to admit
here and now that the treatment and
assistance that I have received from every
Member of this representative assembly
has been all that anyone could expect or
desire.
I note that the Gracious Speech opens
with a very deserving reference to the
distinctive contribution our people have
made to the armed might and military
I am
exploits of the United Nations.
sorry that, occasionally, attempts, which
were allowed to go unchallenged when the
information might be valuable to the
enemy, were made to minimise the efforts
of our people in Northern Ireland; but
all impartial observers will admit that
our assistance to the war effort has been
very valuable. Ships, aeroplanes, tanks,
shells and a tremendous quantity of other
equipment, not known to the public, have
been produced here. In addition, the
number of young men and women who
have made the supreme sacrifice for King
and Country has been numerous, and
Members of this Parliament have suffered,
with others, in the terrible loss.
I
Even those of us who reside in the industrial centres can appreciate the
to the
kind, sympathetic reference
disheartening conditions the farmer experienced during the harvest season.
am sure it will encourage them to persevere and to endeavour to make good
their loss in the coming season. They
have played their part during the war
years exceedingly well, and I am sure
they will continue to do so. Meat, milk,
