only to us, but were provided for those
across the Channel who required it so
much.
I hope their valuable assistance
in wartime will not be forgotten when
victory is won and peace restored.
I am pleased to see that reference is
made in the Gracious Speech to the
resumption of peace-time industrial activity. If this is to be brought about
satisfactorily, and to avoid the terrible
experience of unemployment in the lean
years after the last war, it will require
the most extensive planning. There is a
larger proportion of our men and women
engaged on war work than ever before.
To switch them all over to peace-time
occupations in a reasonable time appears
to be impossible. I am sure the Government will take advantage of every opportunity to press the Imperial Government
to assist them to maintain the highest
level of employment. Assistance from
employers will be absolutely necessary, as
well as the whole-hearted support of
employees bent on giving a fair day's
work for a fair day's wage. Only in that
spirit will we be able to compete in the
open market for work which may very
be
scarce. If we are able to increase our
exports to the foreign market to pay for
the Beveridge benefits it will require all
the skill and labour we can muster in this
Province.
We all welcome the Bill to reform our
educational system. It is very necessary
that our boys and girls should be prepared
with a good education, equal to any in
the United Kingdom, to meet on equal
terms the competition for positions which
may be possible after the war.
controversy which has arisen over the
question of religious instruction will, I
hope, be settled to the satisfaction of all.
The
The Disabled Persons (Employment)
Bill will be very welcome; also the
establishment of wages councils to regulate wages and conditions of employment
in industry. Prevention is better than
cure. If we can prevent friction between
employers and workers it is a step in the
right direction.
I am glad to note that we are to continue to move, step by step, with Great

The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): This House has always
prided itself, and I think justifiably, on
the high level of speaking in its Debates,
and I feel that I cannot better fulfil the
first and most pleasant of my duties
to-day than by saying that the Mover
and Seconder of the Address have fully
maintained that level. Indeed, I think
I might say they have almost attained
the new high level which Senator Leslie
from time to time produces. In fact,
they have fulfilled the duty entrusted to
them with such thoroughness and competence that they have left me very little.
to say.
In the Gracious Speech, the first
reference was to the war situation which
has changed so marvellously for the
better in the past year. I am proud to
think that the people of Northern Ireland have taken a worthy part in
The Gracious
achieving this result.
Speech selects for special commendation,
the efforts made by our farmers and their
workers to maintain the contribution of
Ulster to the nation's food supply under
the most disheartening conditions and in
face of almost insuperable physical
obstacles. I feel sure the House will wish
to endorse this well-deserved tribute to
what has been a most remarkable effort.
The questions of education and housing,
important though they are, have so
recently been discussed in this House that
you will not expect me to make any
lengthy reference to them on this
occasion. It is sufficient to say that as
regards education, the debates which took
place both here and in another place, have
been so fruitful of suggestion that they
cannot but be of great assistance to the
Government in framing the Bill which
will be presented in the course of this
Session. In regard to housing, the Government look upon this problem as of the
very highest priority, and they will spare
no effort to press on with this task,
although it must be realised that difficulties in obtaining raw materials, and
it may be at a later stage the necessary
volume of skilled labour, must impose
some limitations on the results which may
be expected in the immediate future. The
provision of a high level of employment
is one of the questions which will inevitably occupy much of our time and
energies in the months which lie ahead of
It will be noted that in the Gracious
Speech mention is made of a very small
us.
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
commencement in the difficult
very
of the reconversion of industry.
process
The war has caused a complete industrial upheaval, and, while the war
continues, production policy is dictated
by the war's vicissitudes. The course of
the battle determines the demand of the
Service departments for this or that form
of armament, and fluctuations in the demand for various types of munitions inevitably cause dislocation in employment,
which it is not within the power of this
or any other Government to control. So
far as industries not engaged on war work
are concerned, the amount of employment
which these industries can give is
governed in the main by the amount of
raw materials available for their use, and
raw materials available for civilian production are in very short supply.
on
There is no prospect whatever of an
adequate supply of raw materials
generally, until the gigantic drain.
shipping for the prosecution of the war
has been stemmed and the U-boats cease
to function. It is well that the House
should face these facts, for no wishful
thinking will overcome them. We look
forward confidently to an improvement in
the position when the war with Germany
ends, but some restriction in supply must
be expected until Japan is beaten as well.
For the moment the expansion of civilian
production can provide only a very
meagre counterpoise to fluctuations in war
production.
The House must, however, view the unemployment figures with discernment. It
must be realised that there are a great
many more people on the register and a
great many more people in employment
than there were before the war. The
actual figure is, I think, something like
69,000. The size of the unemployment
figure is apt to be misleading. For
instance, the high wages obtainable in
munition factories have attracted into industry large numbers of women, especially
young women, who were not in industry
before. Contraction in certain forms of
output, or maybe more happily marriage,
returns them to their homes and for a
while they swell the unemployment total,
but that is not genuine unemployment.
None the less the Government does view
with anxiety the rise in unemployment. For
months past the difficulties of the labour
situation in Northern Ireland have been
represented to the British departments
concerned. Nor have these representations been without success, for in the
scaling down of war contracts the employment situation here is being accorded the
same consideration as is given to the
development areas in Britain. I
always think that the Government does
not get all the credit that it should in
these matters. If we prevent unemployment from starting nobody says "Well
done!" because nobody knows we have
done it. I would like the House to believe
that in quite a number of cases my predecessor has in fact prevented what
looked like an almost certain increase in
unemployment by his skill in negotiation.
across the water.
to post-war
To turn for a moment
policy? That can be simply and clearly
stated: First and foremost, to secure by
every means within our power the rehabilitation of our established industries
and their smooth transition to a peacetime footing with a restoration of our
export trade. To this end we shall utilise
to the utmost the close relations already
existing between the Ministry of Commerce and the Board of Trade. At home
we shall seek to win, as is essential, the
confidence, goodwill and collaboration of
both employers and employed.
a
Secondly, we shall seek to secure
greater diversification of industry and
consequently, of employment, by the
attraction to our area of new forms of
production. On this we are already working as the House will be aware from the
legislative proposals submitted to it, but
a great deal more is going on behind the
scenes, and the Government have good
reason to hope that, when the full story
comes to be told, it will be found that a
not unimportant extension will have been
made to the employment field.
Thirdly, realising as they do, that some
dislocation in the transition period is
inevitable, the Government will
endeavour to smooth out the curve of
employment first by pressing forward
energetically with their housing programme. The House will realise that there
is what I may call induced employment
arising from housing which is almost
as great if not greater than the direct
employment. It is estimated that for
every man to be employed in housing
another man is being employed somewhere as a result of that man's being
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
22 FEBRUARY 1945
employed. That is why housing is so particularly important. The second way is
by encouraging an extension of electrification, especially in the rural areas. There
again we may hope to get not merely the
direct employment occasioned but a considerable amount of direct employment
due to the cheapening of electricity and
the consequent extension of other forms of
industry. The third way is by having in
preparation and ready to be put into
force where required, in collaboration
where need be with local authorities, a
programme of public works designed to
take up the slack of employment when
and where it is most needed. I do not
want to put too much stress on public
works. Those who had experience of them
between the wars will know that they
are a very useful crutch but not much
more. Still a great deal can be done.
There is being held back at present a
considerable programme of public building which can be undertaken when the
time comes, such as schools, employment
exchanges, even police barracks quite a
large range of building. There is a very
large road programme which could be
undertaken with benefit, especially in bypassing one or two of our congested areas,
and there is also, of course, the big programme which must go ahead regardless of
whether there is any question of employment or not in connection with sewers and
water supply.
Smaller but still useful contributions to
employment can be found in connection
with forestry activities. In the main
these comprise fencing and draining, but
there will also be a certain number of
people permanently employed as forestry
goes on.

As stated in the Gracious Speech, the
Government intend to encourage in every
way possible the provision of new houses
and the repair of old houses. With that
object a Housing Act has been passed and
a Trust appointed. The Trust, which
will work in co-operation with the local
authorities, has received authority to
proceed immediately with the project for
the erection of 5,000 houses, and we shall
watch with close interest what progress is
made.
Apart from the housing programme,
the development of which cannot fail to
have a beneficial effect on the health of
the community, we are glad to know that
the Government policy is being directed
increasingly to the general improvement
of the health services. A new offensive,
long overdue, against tuberculosis has
begun. Accommodation at Musgrave
Park has been acquired for the emergency
treatment of general hospital patients and
tuberculosis cases. I know that some
critics have declared that the site is
unsuitable, but on the other hand the
Minister of Health has told us that the
medical advisers whom he consulted are
satisfied. If there is anything wrong, I
have no doubt that the Health Advisory
Council which has been set up will soon
express its opinion on the subject.
The British Government, some months
ago, issued a White Paper on Social
Security. When effect is given to the
new proposals in Great Britain, corresponding legislation will be introduced at
Stormont. Thus the principle of parity
in social services will be maintained.
It is also gratifying to know that the
Government is collaborating with the
Imperial authorities in the arrangements
for the reinstatement of ex-Service men
in civil employment. We cannot do too
much for those who are fighting our
battles.
As regards employment, we may rest
assured that the Ministry of Commerceof which Sir Roland Nugent is now the
enterprising head-will make every effort
to
secure new industries in order to
supplement the staple industries upon
which Belfast and Northern Ireland have
relied in the past. The diversification and
wider distribution of industry cannot
receive too much attention.
We often hear complaints that the
Government has no policy in this connection. The Prime Minister in the
clearest terms has outlined a policy which
should meet with strong approval. This
policy embodies rehabilitation of
established industries, with a smooth.
transition to a peace-time footing; greater
diversification of industry and,
and, consequently, of employment by attracting
new forms of industry to this area; a
smoothing-out of the curve of employment by pressing forward energetically
with the housing programme, encouraging
developments in electricity supply, and
instituting, often with local authorities,
a programme of public building and
public works, designed to take up the
slack of unemployment when and where
it is needed.
Surely that is a practical programme.
We do not want to experience the conditions which prevailed after the last Great
War. No doubt the unemployment
figures have lately risen, but the Prime
Minister has told us that in the course of
a few months we shall have turned the
corner and that between July, 1945, and
July, 1946, there will probably be an
expansion of employment which will
bring the number of persons employed up
to at least the numbers employed in
July, 1944.
If that comes about the position should
be very satisfactory. After all, it is work
that our people want, unemployment
relief and benefits are mere palliatives.
They stop the pain but the disease is
unchecked.
In the post-war years the
creation of employment rather than the
payment of relief, the prevention of sickness rather than the payment of benefits,
and the encouragement of thrift and
savings should be our guiding principle.
It is pleasing to see that a big London
firm has acquired premises in Lurgan for
the manufacture of lenses, spectacles,
frames and plastics. This should absorb
a good deal of local employment. Already
101 applications under the New Industries
Act have been received, and if these
applications are granted there should be
a considerable extension in the employment field. Regarding the rising generation the State must pay more and
attention to the physical, mental and

Reference is made to agriculture. We
sympathise with the farmers who have
been severely handicapped by unfavour
able weather, but despite the drawbacks
we recognise the magnificent work which.
they have accomplished. The development of agriculture and those industries
based on agriculture should have an
upward trend after the war. Agriculture,
with its attendant industries, must play
a more prominent part in the life of the
community.
Our economic life to a very great
extent centres round agriculture and its
products. Though for many years we
have produced and exported the raw
materials it is only within recent times
that we have begun to process that raw
material. Yet such processing gives
greater employment and adds to the
wealth of the country. By establishing
and extending such industries for the
development of these agricultural products we increase their range and
adaptability. We not only increase
wealth and employment but add a further
incentive to production.
Our European competitors are for the
moment down and out. They will be so
for some time after the war.
Such a
period will be our opportunity. If we
fail to make use of it we will have only
ourselves to blame. I see that attention
is to be given to the improvement of
water supplies and sewerage. The supply
of water and the provision of sewerage
facilities are essential public services, and
they are essential to healthy living under
modern conditions not only in cities and
towns but also in the country areas.
Many towns and villages in Northern
Ireland are badly in need of such
facilities.

Mr. LENNON: There are
matters in the Speech which are of very
considerable interest. May I take them
in the order in which they occur in the
Speech and deal first with the matter of
agriculture. It is interesting to note in
the Speech that the Government are
conversant, as well they might be, with
the efforts which the farmers in our community have made within the past years,
but it is still more interesting to note
that the Speech appears to make no provision whatever for the situation in which
farmers will find themselves when hostilities cease, and ordinary peace-time conditions prevail. One would have felt
infinitely more pleased had some indication been given that the Government has
in mind and has not forgotten the lesson
which it should have learned after the
last war in connection with agriculture.
One would welcome some sign that the
Government had made provision whereby
the farming community after this war
will not be plunged into that state of
almost impossible poverty into which they
were plunged after the last war.
We have had to depend, in a very large
measure, for the absolute necessities of
life on the farming community, and that,
so far as one can see, must of necessity
remain the condition for some short time
after the war. But what promise have
the farmers got that when this war is
over they will not be allowed to slip back
to where they were before the war started.
What promise have they got in this
Speech that any effort is going to be made
to see that the market will not be again
flooded by foreign imports of foodstuffs
and the ordinary necessities of life?
Everything seems to tend in the opposite
direction. We are told there will be a
lack of shipping space. That lack of
shipping space will be very short-lived.
Any person applying ordinary intelligence to the trend of affairs within recent
years must realise that there will be an
abundance of shipping, but not an
abundance of shipping over which any
person in Northern Ireland will have any
control.
One would have welcomed some sign
that efforts were being made by the
Government to secure for the farmers at
least a certainty of stabilisation for ten
years after the war. Any farmer who
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Lennon]
farms with any reasonable knowledge of
his work will tell you that it is necessary
to plan in advance the outlays which the
farmer has to make upon his farm, and
it is only reasonable that any person so
planning should at least have the promise
that his produce, when he has grown it,
will not be left to be dumped behind
ditches to rot, as it was a couple of years
preceeding the war. It has happened in
the Six Counties that the produce of the
farmers has been left to rot and has gone
back into the earth in the form of
manure. Most particularly has that been
so in the apple-growing district from
which I come. What promise has the
farmer that when this war is over conditions will not be the same as those under
which he struggled previously.
Coming to the question of unemployment, it is true, as the Leader of the
House said, that this Speech is a difficult
one to debate. It is particularly difficult
to debate by reason of the fact that the
Speech has of necessity to deal in general
terms with the subjects which it covers.
But although it is necessary for the
Speech to deal in general terms, it would
have been possible nevertheless to have
given some indication as to what is in
the mind of the Government with regard
to unemployment. The present situation
is that after almost six years of war there
are thousands of unemployed in the Six
Counties. That is a record of which any
Government should be justifiably
ashamed. What effort is being made at
the moment to get rid of that situation?
The. Leader of the House makes an effort
to explain it. He says, with a certain
show of reason, that, due to the war,
there has been inducted into the labour
market a large amount of female labour.
That is true, but what has become of the
male labour that is being inducted into
the Armed Forces ? To my mind, the
situation remains unchanged, and the
figures appear to indicate that those who
since the outbreak of war have become
available for work have been left without
that work.
What is the remedy which is being
applied by the Government? It would
appear that the remedy is economic conscription for labour in another country.
That is the present situation as I see it
with regard to the Northern Ireland
Government's effort to attempt some
palliation of the unemployment situation.
That is not a remedy. It is merely, as
one of the Members said to-day, a palliative, a palliative which gives no return
to the Six Counties. From here we
export that man power, and in return we
get nothing. It is true, perhaps, that
wages come back, but what is the price
that is paid for those wages? It is the disruption of family unity here. The father,
the husband, the bread-winner, the head
of the house, torn from the bosom of his
family, sent to work elsewhere, and his
family left behind. When that happens
family life disintegrates, and all this is
detrimental and harmful.
Then we
were told that the Government has in mind the necessity for providing after the war that unemployment
will not reach the state. it reached after
the last war. If the Government is
unable at the moment to control unemployment to such an extent that there
are thousands of unemployed in our midst,
one may be pardoned for asking how,
when the war is over, the Government
propose to see that there will not be
infinitely more thousands unemployed
than there are at present.
Then the Speech deals with industry,
and here is perhaps the most illogical
portion of the entire. Speech, because it
says that legislation will be introduced.
to the end that there shall be further
industries in Northern Ireland. I am
paraphrasing it, of course. Since when
has legislation found it possible to introduce industry, to commence new industries, and to rehabilitate old industries?
What virtue is there in legislation that
will produce industry as if by the wave
of a magic wand? It is interesting to
note that when this Speech was being
debated in another place, it was
tioned that it was hoped it would be
possible to induce. industrialists from
England to interest themselves in industry.
over here. Indeed, one might see in the
offing the preparation of an excuse for
failure to induce these industrialists from
England to come here.
men-
It was said that it was hoped that
the efforts of those who had not the
interests of the Six Counties at heart,
and who desired to see the Six Counties
reunited with Ireland and separated from
Great Britain, would not prevent these
industrialists from coming here. If that
is the excuse which is to be offered later,
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
22 FEBRUARY 1945
may I say at the outset that that excuse
is not a valid excuse. The situation to
be faced is a matter of reality. What is
that reality? That reality is that at the
moment in Great Britain industry has
been disrupted; industry is suffering from
shocking loss; the destruction of factories,
the disruption due to having to move
factories from one place to another; and
general industrial upheaval due to war
conditions. Does anyone in his senses
imagine that with that state of affairs in
England the English people will permit
industrialists to come here and start industries in the Six Counties?
If industries are to be started here
they must be started by the people. To
look hopefully across, the Irish Sea will
not in my opinion cause one single brick
to be laid in any factory in Northern
Ireland. The efforts must come from the
people in the Six Counties. They cannot
expect that the people in Great Britain
will permit their capital and their industry to leave Great Britain and come
here while there are in Great Britain
depressed areas such as existed before the
war.
Then one comes to deal with the question of education. One had hoped that,
having regard to the debate on the White
Paper, the views of such a substantial
portion of the community, at least onethird, might have had some bearing on
the Government's outlook on this question. In view of the fact that this Speech

and other Catholic Bishops, part of whose

made it clear what their attitude to the
White Paper was, one might have
thought that in this Speech some indication would have been given to show that
their views were such that the Government were prepared to consider them,
and that the ordinary honesty which one
expects to find in matters of this kind
would be brought to bear on this
question.
May I say here and now for all
Catholics in the Six Counties that this is
a matter of common justice. It has been
said that hand in hand with the spending
of public money goes the question of
public control, but the whole question
appears to have been approached from a
wrong point of view. The point is that
that public money does not belong to the
Government, nor does it belong to any
portion of the community, no matter how
large that portion may be. That public
money is contributed by the public
generally, and of that public the
Catholics form one-third. All that we
ask is that ordinary justice shall be
dealt out to us in connection with this
matter. The answer we get is, "You
have an equal opportunity with your
Protestant brothers." We have, but at
what price? At the price of the forswearing of our conscience. May I say
here and now that firmly and solidly
behind the declaration of His Eminence
the Cardinal and the other Bishops stand
the entire 33 per cent. Catholic population, and there will be no standing back
on this question.
We are asked to hand over our schools
completely in order that we may get the
grants to which we, as a section of the
community, are in all justice and
honesty entitled. We are asked to hand
over our schools, and we are told in the
same breath that once they are handed
over there must be a public act of
worship. A public act of worship is
something which is contrary to our faith.
We are therefore asked to hand over our
schools in toto in order that we may get
their total cost and their cost of maintenance. We are told in answer to that,
"You may keep your schools, but in
order to get larger grants than those
which you get at the moment you must
establish a four-and-two committee."
There again the Catholic viewpoint has
been made plain, and behind that viewpoint we stand solidly.
It was to be hoped that after the
debate which took place on the White
Paper it would have become clear that
the matter was being approached from the
wrong angle, that the function of the
State and the function of the Church in
education should have become clearly
apparent, that in this matter there is a
distinct line of division, a line of division
which allows of absolutely no intermingling of the different functions. It is
the function of the State to provide the
requisite books, the requisite buildings,
and the requisite necessities of education;
it is the function of the parent and the
Church to provide that education and
direct it. That is not the function of the
State. There appears to be some grave
confused thinking on that subject. Further,
one might have thought that having regard to the advice which was given to
the Government by the learned Attorney-
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Lennon]
General, the Government might have
seriously considered this position, but
instead of that the answer which is given,
and which appears clear from this Speech,
is that the Government have made up
their mind to proceed along the lines of
the White Paper.
In opening the debate on the White
Paper the right hon. and gallant Leader
of the House said at the conclusion of
his speech that it would be well for the
Members of this House to consider and to
think deeply before any of their rights
were bartered away. That is exactly
what we did. We thought deeply, and
we thought deeply of those rights. It
may be that perhaps the Leader of the
House might differ from us as to what
those rights are, but I should hope that
both he and the other Members of this
House who think differently from me will
at least do us the justice of saying that in
this matter we are sincere; that these
rights are matters of conscience; and that
in these matters of right and conscience
there is no room for anything only a
definite stand. We have made our
position clear.
Is it too much to expect
that when the Bill does, in fact, come
before this House, as is presaged by this.
Speech, some effort will be made to meet
our just demands?
Passing from that, one comes to the
question of health, and this is a very,
very serious problem. We are told in
the Speech that efforts are being made
for the supply of mechanical instruments
and other such equipment which will
make the detection of tuberculosis more
easy. Here again there appears to be
confusion of thought, because one would
have thought that, instead of its detection, steps would rather have been taken
to provide for its prevention. In this
connection, standing particularly in the
forefront is the question of accommodation for those who unfortunately suffer
from this disease, and it is a matter of
shame, or it should be, that the accommodation available for the treatment of
persons afflicted in this way in our midst
is so poor that at the moment it is a public
disgrace. Finally, with regard to this
Speech, one might say that it appears to
be an effort to whitewash and windowdress the position of the Government
here; that in it there does not appear
to be any genuine effort, nor is there.
any clear indication that in the future
there will be any genuine effort made to
meet the many blatant and perfectly
clear matters which require such obvious
remedies.
Mr. LESLIE: I trust that I may be
allowed to join with the other speakers
in expressing by own humble acknowledgment of, and satisfaction in, the two
admirable speeches which we heard at the
beginning of this debate on Tuesday last.
We have had the privilege of hearing my
hon. and gallant Friend on my left
(Major Dobbs) more than once in the
past, and I would express the hope that
we might hear him more often in the
future.. We heard him on Tuesday at
rather more length than is his custom,
and we heard him express very well
thought out ideas, if I might say so, in
clear language. We enjoyed the speech
very much, and we must re-echo the
words that fell from the right hon.
Gentleman the Leader of the House in
regard to the Motion for the Address in
Reply to the Gracious Speech. It was
seconded by another Friend of ours (Mr.
Joseph Cunningham) who has been with
us from the beginning, one of the very
few, and he gave us on Tuesday another
example of his usual common sense
method of dealing with things. My hon.
Friend goes directly to the point. He
does not waste words, and I do not think
that his words to this House are ever
wasted. We enjoyed his speech also, and
we expect to hear him again in the
future.
Passing to the Speech itself, the first
paragraph, of course, deals with the war,
and I need not say anything about it. It
goes on to agriculture, and I was pleased
to observe the tribute that it pays to
the great effort which the farmers made
during last season. I was also gratified
to notice that it did indicate appreciation
of the extraordinary difficulties which
unfortunately the weather cast on the
unfortunate agriculturist last year. I did
not observe any suggestion that there
would be any modification of what is called
the tillage quota during the ensuing
season. There is rather a pious hope
that the farmer will continue his efforts.
I do not say I am against tillage quotas
in general during war-time, but I do
think that the experience of last year
does show that a much better system
would be to apply your quota to indivi-
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
22 FEBRUARY 1945
dual farms and not make a broad and
general plan applicable throughout the
Province.
It is quite true that the great bulk of
our cereal product here is oats. It is true
that oats can stand more abuse than any
other form of cereal, and certainly oats
got that abuse during the last season. I
believe there is some hope that the
resulting loss is not so catastrophic as at
one time seemed possible. A good deal of
the corn that was brought to the threshing machine gave a greater yield than
many of us thought it would when we
saw its state as it left the fields. In so
far as the Speech deals with agriculture,
as has been pointed out by one speaker
already, it is limited to one year. There
is nothing definite about the future.
There is nothing in the Speech which goes
to offer some reason to expect that provision will be made in the future to protect the farmer from being left, when his
land is more or less exhausted perhaps by
the effort made during wartime, to face
the difficulties of rising wages and rising
costs. When I say rising wages, I am
not speaking entirely of the wages paid
to the labourer, but rising wages as they
affect the things required, like implements. A man might want to buy a new
plough, but the higher cost of wages for
plough-making is reflected in the price of
the finished articles and so on. The
plough is only an instance. What the
farmer does want is some form of
guarantee that something in the nature
of a minimum price will be maintained
constantly for some future years, and also
that control will not be continued in anything like its present form and in nothing
like its existing stringency.
Although it is easy enough to see what
happened last season and it is not difficult
to see what is required in the future, it
is not fair to suggest, as is sometimes
suggested outside, that during the period
between the two wars, governments have
done nothing for agriculture. It must be
remembered that the great Measure of
de-rating has been introduced, which has
special reference to and specially affects
agriculture. The de-rating system was
introduced between the two wars, and the
system is still going on. The effect is to
relieve the farmer of a burden which
during some of those pre-war years he
would certainly have found crushing. He
is relieved of it now, and for that he has
to thank successive governments.
Passing from agriculture, the next
point mentioned is employment. The
Speech naturally claims to have done
something for that, and, after all,
successive governments have introduced
into this Parliament more than one New
Industries Bill. They have done what
they could in the way of attracting new
industries, and for the moment I think
you must be content to leave it at that—
to leave what has been done in the past
as an indication of what to expect in the
future. As regards the actual unemployment figures, there has been a reduction
compared with pre-war years,
as the
Leader of the House has pointed out, but
I think it must not be forgotten that
amongst those
the unemployment
register in Northern Ireland there are
now and always have been and always will
be a high percentage of unemployable
persons. What that percentage is has
not, I think, ever been published, but it
must be known to some of the experts in
the Ministry. That is one thing that
must not be forgotten.
on
There is another thing which has been
suggested, and that is that instead of employing people at home the Government.
are sending them out of the country. I
do not believe that that is as true a way
of looking at it as this is that you are
sending people from the place where they
cannot get work to a place where they
can get work, and not only get work for
themselves but do work that is urgently
required. Therefore, the object of moving the unemployed people from Northern
Ireland to Britain is not so much to take
them off the unemployment register here
but to get them to work at work which is
national work and is of the utmost importance. I think that is a fair criticism
to apply to the unemployment difficulty.
Passing from that, the next thing is
this question of insurance. Some Members of this House will know I have never
been an advocate of compulsory insurance
at all. I am not going to dilate on that
at all to-day, but I should like to say
once more that before any new widespread
scheme of compulsory insurance comes in
steps ought to be taken to set clearly
before the people likely to be concerned,
in tabular form if you like, what they
are paying now for this or that form of
insurance, what they are getting for it,
or rather what they would get for it if
the misfortune of sickness or unemployment comes on; also what they may be
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
required to pay in the future, and what
they may expect to get in the future. I
think those sets of figures should be
clearly put before every elector in Northern Ireland, and we ought not to jump
at a scheme of compulsory widespread
insurance merely because it is adopted in
England.
Complete fiscal union with Great
Britain is a most desirable thing, and I
think the House will agree that I have
never said anything that would lead anyone to think that I held a contrary
opinion, but complete union with Great
Britain does not necessarily involve the
obligation of Northern Ireland having the
same system, if that system does not suit
Northern Ireland. Our conditions here are
not the same as they are across the water.
To begin with, this is primarily an agricultural country That is not so
the water. I believe that our population
as between the agriculturists and the industrialists is about fifty-fifty. The proportion, I think, across the water is very
much in favour of industry. There are
things which do not suit Northern Ireland but which do suit Great Britain,
and I feel it is our duty to be very careful to see that anything introduced here
is introduced because it suits us here; not
merely because it has already been done in
Great Britain.
across
The next subject mentioned in the
King's Speech is education. It has
already been said that educational matters
have been recently discussed in this
House on a White Paper. I am not going
to enter into any further discussion on the
subject to-day. I think I might express
the hope that if we are to get the Education Bill this year at all we will not get
it in the winter time.. I do not look
forward to dealing with an Education
Bill in a winter Session. If you cannot
get it through before the winter, then,
for the sake of the Senate of the day, I
hope it will be held over to next year so
that we can get at it when the days are
long, and not during the appalling darkness and cold and misery which overtook
us during the past few weeks.
There is one other remark one must
make what I may presume to call the
dissatisfied third. I think that the dissatisfied third should look back a bit, not
to so long ago, but to 1922 and 1923.
What help did that dissatisfied third offer
to the Government of that day when it
was inquiring into education? How many
of the dissatisfied third accepted the invitation to serve on the Lynn Committee?
That matter belongs to past history.
Some suggestions were made during the
discussion on the White Paper I have
referred to, and I am sure any suggestions
that were made will be considered. The
right hon. Gentleman has said so to-day.
There is one point which was made on
this matter the common act of worship
at the commencement of the school day.
I am quite certain that the form that that
act will take will be left to those responsible for the school. The Government will
not impose any particular form of worship.
I am quite certain about that.
I go
further, and I say I myself would refuse
to support any suggestion of that kind
whatever. I do not expect to be called
upon to stand up for that. I think I
will leave education for the moment.
Something has been said about tuberculosis. If there is one. thing that stands
out more than another it is that in the
combat against tuberculosis you must
depend more upon self help than upon
science. Science will tell you if you are
threatened with it, and it will tell you
that most of us during the day breathe
in a certain number of germs, but the
large majority of us are so constituted
inside that these germs are discarded.
When people get this disease they must
help themselves. There must be more
care taken in the family against sleeping
not only in the same room, but too often
in the same bed. That, to some extent,
can be controlled. There must be also
more willingness to submit to medical
examination, and there must be morethis is the most painful thing of all—
readiness to agree. to segregation. You
must face the fact that where tuberculosis has got into a family, that family
has in many cases got to separate, and
the patient has to go to a hospital, about
the saddest part of the whole horrible
thing. Until the people recognise that,
there is very little hope of stamping out
the ravages of this disease.
I have just spoken of self-help being
particularly applicable to the combat
against tuberculosis. I am going further,
I say that if you take the Gracious, Speech
all through you cannot expect success in
any one of the projects which are adumbrated in it unless you can rely on the
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
22 FEBRUARY 1945
help of the people of the Province. You
can get it in all sorts of ways. There is
no such word as "dishelp" but it would
be a good word to use. There is "dishelp"
on the part of people who stand out.
They not only cramp schemes but they
most carefully refrain from helping, and
if they can put an obstacle in the way
they do. If you cannot get rid of that
you will not get far with some of these
schemes. That is for the people themselves; it is not for the Government, and
it is not for Parliament.
Taking the Speech as a whole, and
looking back, I think we may be satisfied
that the Government intend to do everything they can to promote work and
houses and do everything they can to
avoid a combination of the two. You
want work and you want houses, but you
do not want workhouses. The Government in past years have done nothing to
forfeit the confidence of the people;
rather the contrary, they have done much
to pour concrete into the foundation of
that confidence. If difficult times are to
come we shall not be let down by our
leaders. I have the honour to support
the Motion for an Address.
Major BOYLE: I should like to pay
my tribute to the Mover and Seconder of
the Address. We all enjoyed their
speeches very much. In the coming year
we all hope that the war in Europe will
be brought to a victorious end, sooner
perhaps than some of us think. Out of a
population of 45 millions in Great Britain
25 millions are performing work of
national service, but in this Province the
proportion is not SO high. When
demobilisation starts it will be controlled
in such a way that there will be a filtering back of all sorts of labour. It is good
to learn that legislation is to be introduced to deal with that matter. It is
essential that every possible preparation
be made so that the great flow of labour
of all sorts, the re-creation of industry
and the re-establishment of ordinary life
will be dealt with in an orderly manner.
We have now in our Province a considerable amount of labour-I might call
it "caged labour"-in the shape of
prisoners of war. Would it be possible
for the Minister of Labour and the
Minister of Agriculture to consider the
drawing up of a scheme under which some
of this idle labour could be used on the
reclamation of land, drainage work, and
the cultivation of gardens in order to
provide foods for themselves? Drainage
and reclamation work is much in arrear
owing to the shortage of labour. The
farmers would be quite willing to pay
their share of the cost, and the prisoners
of war would earn some money and thus
be able to purchase cigarettes and other
things for themselves. That money would
replace the remittances which, I understand, are not now coming to them from
Germany owing to the dislocation of
matters in that country.
I am pleased to know that the Government will encourage private enterprise in
the building of new houses and the repairing of old ones. Outside the city of
Belfast the housing shortage has been
eased somewhat by the derequisitioning
of houses by the military authorities. If
all the facilities and advantages given to
local authorities and to the Housing Trust
were given to private individuals, and if
economic rents were restored, I believe
houses would be erected and old ones improved for letting and not for sale. If
that were done it would help the workers
to secure houses the rents of which they
could afford to pay.
The White Paper on education was
very fully debated in this House and
in another
the
place,
religious
question getting, I think, pride of
place. I think any difference of opinion
in regard to this matter has been or will
shortly be bridged. Bailey bridges have
been
useful to our armies on the
very
Continent and in Burma. Could
Corkey-Hall-Thompson bridge not span
the unfortunate gap there is in this
matter?
a
I should like to refer to one matter
mentioned in the White Paper-the
training and supply of teachers. We
require more teachers, and we must train
more each year. Our training colleges
are too small; they must be enlarged. I
suggest that these colleges should be more
closely affiliated with our University. It
is estimated that there will be an increased annual expenditure of 1 millions
during the next ten years, and money
spent on what I have suggested would be
well spent and would also help our
University. The colleges should become.
part and parcel of the University. There
should be a training school similar to the
medical and engineering schools. If that
were done it would attract the best boys

THE SENATE

and the best girls. The teaching they
would receive would give them a broader
outlook on life. In the not too distant
future I hope that all our principal and

The teaching profession should be made
more attractive in order to get the best
boys and girls to enter this noble profession and teach the youth of Ulster the
way it should go. I also envisage pupils
spending the whole day at school, where
they will be able to get their meals, play
their games and do their home work, returning home for supper and sleep. It is
hoped that Ulster folk will do justice to
this Province and so keep it on the map.

Address to His Grace the Governor.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT reported to
the House that a Privy Councillor of
Northern Ireland had waited on his
Grace with the Address from the Senate
(pursuant to the Order of 22nd February)
to which his Grace was pleased to return
the following answer:-
I have received with great satisfaction the expression of your thanks for
the Speech with which I opened the
present Session of Parliament.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have read The National
Trust Bill a Second time; they have
passed the Local Government (Finance)
Bill, the Representation of the People
Bill, and the Disabled Persons (Employment) Bill, to which they desire the concurrence of this House.

Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."

LOCAL GOVERNMENT (FINANCE)
BILL.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I beg to move, "That
this Bill be now read a Second time."
This Bill provides for payments from
the Exchequer to local authorities for the
purpose of helping to meet the cost of the
maintenance of the public mental
hospitals in Northern Ireland. Clause I
Representation of People Bill--

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Sir Roland Nugent]
of the Bill provides for an amendment of
the Local Government (Finance) Act,
(Northern Ireland), 1940, to raise from
£60,000 to £90,000, the maximum amount
of the annual grants which may be made
by the Ministry of Health and Local
Government.
Since the beginning of the war, the expenditure of the county authorities has
increased substantially and the yearly cost
of running the mental hospitals is now
about £50,000 greater than before the
war.
The Exchequer has for many years
contributed towards the cost, but the
greater part of the net expenditure falls
on local rates. The mental hospital
authorities have for a long time held the
view that the State should bear a higher
proportion of the cost of maintaining
those hospitals, and strong representations.
to this effect were made to the Ministry
of Home Affairs shortly before the Ministry of Health and Local Government
took over responsibility for the
tral supervision of the mental treatment
services. After carefully examining the
position, the Government is satisfied that
the claims made for an upward revision
of the Exchequer grant are well founded.
cen-

Moved, "That this Bill be now committed." (Sir Roland Nugent.)
On Question, Motion negatived.
Ordered, That the Bill be read the
Third time on Tuesday.
REPRESENTATION OF THE
PEOPLE BILL.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time."
principle of a yearly register, and to
institute instead the principle of an ad
hoc register for any by or general
election.
per-
It was found, however, in Great
Britain that this ad hoc register presented
certain difficulties, particularly in connection with the printing of the lists,
and it was decided that it would be preferable, at any rate for the first register
to be published since 1939, to publish a
a fixed date. The Governregister on
ment here felt that it should take advantage of this decision and obtain
mission to use the Westminster register
for Stormont elections on this occasion
and accordingly decided it also would
substitute a fixed register in 1945 for the
ad hoc register. Clauses 1 and 2 of the
Bill give effect to this decision under
which there will be published on the 7th
May a register which with suitable
markings will be available for Westminster and Stormont elections.
There
are three reasons why it is
necessary to introduce a second Representation of the People Bill so soon after the
similar Measure passed last December.
It will be remembered that by our Act of
1944 it was decided for the purpose of
war-time elections to depart from the
registration principles laid down in the
Representation of the People Act, 1918,
and to provide for a register based on
registration cards, and to fix a qualifying
date instead of a qualifying period. It
was also decided to do away with the
As regards Westminster elections, it
will be superseded by a further register
to be published in October which will
be both the Local Government and
Parliamentary register. For Northern
Ireland purposes, however, it has been
decided that the May register will remain
in force for any by-election initiated
after the 1st April, and for any general
election up to the 1st March, 1946.
Immediately a general election has taken
place the May register will cease to have
effect, and the principle of the ad hoc
register will be reverted to.
The second reason for the introduction
of this Measure was the decision of the
British Prime Minister to extend the
principle of postal voting to members of
the Forces practically all over the world.
Under previous legislation a member of
the Forces could vote only by proxy
unless he could give an address in the
United Kingdom. In view, however, of
the great numbers of voters among the
Services and war workers residing in
various theatres of war, it was felt that
it would be only equitable to give them
the chance of making a personal vote.
The Government here felt that it would
be only right that the same facilities
should be given to those voters to vote in
in Northern Ireland elections, and provisions to give effect to this decision are
contained in Part II of the measure.
Members will also observe that, following the English provisions, an opportun

Representation of People Bill- 15 MARCH 1945

Second Reading
The Bill is necessarily somewhat
intricate and complicated, and contains
frequent references to the previous Acts
which have been passed. It is realised
that this is undesirable generally, but as
many of the provisions will apply only to
the next election it was thought preferable to legislate by reference rather than
to make the Bill more cumbersome by reenacting the provisions in the more
permanent Measure.
Question proposed.
tunity will be given to each candidate to
send a copy of his election address to
each Service voter. Arrangements will
be made for this address to be despatched
by the returning officer with the ballot
paper. It is recognised, of course, that
some of these postal votes recorded by
members of the Services may go astray
and accordingly the proxy, whom each
absent voter is entitled to nominate,
will also be allowed to vote. Arrangements will then be made to secure that
the postal vote alone will be counted if
it is received in time, but that if it is
not received in time the vote of the
proxy will be counted. In order to give
as much time as possible to enable postal
votes to be received in time, the counting
of the votes will not commence until the
morning of the twentieth day after
polling day.
These are the reasons why it is necessary to introduce an amending Measure,
and as the register has to be published by
the 7th May the preliminary procedure,
namely, the publication of the lists, the
dates for the receiving and hearing of
claims and objections, and so forth, have
to be fixed by regulation in time to enable
the register to be published as has been
stated. The latest date on which the
lists can be published is the 31st March,
and it essential, therefore, that the Bill
should receive the Royal Assent before
that date, which is the reason why I ask
the House to take the Second Reading
to-day.
The opportunity has been taken at the
same time to introduce a provision in
regard to personation. In Part III of
the present Bill, Clause 14, a form of
words has been devised which should
make personation almost impossible, or at
any rate extremely difficult. According
to this provision any person applying for
a vote must produce to the presiding
officer a document which will identify him
with the person on the register for whom
he is applying to vote. This document of
identity has to be approved by the
Minister of Home Affairs, and will be
either an identity card or travel permit
or passport, provided of course that the
names and addresses on these documents
are the same as the names and addresses
on the register or if, owing to a change
of address or change of name, this is impossible, a document of identity which
can be obtained from any police barracks.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: After
listening attentively to the speech of the
Leader of the House, it is quite evident.
to me that a great deal of trouble has
been taken to enable our ex-Service men
to express their opinions on candidates
who may stand for the various constituencies. I think it is only fair and
proper that the men who are out fighting
should have an opportunity of selecting
their representatives for the Imperial and
Northern Ireland Parliaments. There is
nothing that pleases me more in this Bill
than the attempt which is being made
to prevent personation at elections. In
the past this has been a very troublesome
thing, more so, I think, in this part of
the United Kingdom than in any other
part. I myself have taken part in some
very keen trade union elections, and I
have always been prepared to stand or
fall by an honest poll. There is nothing
more disappointing than to find that
certain groups adopt dishonest means in
an endeavour to defeat one. If the provision regarding personation in this Bill
is successful in abolishing it I am sure it
will go a long way to have proper representation in the Imperial and well as in
the Northern Ireland Parliament. As I
have said, personation seems to be more
prevalent in this country than it is in
Great Britain, and I am particularly
pleased to see some attempt being made
to defeat those responsible for personation. For this reason I for one am only
too anxious to give my support to the
measure.
Major DOBBS: There is one point on
which I should like to ask the Leader of
It has always
the House a question.
been one of the principles of our voting
system that we should be able to vote,
and that how we voted was a matter of
secrecy and privacy. As I understand.
the provisions of this Bill, an elector may
vote himself and by proxy. If that is

Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
done, the papers have to be checked to
find out if he had voted and if his proxy
had voted. That being so, surely the
secrecy of the ballot no longer exists,
because the returning officer and the
officials checking those papers will know
exactly how the proxy has voted.
Mr. STEVENSON: I would like to
congratulate the Leader of the House on
the very lucid explanation he has given.
of this Bill. To me it was very obstrusely
worded, and until I got his explanation I
could not make head or tail of it. I
would like to ask if it is intended in the
post-war days to get back to the original
idea of making a fresh register for every
election. To me that seemed ideal, and
it is rather disappointing to find that we
have had to depart from it. With regard
to the Clause dealing with personation, I
think all parties will welcome this Clause.
Personation has been a crying shame and
disgrace. I would like to ask the Leader
of the House what punishment will fit this
crime. It seems to me that we are altering the conditions, because under the old
system, when a man attempted personation he was doing it on his own account
entirely. Now he will have to have an
identity card, and if he uses someone
else's, the owner of the identity card
will also be guilty of an offence. I should
have thought the Bill would have stated
more clearly the punishment that will be
meted out to a voter who attempts to vote.
with an identity card that is not his own.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: In reply to
Senator Major Dobbs, of course proxy
voting applies only to the Service voter,
and there will be identification marks
placed on the voting papers so that
secrecy will be observed. Both the postal
vote and the proxy vote will have a
similar identification mark, and the proxy
vote will go into a basket and will only be
taken out and used if the postal vote does
not arrive. I understand that this method
does effectively preserve the secrecy of the
ballot. In reply to Senator Stevenson, I
understand that it is intended to revert
to the system of having a register for each
election after the first election. On the
question of punishment for the wrongful
use of an identity card, there is already a
fairly heavy penalty for being in possession of a wrong identity card.

be now read a Second time," agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.

DISABLED PERSONS (EMPLOY-
MENT) BILL.
Order for Second Reading read.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): We are fortunate today in having with us my right hon.
Friend the Minister of Labour, who can
speak with much greater knowledge of
this Bill than I can. I therefore beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time."
Question proposed.
The MINISTER OF LABOUR (Mr.
Midgley): The Bill which is before this.
House embodies proposals which are well
in advance of anything previously attempted towards the removal of disablement as a stigma from those in our
community who are so unfortunate as to
be handicapped by impairment of mind
or body. Many such persons, if left
without active sympathy and assistance,
are in danger of being condemned to
become objects of charity, dependent
solely on adventitious help.
The provisions of the Bill are intended
to secure that the disabled, whatever the
cause of their disablement and no matter
how sustained-whether on the battlefield
or by enemy action elsewhere or in industry or through some mischance which
may perhaps date from birth, will, if
they are substantially handicapped in
getting or keeping employment, be
assisted to take their places as independent, self-supporting citizens, an asset to
the country. Courses of vocational training will be provided for disabled persons
over 16 years of age who need such
assistance to enable them to take up work.
Courses of industrial rehabilitation may
also be provided for those who need
preparatory conditioning by means of
physical training, exercise and occupation
before embarking on full-time employment or on a course of vocational training.
These courses will be free and maintenance
allowances and travelling expenses will be
paid.
It will be necessary, not only for carrying into effect various provisions of the
measure, but in order that the Ministry
of Labour may have full information

Disabled Persons Bill-
Second Reading
22 MARCH 1945
as to the number within the scope of the
scheme and the types of their disabilities
in relation to potential employment, that
a register of disabled persons should be
set up and maintained. Registration will
be voluntary but will carry with it certain
advantages. Conditions of eligibility for
registration are imposed, but if any doubt
as to eligibility arises the Ministry will
have the help of an advisory committee
in reaching a decision.
Employers with twenty or more workers
will be required to employ a quota of
registered disabled persons. This quota
will be a percentage of the total employees;
special quotas may be fixed for special
classes of trades or employers. Employers
will not be required to dismiss existing
workers to make room for disabled persons, but they may not engage a nondisabled worker without permission if by
doing so they leave themselves below their
quota, nor may they dismiss without good
cause a disabled person where the effect
would be the same. There will, however,
be no duress on an employer to engage a
disabled person who is unsuitable for any
particular vacancy which may arise.
Power is sought under the Bill to earmark certain classes of employment as a
close preserve for the disabled. What is
contemplated is a relatively small number
of occupations of minor importance and
requiring comparatively little skill. This
wli be done after consultation with the
appropriate organisations of employers
and workers. When the type of employhas been so designated, vacancies in that
occupation may not be filled otherwise.
than by a registered disabled person except with the permission of the Ministry.
Here again employers will not be required.
to dismiss existing workers nor to engage
unsuitable labour. The Bill also makes
provision for the more severely disabled
who, despite all that can be done towards
their rehabilitation, are unable to work
under ordinary conditions. Schemes for
sheltered employment or for the provision
of work on their own account for these
persons may be started or assisted by the
Ministry and will include training with
maintenance allowances as well as employment in their own homes.
The problem of the rehabilitation and
resettlement of the disabled is a difficult
one, and to some may seem almost insoluble. Experience during a major war
has demonstrated, however, that many of
these difficulties can be overcome. It has
been found, for instance, that by a simple
alteration to or adaptation of a particular
machine disabled persons could produce
as much as fit persons and were no more
difficult to train, because they had the incentive to overcome their handicap and
so attain to the enjoyment of a full life
on equal terms with their more fortunate
fellow workers.
It will be essential for the success of
the measure now before the House that
the interest and good will of employers
which have been aroused, and the research
which has begun, should be maintained
and expended, and should be linked with
the advice and assistance of the advisory
commitees which will be set up under the
Bill. This combination, together with
the resources of medical and surgical
science, backed by the assistance of the
State and inspired by a determination on
the part of all concerned to overcome
obstacles will, I am confident, ensure the
success of this comprehensive
humanitarian social experiment.
will not only benefit an important section
of our less fortunate fellow citizens, but
will also ensure that they can take their
due share in the work of rebuilding the
post-war world, a task demanding the
fullest use of all our available resources.
and
This
Major PERCEVAL - MAXWELL :
The main principle of this Bill is so very
wise and so very just that I feel great
reluctance in having to criticise some of
the sub-principles that are contained in it.
The first point I wish to make is that
fixing a standard may give the impression
to employers that provided they fulfil the
requirements of the legislation, they have
done all they need. I think that impression should not be allowed to go forward. I think we owe, whether we are
employers or not, such a deep debt of
gratitude to those who have fought for us
in this war that we should make it a rule,
quite irrespective of any legislation and
any standard that may be laid down
generally, to employ to the utmost of our
capacity, and so far as our businesses will
allow, consistent with efficiency, the
largest number of disabled men and
women that it is possible for us to employ.
In fixing the standards which are set, I
think the Ministry will bear in mind that
they must always be mimimum standards.
You cannot handicap trade too much or
you will put a far greater number of
people out of employment.
Disabled Persons Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Major Perceval-Maxwell]
I am sorry that this Bill is in many
respects impracticable. A Bill of this
sort is very likely to be impracticable, and
whether it will become practicable at a
later date is a matter which, of course, we
in this House are unable to say; but, at
the present moment, it is impracticable,
because there is not a trained staff to train
the disabled men. There is no psychological organisation in Northern Ireland
and no means by which people can be
trained. There is no psychological school
at Queen's University. A great many of
the men disabled in this war will be
affected by shell shock and other things
with which we, who served in the last war,
are familiar, and from which some of us
suffered. Without training some psychological experts it will be quite impossible
to improve the state of mind of many of
the men and women who will come back
after this war.
I am afraid also that this Bill-it is
really inevitable in a Bill of this naturewill be very difficult for the ordinary
business man to understand. I read
through one Clause six times, and I am
not sure that I understand it yet. I am
not a business man by trade, but I have
some experience of reading through Acts
of Parliament. I will refer to the specific
Clause in a moment or two.
There are
a great many other Clauses in the Bill
which will tire the minds of busy business
men before they discover what they mean.
""
the benefit of the Act all persons not
ordinarily resident," it will be for the
courts to decide and not the Ministry what
is the meaning of the words "ordinarily
resident."
I should like to ask the Minister to
explain how this Bill will act in the case
of boys or girls who leave this country at
the age of 18, boys who go to sea or become soldiers or airmen. They will
ordinarily be resident anywhere that their
ship or their regiment takes them. They
will not have acquired any residential
qualification in Northern Ireland at all.
It may be that their parents are in Northern Ireland, but that in itself will not
make them ordinarily resident here.
should like the right hon. Gentleman to
inform us how those people come within
the Bill.
I
Then, again, there are questions affecting us very much, such as where people
who may have been ordinarily resident in
England or one of the Dominions come
over here for treatment and are in a
hospital. Will it be possible for those
people, who are not in any sense
ordinarily resident in Northern Ireland,
to have their treatment completed and to
have their rehabilitation completed in
Northern Ireland? I do not think it will
under this Bill. I think that the Services
will rightly and deeply resent
resent any
differentiation between one soldier and
another or one airman and another. If a
man is good enough to offer his life, to
give his limbs, his health, or shorten his
life for Northern Ireland and the rest of
the Empire, then, I think, he should be
treated in all respects as if he were one
of our own. Unless the Government give
me a clear undertaking that no person
who has served in this war or who has
served in a ship of the Merchant Navy
will be excluded from rehabilitation I
cannot support this Clause.

(c)-I should mention here that there are
benefits of rehabilitation as well as employment a person will be excluded
from the benefits of the Bill if not
ordinarily resident in Northern Ireland.

under this Bill; in fact, so wide that one
might almost say that Parliament has had
very little say as to how the Bill is to be
later

the Regulations are laid on the Table. I
do not think it is possible for Ministeries
to regulate as to the meaning of a word
in an Act of Parliament. If we say in
this Parliament that we exclude from
As regards employment the point is
slightly different. It is obviously right
that we should give first priority of
employment to those of our wounded
comrades who are

Clause I am afraid that those people will
