"
The next point I wish to make is in
regard to a Clause which, I admit, I
cannot understand. That is Clause 10,
sub-clause (6). I will not say anything
about that Clause because I do not understand it. I hope the right hon. Gentleman or some Member of the Government
will explain it. It is one of the most
involved Clauses that I have ever read in
of Parliament. Perhaps I
any Act
ought to say that there is one thing
that makes it particularly hard to understand the words, "his quota . . . is likely
to represent. I can understand a Member of Parliament representing his constituency, the written word, the spoken
word, but I cannot see that a quota can
represent anything but a quota. I think
the word "represent" must be a misprint.
I cannot understand what it means.
Another fact which makes it difficult to
understand is that it seems to refer to a
possible accretion of labour during the
12 months after the determination is
made. What that accretion is due to I
do not know, unless it is casual labour.
I hope the right hon. Gentleman will
explain it.
The last point I have to make is in
connection with Clause 19, sub-clause (4).
I think this is one of the most unjust
Clauses I have seen in an Act of Parliament. I cannot imagine anyone putting
it in. It is a most amazing Clause.
will read it:
I
Where an offence under this Act has
been committed by a body corporate,
every person who at the time of the
commission of the offence was a director
or officer of the body corporate, or was
purporting to act in any such
capacity-
Junior clerks might be involved, and
certainly the welfare officer would be involvedshall be deemed to be guilty of that
offence, unless he proves that the contravention was committed without his
consent or connivance, and that he
exercised all such diligence to prevent
the commission of the offence as he
ought to have exercised, having regard
to the nature of his functions in that
capacity and to all the circumstances.
I think the House will agree that that
is a most involved Clause. Looking at it
from the purely legal point of view, it
would appear to me that no director or
officer has any locus standi to defend himself until his company has been convicted
of an offence. By that time it would be
no good defending himself, because he
would have become guilty of the offence.
It is possible for a person to brief counsel
to sit and listen so that his interests may
be protected. In some big companies I
can see everyone down to the junior submanager engaging counsel, and that will
mean a vast array of counsel, who cannot
open their mouths, sitting in court. They
will be there simply to see that no evidence
is given which is likely to damage their
clients. That will be a good thing for
many people connected with the law.
There will be many briefs, a great deal of
money will be spent, but nothing will
come of it.
It cannot be fair for a director-he is
appointed for his qualifications to be
made responsible for every managerial defect which may take place, and responsible
in such a way that he cannot defend himself. I think when the Government have
had time to reflect on that Clause they
will realise that nobody who has any
knowledge of law or business can support
it. I think it reasonable that the Government impose a penalty on directors who are
negligent in their duties, but this Clause
penalises everybody down to the junior
clerk if he has any official or overseeing
duties to perform. If he holds the keys.
of the safe he will be responsible.
Certainly the welfare officer will be responsible and he cannot have any power
to effect managerial acts which should
have been committed, but which have not
been committed.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I want to
express to the House and to the Minister
of Labour in particular, my hearty
approval of the general principles of this
Bill. I think it is a Bill which the
Minister of Labour might well feel proud
Disabled Persons Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
He
to have the privilege of introducing and
of guiding through our Parliament.
was right in saying that it marks a new
standard of advance in our social legislation. It carries the principle of social
security for those who are unfortunate,
underprivileged, or disabled. It goes
further than our legislation has ever gone
before.
I should like, before I go any further,
to say that I am in agreement with the
last speaker with regard to a number of
the points which he mentioned. In giving
my hearty approval to the principles of
the Bill, I do not mean to controvert all
of the points which he made, but I think,
perhaps, that, so to speak, he failed to
see the wood for the trees. In principle
this is a fine measure, and it gives expression to what I might call the corporate
goodwill of the community in that it
enables the community as a whole to bear
a little bit more of the burden of life and
the struggle for existence which
individuals in the community cannot very
well bear themselves.
some
The principle of our social legislation
being closely co-ordinated with similar
legislation in Great Britain appeals to
me, and I feel that the more closely we
are linked up with our bigger and
stronger partner in social legislation such
as this, and, indeed, in all our whole industrial system, the better it will be for
us who happen to be the weaker partner
in this combination. I could casily travel
outside the ambit of the Bill on this subject, but perhaps I may be allowed to say
that the work of the Minister of Labour
would be a good deal easier, and he could
look forward with a good deal more hope,
if we were industrially united with Great
Britain in a far more intimate way than
we are at present, and if we could make
arrangements for our industrial system
similar to the arrangements made in this
and other Bills for our social services.
There we go step by step and I think that
policy is very wise.
The last speaker suggested it might not
be possible under this Bill for a disabled
person who had begun the process of
rehabilitation in Great Britain to have it
continued and completed in Northern
Ireland. I hope that is not so, and I
hope it will be possible to have our legislation so closely integrated that, just as
the benefits of industrial insurance are
mutual as between Great Britain and
Northern Ireland, so the benefits of this
Bill will also be mutual. I think that
the last speaker made a very good point
there. I should like to say also that a
feature of the Bill which appeals to me
very much is that the Government will
take power to establish industries, if
necessary, on a non-profit basis for the
benefit of those people who are unfortunate enough to be disabled. Such
legislation fifty years ago or even twenty
years ago would have been thought outrageous, but our sensitiveness to the needs
of certain people in the community has
become much keener, and we tend now
to think of the welfare of the workers as
the ultimate aim of our whole social life
and industrial activity rather than mere
profits.
Of course the country as a whole must
be able to pay its way, but I do not think
there is anything foreshadowed in this
Bill which is likely to bring either our
own area here into bankruptcy or to bring
bankruptcy to our great partner Great
Britain. Ruskin said that the only wealth
is life, and that is a principle which seems
to me to be given expression to in this
Bill. Just one word more on the general
principles as far as approval of the Bill
is concerned. I think that in this Bill
we have a very interesting and significant
example of the sort of planning and
central control which we will experience,
I hope, after this war for the benefit of
the country as a whole. Here is an effort
to help unfortunate people. It is an
effort which very few individual firms
could afford to make on their own without
legislation. There are very few firms who
could undertake to employ a considerable
number of handicapped and disabled
people, and have a chance of survival
in competition with other firms who have
no such charitable. principles, but when
legislation is adopted, planning and controlling industry to an extent that these
people will find an entrance into all forms
of industry, then industrialists are quite
willing and happy and gratified to be
able to do that sort of thing because they
are all on an equal footing. May I say
that nobody wants control simply for its
own sake, and yet after the war I feel
we will have no security either for the
individual or the community without a
considerable amount of control.
I should like further just to mention in
a word a couple of the criticisms which
Disabled Persons Bill-
Second Reading
22 MARCH 1945
the last speaker made, and in which I
think there is a good deal of substance.
Possibly this is a matter of wording rather
than of any difference in principle. In
this much debated Clause 19 I observed
that the Minister of Labour survived a
running attack of a very tenacious character in another place. I am inclined to
agree with the criticisms of the barristers
on this point that sub-clause (2), page 20,
of the Bill is very ambiguous. It does
seem to put no limit to the time within
which an action in law may be commenced
against an individual or a firm. I think
that phrase "whichever period comes last"
is very awkward, obscure, opaque and
ambiguous. If I am free to say so, the
Attorney-General in another place said.
that what was meant was that no action
could be commenced after 12 months' time.;
that is what he seemed to say. If that is so
the Clause should be worded and could be
worded far more clearly by adding the
phrase, "No such proceedings shall be
commenced after a period of 12 months."
That is what the Clause seems to mean,
and that is what the Attorney-General
said it meant, but that is not what it
obviously says as it stands here.
Sub-clause (4), I think, also should be
differently worded. After all, no one in
this country should be deemed guilty before the process of the law has established
his guilt, and probably what is meant is
simply that all members of this corporate
body, this firm or whatever it may be,
would be chargeable with the offence and
not treated as guilty of the offence until
they had been proved to be so. If a corporate body were proved to be guilty of
an offence then it might be reasonable to
charge individual
individual members with the
offence. There again the wording of the
Clause does not seem to be ideal, and I
would be glad if the Minister of Labour
and the Government would make it more
explicit so that there would be no doubt
as to the meaning of it. Certainly it has.
always been a principle in our law that
no man should be regarded or treated as
guilty until proved to be so by the
regular process of the law in our courts.
With these observations I should like to
conclude by saying that I think it is a
very fine Bill, and I hope the principle
of it will become the law of our land.

Mr. LESLIE: The Bill before us is
undoubtedly a Bill which at first sight is
very attractive. At second sight one perhaps is not surprised at the remarks made
by my hon. and gallant Friend opposite to
the effect that the Bill is likely to be
somewhat impracticable, and I think that
that impracticability remains even when
you reflect on the last sub-clause of the
last Clause in the Bill. Even that will
not save the impracticability altogether.
However, as I say, the Bill at first sight
is distinctly attractive, because the provisions for the vocational training and rehabilitation of those who have suffered in
the war appeal to all of us. I do not
think there is anybody who is not anxious
that everything that can be done for those
who suffered in the war should be done at
the
expense of the State, and even at the
cost of some individual sacrifice by persons. That, I think, appeals to all of us.
The Bill goes a great deal further than
that.
It brings in not only those who
have suffered during the war but everyone
else who has suffered injury. It brings
in those who are afflicted with congenital
deformity. I think the right hon.
Gentleman agrees that the Bill is in no
sense limited to those who have suffered
either in this war or in the last war. The
two classes of sufferers seem to me to be
in very different categories, but of that
I shall speak presently. It is probably
one of the most outstanding features of
the Bill to bring in everybody who has
suffered. It brings in everyone who has
has suffered in one way or another
whether by disease or infirmity.
We start with a definition of the expression "disabled." "Disabled" in the
ordinary sense, I think, usually means an
able person who has been made unable or
disabled; in fact, a man has to be able
before a man can be "dissed," if I may
put it that way. This Bill gives a
different form of expression altogether.
"Disabled" means a man who never was
able, the congenitally deformed man
again. That is rather curious, and I do
not observe that it is altogether carried out.
through the Bill. If anyone would take
the trouble to look at sub-clause (2) of
Disabled Persons Bill-
Second Reading
22 MARCH 1945
Clause 3, I think he will see what I mean.
In the sub-clause to which I refer we are
told that:
The facilities which may be provided
under this Section shall consist of
facilities whereby such persons may
under adequate medical supervision and
under circumstances conducive to the
restoration of physical fitness obtain
physical training, exercise, and occupation conducive to the restoration
thereof...
That seems to suggest that the effect of
that Clause is limited to those who had
fitness once and want to have it restored.
It does not seem to touch people who never
had any fitness at all, that is, the people.
who never were able. That is a matter
which, I think, deserves attention at a
later date.
As I have just said, the Bill is not.
limited to cases arising out of the war.
We have got to remember that the
sufferers from this war, as from the last
war, will some day or other pass from us.
The sufferings due to the last war and to
the present war will some day come to an
end. There is no reason to suppose that
ordinary accidents, ordinary disease,
ordinary congenital deformity, or extraordinary deformity, will come to an end
in the future. Although you must have
in the course of time a cessation of sufferings arising from the war you will always.
have, as it were, a new supply of recruits
coming forward to take advantage of this
Bill, and in time the latter, whom I call
the civilian casualties, will outnumber the
military casualties. That position will
arise some day or other by degrees, and
ultimately one will outpace the other.
The Bill draws a distinction between
sufferers, however they became sufferers,
who are registered and those who are not
registered, so that as things stand apparently a man whether he served in this
war or otherwise if he is not registered
will not get such advantages as are obtainable under the Bill by those who are
registered. I do not know that that is
intentional, but I presume the idea is to
have the path of the registered made
easy. The reason is that a very large
number of men who suffered during the
last war are in employment at the present
time, and employers very often took them
on simply for the reason that they had
suffered in the war. Very often the employer himself had suffered in the war
and there are quite a large number of
people in that category who are employed
for that reason.
As the Bill stands I imagine it will be
necessary to get registered otherwise the
employer, if this quota business goes on,
may be in a way unfairly handicapped.
In fact, the whole Bill bristles with cases
where it seems the intention is that in
order that a man may not be handicapped'
by disablement it is proposed to handicap
the employer. Any hindrance to the
employer will react unfavourably on the
trade of the country, and it is on the
success of the trade of the country that
our future prosperity depends. We must
be extremely careful that in order to help
A we do not injure B. That has to be
borne in mind throughout.
Not only is there this distinction between the registered and unregistered, but
I am afraid that effect of the Bill is
ultimately to differentiate against the fit
in favour of the unfit. There are elaborate
provisions in the Bill, the right hon.
Gentleman referred to some of them in his
speech. I think he pointed out that
there is no provision requiring a fit man
to be discharged by anybody in order to
let in an unfit man. I think he was
careful to lay stress on the fact, which is
apparent in the Bill, that it is only when
vacancies arise. the claim of an unfit man
comes in, and when I say an unfit man I
really mean a registered man.
In nine
cases out of ten the registered man will
be just as fit as the man who has not
been registered. There is no suggestion
of sending a man with one leg to drive a
motor-car. The man who is unfit to-day
will, we hope, become fit to-morrow, and,
therefore, that matter to some extent
will disappear.
The Bill merits the fullest possible discussion. Taken all round it is sketchy,
and in many cases it is even hypothetical;
it is not only sketchy in some of the main
points, but some of those main points are
nebulous. First of all there is the combination of the war casualty with the
civilian casualty. It is the intention to
make them all alike in the future, to
blend them, but I have tried to point out
in the future at all events that cannot be
SO. The next main point is the coercion
of the employer of whatever class. It is
proposed that an employer of a certain
number of men-it says 20 or such lesser
number as the Ministry specifies-I think

hope to do is to put forward the fundamental reasons why, first of all, this Bill
was brought forward in Great Britain,
why it became law, and why it must become law here. The first speaker this
afternoon said that the Bill was largely
impracticable. I think it was Senator
Perceval-Maxwell who said that.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I

Mr. MIDGLEY: He said it was
impracticable. because of the lack of staff
and equipment to deal with all classes of
disablement arising from the war. Later
on in the debate another hon. Senator
said that the Bill was impracticable because it was going to lead to many people
being appointed, and because it would
lead to increased bureaucracy, increased
officials and all the rest of it.
In meeting these points as they occur
to me I should like to say that it seems
to me very unreasonable on the part of
the public in Northern Ireland or elsewhere to be clamouring for additional services and then, having achieved or secured
them, to be protesting against the
Government's right to appoint people to
work or administer those services.
Major PERCEVAL - MAXWELL:
The right hon. Gentleman took me up
wrongly.
Mr. MIDGLEY: I am not dealing
with the hon. Senator specifically.
I
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
said there were no training facilities, not
that there were not enough civil servants.
Mr. MIDGLEY: I combined your
observation with another one and I am
dealing with the other one first. I will
deal with yours now. I do not regard the
Bill as impracticable because of lack of
staff. Already there is a great deal being
done in regard to rehabilitation and fixing
up buildings. We have the advantage
from an advisory point of view of all the
Services established here during the war.
We have got institutional experience.
We are at the present time taking steps,
to see to it, so far as it is possible to do it,
that registration shall be prepared, and
we will at the appropriate time be able to
set up training schools or training centres
appropriate to the size of our task in this
particular time. At the present time we
are taking advantage of the interim
scheme with Great Britain, and any of
our people who come back and desire
training can get it by specialists at the
present time.
I do not think we need
anticipate any fear at all in regard to lack
of equipment or lack of staff who will
have the necessary skill to deal with this
problem.
I should like hon. Senators to keep in
mind that as far as British statesmen are
concerned this problem is not entirely one
for the Services. It covers both civilian
disabled and war disabled personnel. That
was the intention from the beginning. The
intention in this Bill was to deal with a
class of humanity never dealt with before,
and to deal with them adequately so as to
try as far as possible to get them a useful
place in society. Looking at the problem
from that point of view, the Government
decided that it must be done in its
entirety. Indeed had there been no problem of civilian disabled at all it would
not have been necessary to have a Bill
here in Northern Ireland. Our Services'
problem would have been covered in the
British Act, but by virtue of the fact
that we have a civilian disabled problem,
which is comparable, if only in a smaller
way, with that in Great Britain, we are
charged with the task of looking after
both our civilian and our Service disabled.
The British Government delegated that
responsibility to us to do on their behalf
and we are doing what they are doing on
the other side. That is combined within
the scope of the one Bill, a scheme for
the rehabilitation and treatment of the
civilian and war disabled. Hence the
justification for the Bill.
A great deal has been said here this
afternoon about Clause 7. Dealing with

to

and commerce throughout Northern Ireland, and we hope to have committees
like this to advise us from time to time
and to give us the benefit of their experience in research in regard to the
administration of the legislation.
We will have bodies like this consisting
of influential citizens to give us their help
and they, along with representative
bodies of employers and work people, will
be able to collaborate to the end that we
may solve fresh problems as the assimilation of disabled persons into industry
along the lines of common-sense without
any disability being inflicted upon the
industry itself. This will be universal.
It will not apply to one employer and not
to another. All industry in the United
Kingdom will be carried on on that basis,
and I do not think that anybody will be
groaning under any disability or load imposed upon him.
Senator McAllister referred to the
central committees and local committees
to be set up, and dealt with the question
of independent chairmen. I think he
made some special plea that lawyers
should be considered.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Not a plea.
Mr. MIDGLEY: I thought you did;
I am sorry. We have not yet reached the

be, but we certainly want to make these
committees as representative as we can
possibly make them right throughout the
country, and to get the various interests
concerned without allocating to any
particular section a prescriptive right to
put on conditions. We want them to be
as impartial as possible, and it is precisely because of that that we reject the
principle advocated by the right hon.
Senator and other speakers here this
Disabled Persons Bill-
Second Reading
22 MARCH 1945
afternoon. We want to keep the final
word with regard to decisions inside the
House of Commons and not have it left
to any tribunal outside.
If we concede the right of appeal from
the decisions of these district or advisory
committees and the central council subject
to a final appeal to some tribunal outside,
the matter is ended and is no longer
within the scope of Parliamentary control. We want to see to it that all the
decisions in regard to policy in connection
with this matter shall be answerable inside the House of Commons, that the
Minister shall be held responsible, and
that the democratically elected Members
of the community shall be compelled to
come to the House of Commons and raise
the matter there if they desire to do so,
and thereby use public opinion as a safeguard for the preservation of all the
rights that are implied in this legislation.
I think some misunderstanding exists
in the minds of Members with regard to
how quotas are going to operate. Power
is sought in the Bill to have quotas for
particular classes of industry and business, and to have several quotas. For
example, a quota that might be good in
one business might not be good in another.
There might be heavy industries where it
would be ridiculous to impose any heavy
quota, and there might be light industries
of not the same commercial importance in
which it might be possible to have a higher
quota. A large firm conducting several
kinds of businesses could have several
quotas, and this legislation gives us power
to have different quotas for different sections of trade and industry. It is also
proposed that the disabled shall be classified in different ways. There are disabled
who can be built up by a combination of
surgical skill and industrial training and
made useful members of the wage-earning
community, and who will be able to produce just as effectively as an able-bodied
person. There will be other cases where
people obviously will not be able to take
part in any competitive industry, and in
such cases we will have special designations, and there will be special assistance
given to non-profit making bodies who
desire to use these people, when trained,
to the utmost possible extent.
I am cooperating with certain people in the community who are putting forward proposals
with reference to organisations of this
kind assimilating that particular type of
disabled person.
Senator Leslie stressed several times the
element of compulsion, about which I
should like to say something. He declared
that we ought not to have any compulsion
and that we ought to do this by persuasion. Since the last war I have had a
long and painful experience of trying to
use persuasion. From the end of the last
war up to the beginning of the present
war I served on many committees, and I
know that powers of persuasion and moral
obligation did not accomplish very much.
I know that some. people are more responsive than others, but if you object to
legislation because it is compulsory you
must object to all legislation, because I
cannot see that legislation is anything
else but compulsion in one form or
another. The laws relating to public
health, pensions, and our social security
schemes are all compulsory on the whole
community. Between the two wars we
had never less than 800 disabled ex-
Service men signing our unemployment
register in Belfast. That was despite the
fact that we were making all the appeals
we possibly could through such organisations as the King's National Roll of
Honour. In the light of that unhappy
experience we feel that it would be in the
interests of the disabled ex-Service men
and the community if we applied some
compulsion in the form of the words
embodied in this Bill.
Senator Lennon took great exception
to our claim to disqualify a person who
is an habitual bad character. I do not
want to attempt to define an habitual
bad character, because I am very conscious of the fact that
There is so much good in the worst
of us,
And so much bad in the best of us,
That it hardly becomes any of us
To talk about the rest of us.
I do know there are standards laid
down by law as to the definition of an
habitual bad character, but apart from
that we do know that throughout the
community there are people who by their
general attitude to the law give an indication as to whether or not they are bad
characters. I could give you many
instances of this. This principle has been
accepted throughout the United Kingdom
and no organisation of ex-Service men
has objected to it, and I have met them
all. I have met representatives of all the
fighting Services, and I have discussed
with them the whole problem from every
Disabled Persons Bill

THE SENATE
[Mr. Midgley]
angle and every point of view. They want
to safeguard the administration of this
Bill in every way possible, and they want
ex-Service men protected from the funds
and institutions which try to trade upon
these things rather than to conform to
good standards and citizenship.
as
Mr. LESLIE: The right hon. Gentleman says the military organisations are
anxious to have these restrictions
regards an habitual bad character, but

Mr. MIDGLEY: Until I came here

Mr. LENNON: The question I asked

the definition of an habitual bad

was
Mr. MIDGLEY: When I
beginning to serve my time in Belfast
there used to be a man well-known
throughout the community who went to
jail once a month. He generally came out
one day, had a good booze, and went
back to jail next morning. For the purposes of this Act I would regard him as
an habitual bad character. I suggest that
people have proclivities of many kinds
which indicate whether they are or are
not fit and proper persons to take advantage of registration under this scheme. If
the general principle is accepted the
definition can be left to those who are
going to administer the Act, subject also
to this fact, that we will have in every
area an advisory committee composed of
representative people in the area who will
be able to go into the question of whether
or not an injustice has been done. If
they think an injustice has been done they
can make representations to the Ministry,
and although there is no tribunal set up
to act as a final authority, in the majority
of cases, I can prove from experience,
where recommendations have been made
by advisory committees to the Ministry,
in ninety-nine cases out of a hundred
these recommendations have bee.n
accepted. In the one case out of the
hundred where they have not been
accepted, the person, through his representative, can raise the matter in the
House, of Commons.
In conclusion, I should like to appeal
to the Members of this House to accept
this Bill in the fine humanitarian spirit
in which it has been accepted throughout
Great Britain. It is already law there..
Some of our disabled people are receiving
services now through the medium of this
humanitarian code. We want to delay
no longer. We want to fall into line
with the United Kingdom. We want to
have reciprocal arrangements made. We
want to have the machinery prepared to
the fullest possible extent to safeguard
the rights of the people returning from
the services. If we are determined to do
that, to give this measure of justice and
at the same time assist disabled civilians
in a way they have never been assisted
before, then the right thing to do is to
pass this Bill and see that it is carried
into effect with all convenient speed, so
that we may not lag behind our brothers
and sisters on the other side.
Mr. LESLIE: I think probably the
House is prepared to let this Bill go to
Committee. I think that is the general
opinion, and therefore I do not think I
need take up time in dealing with the
right hon. Gentleman's replies on the
Bill. I must say I think he was rather
anxious to use. the sovereignty of Parliament to the destruction of the rule of
law. In that, of course, he cannot expect
us to help him. Apart from that, we
can deal with these matters in Committee..
If the House will permit me, I will withdraw the Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in
Clause 3, sub-clause (2), to leave out the
word "restoration" in line 7, and to insert instead thereof the word "attainment." I mentioned this small matter
on the Second Reading. The point is, of
course, that you cannot restore to a man
a fitness which he never had. The difficulty arises from the introduction of the
word "deformity," which I presume is
intended to mean congenital deformity.
It seems to me that the word "restoration"
is incorrect; that it is necessary to
substitute another word, and the best
word I can find is "attainment.' I cannot say "acquisition," because that word
is always used in connection with real
property and not abstract matters. Therefore the best word I can find is "attainment." A poor chap who is congenitally
deformed never was fit, and you cannot
restore a fitness he never had. The only
suggestion I can make is for the insertion
of the word "attainment.'
Question proposed, "That the word
' restoration be left out."

improvement in the form of the wording.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in
Clause 7, sub-clause (1), to leave out of
paragraph (d) the words "habitual bad,"
and to insert instead thereof the words,
"the fact that a person is not reputed to be
of good.' This troublesome question of
the habitual bad character evoked considerable criticism in this House when the
Disabled Persons Bill---
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
matter was last before us. Of course the
words "habitual bad character" appear
in the English Act, but I am afraid I am
bound to say that I do not think they fill
the bill. They will not do. I do not
think the Ministry will take upon itself
to decide whether anybody is an habitual
bad character or not. It is not a question for the Ministry. This question, if
it arises at all, is one for a court, and I
do not think the Ministry has any intention of deciding that for itself. I
think the idea of the Ministry is probably
to refer it to the district advisory committee, for which possibility provision is
made in the Bill, but I do not think the
district advisory committee will go so far
as to say that A or B is an habitual bad
character. They would not do it. Nor
do I think they ought, because the same
question arises, that that is a question for
a court of law and not for anybody else.
However, you can get round it, and I
think the words I have suggested as an
alternative are good enough to meet the
point. The object of inserting this provision is that a possible employer shall
have some security that a man he has to
employ is of decent character. I cannot
agree that the question of an habitual
bad character is for anybody but a court,
but the Amendment I suggest the fact
that a person is not reputed to be of good
character is a question of fact as to
which your district advisory committee
can advise the Ministry, and there is no
necessity for an appeal. Accordingly I
beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the words.
'habitual bad' be left out.'
Mr. MIDGLEY: We appreciate the
difficulty of defining the words "habitual
bad character," and as it has beggared
the imagination and capacity of this.
honourable House you will appreciate
how hard it was for people like ourselves
to define it on the spur of the moment.
Having regard to the exposition given by
the right hon. Senator we think, on
balance, there is more security for the
person concerned and more security for
the community in general in our form of
words than in the form of words suggested by him. He suggests we should
leave out the words "habitual bad" and
insert instead thereof the words, "the
fact that a person is not reputed to be of
good." Do you know anything more
cowardly and reprehensible in the community than this question of repute?
How many people in the community do
we know of sound character and integrity whose repute is not as good as it
ought to be in accordance with their
characters? Even hon. Members of this
House are not immune from that. I know
many Members who are of good character,
but if you were to judge them upon their
repute, based for example upon newspaper reports, they would stand condemned from many points of view.
Under this Bill we desire to help people
who are in difficulty. If we allow those
words to go that a person is not reputed
to be of good character-a man who might
be a good candidate for registration and
training could, on account of this definition, be precluded from obtaining under
this Bill that assistance which we desire
to give him. The last hon. Senator who
spoke stated that there are bodies in
existence constituted by law who are the
only bodies thoroughly qualified to say
what,the words "habitual bad" mean.
We will come across cases which have been
so designated by the courts, and such
people may make application to us for
registration and assistance under this
Bill. We will not have the slightest difficulty in dealing with the cases SO
designated, but when it comes to a question of repute a wide avenue is opened up,
and we think, having regard to all the
implications, that there is less danger to
the community in our form of words than
in the form of words suggested by the
right hon. Senator.
I should say without any dogmatism
that we are not married to those words.
If this House thinks the words suggested are better and safeguard the community we are prepared to accept them,
but we have examined the thing up and
down, and I say quite sincerely that we
think our words are better than those
suggested.

Mr. MIDGLEY: Twenty is there indicated because it is taken to be the number

likely to be involved and the nature of
the occupation likely to follow, 20 had
become fixed and, therefore, largely
rendered the spirit of the Act inoperative.

number than twenty employees as a

able to administer it under conditions less
favourable would, in my opinion, eventually create a situation which would be
very difficult and not easy to defend. For
these reasons I ask the House to reject
the Amendment.

Mr. LESLIE: This is an attempt to
try to get something out of the Government which will enable the House to pass
the Bill with an easier mind. Can the
right hon. Gentleman give us any idea
as to the number of Service men likely to
be involved? Approximately how many
people, principally these civilian casualties, are to stand in front of the Service
men? Again and again I have pressed
the case of the Service man against whom
this Bill is specially directed. Unless he
is injured he gets no benefit from the Bill,
and a convenient method of raising that
question is to put down a possible percentage as a maximum, with a proviso
that if the Ministry want to alter that
percentage they can come back to Parliament, a thing the Ministry hate doing.
This dislike of the Ministry to come before
Parliament for authority is most remarkable, but I devised this Amendment to
give them this opportunity of asking
Parliament to revise its decision at a
later date when the Ministry has certain
knowledge. For the moment I am suggesting that no standard or special percentage shall exceed five. I hope five is
too high, but possibly the right hon.
Gentleman will be able to tell us.
We do
not want to leave bureaucracy entirely
unfettered. The Committee has shown
rather a tendency to be unduly tolerant
of bureaucracy, which the country does.
not like, but that is their business, not
mine. I suggest that the percentage shall
not exceed five.

to have the power to adjust the percentage
to meet various circumstances as they
arise. In his Amendment, the right hon.
and learned Member suggests that until
Parliament otherwise determines, no
standard or special percentage shall be
higher than five. It is too dogmatic to
lay down such a percentage without
regard to circumstances. In the special
circumstances of some industries, a percentage of five might be too high, while
in other industries it might be higher
than five. We cannot tell at the moment.
Therefore, we seek power to have a
standard percentage, and if necessary a
special percentage either
either greater
smaller than the standard percentage. I
do not think I need elaborate that point
any further. Because. of the arduous and
laborious nature of some industries it
might be possible to have only a small
percentage, but in other industries, in
which the work was easier and more. in
accordance with the abilities of the disabled persons, a higher percentage could
be laid down. For this reason we think
it is impossible. to dogmatise.

This Clause was discussed at great
length on the Second Reading. I understand it did not have an easy passage in
another place. Consequently I thought
I had better look into it a little. I am
suggesting that this House should now
take the course which was taken under the
Agricultural Wages Bill in 1939. In
1939, it will be remembered-on the 2nd
of November of that year to be accurate
a Clause exactly similar to that now before us was presented to this House. This
House would not have it. We first of all
took some objection to the twelve months'
business, but on it being represented by
the right hon. Gentleman, the then
Leader of this House, that there was some
justification for twelve months in that
case, and that he had spoken to the

his name is on the back of the Bill before

which I now suggest. That is why I put
those words on the paper. The origin of
those words will be found in Section 5,
sub-section (6) of the Agricultural Wages
Act. I think that this Committee will
probably regard them as entirely unobjectionable.
The Clause before us is, for many
reasons, not desirable. There is the
usual objection that if you wait twelve
months your summary jurisdiction ceases
to be summary, and there is the more
serious objection that if you delay prosecution too long you prejudice a man's
defence. Having those and other matters
in view, this House, during the present
Parliament-it was in November, 1939—
adopted the form of words which I have
now put on the Paper. I beg to move
the Amendment.

be left out.

DEATH OF PRESIDENT
ROOSEVELT.
Message to American Senate.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): This is the first occasion
on which the House has met since we
received the sad news of President
Roosevelt's death. I feel sure that the
House will wish to give expression to the
grief which is felt by all classes in
Northern Ireland, and to send a message
of sympathy to the Senate of the United
States in the grievous loss which has
fallen upon them and upon their
country.
Disabled Persons Bill

President Roosevelt was a great
American, a great citizen of the world,
and a wise and fearless leader of his
people, both in peace and war. He was
the tried and trusted friend of this
country, but of all his great qualities, I
think the world will remember best the
indomitable courage which enabled him
to triumph over ill-health, fatigue, and
difficulty, and to work incessantly for the
welfare of the world. He died working
for America, for us, and for mankind,
and now, in the last words of another great
American, "he has crossed over the river
and rests in the shade of the trees."
I beg to move:
That we, the Senate of Northern
Ireland, moved by feelings of heartfelt
sorrow at the death of President
Franklin D. Roosevelt,, tender to the
Senate of the United States of America
our profound sympathy with them in
the grievous loss which they and their
country, in common with all the United
Nations, have sustained.
On Question, Motion agreed to nemine
dissentiente.
Resolved, "That the Speaker do transmit the said Resolution to the Senate of
the United States of America through
the appropriate channels."-(Sir Roland
Nugent.)
Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.

DISABLED PERSONS (EMPLOY-
MENT) BILL.
Order for receiving Report of Amendments read.

The said Amendments were accordingly

CLAUSE 19 (Provisions as to offences).
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :
At the end of Clause 19 insert the
following new sub-clause :
Disabled Persons Bill-
Report
THE SENATE
(6) Any person aggrieved by a conviction or order made by a court of
summary jurisdiction on determining
any information or complaint under
this Act may appeal in the manner prescribed by the Summary Jurisdiction
Acts (Northern Ireland.)
Mr. LESLIE: The Amendment on the
Paper is, perhaps, if I may say so, not
altogether without its humorous side. I
imagine the House is acquainted with the
terms of Section 24 of our Summary
Jurisdiction Act of 1935, and the House
will remember the terms of that Section.
The House must also have taken notice of
the fact that our procedure in such cases
as this has not been uniform. Sometimes our Acts of Parliament passed since
1935 included such words as those on the
Paper, and sometimes they did not. I
am not going to presume to advise the
House as to the effect of either the insertion or omission of these words. All I
am going to say is that sometimes they
are put in and sometimes they are not,
and I do not think, that being so, that
this is a case where they should be
omitted. The House will, no doubt, remember that such words do occur in the
Workshops and Factories Act of 1938.
The House will also remember, no
doubt, that they do not occur in the
Agriculture Wages Act, 1939, but the
House may be aware that there is a Bill
now proceeding in another place dealing
with medicines, pharmacy, and things of
that kind in which these words do occur.
The question the House really has to
decide is whether these words ought to go
in or whether they ought not. As things
are, in the uncertain state that exists at
present, my own opinion is that they
ought in this case to go in, but I repeat
that I do not presume to suggest to the
House as to whether their insertion or
omission does or does not make any difference in this case.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I have taken
advice on the difficult or, to use the right
hon. and learned Senator's own words,
amusing point raised, and the advice I
have received is that the Amendment
should be resisted, not that we object in
any way whatever to the sense of the
Amendment, but we believe that Section
21 (1) of the Summary Jurisdiction and
Criminal Justice Act, 1935, does in fact
cover any cases which will arise under this
Bill. Consequently it is desirable not to
put these words into this Bill lest any
doubt should be raised in the case of those
Acts which do not include these words.
Some of my predecessors have included.
such words in Acts of Parliament, and
although consistency is the hobgoblin of
little minds, I do think this is a matter in
which in the future we should strive to
be more consistent. I am advised that
in these cases the practice to which we
should try to adhere in future is not to
make this reference when a portmanteau
Act, as it were, already covers the point.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Might I say that
I am inclined to accept the view expressed
by the Leader of the House. My recollection is that the Summary Jurisdiction
Act of 1925 contained powers that were
very comprehensive. I think the wording is to the effect that there is an appeal
from any Order made, and I think it is
not desirable to state in every individual
Bill coming before this House that there
is a right of appeal in the manner prescribed by the Summary Jurisdiction
Acts, when the comprehensive power of
appeal under those Acts still applies to
all Orders made by a court of summary
jurisdiction. For this reason I am inclined to support the attitude taken by
the Leader of the House on this point.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: If it is
any assistance to the House I have
the Section here. It is Section 24
(1) of the Summary Jurisdiction and
Criminal Justice Act, 1935. It reads as
follows: :-
Notwithstanding anything in any
previous enactment, an appeal shall lie
to a court of quarter sessions against
an order of a court of summary jurisdiction, in cases of a civil nature by either
party, whether he is the complainant or
defendant, and in other cases by any
party against whom an order is made
for payment of any penal or other sum,
or for any term of imprisonment, or for
the estreating of any recognisance to a
greater amount than twenty shillings.
That is about as wide as it can be.
Mr. LESLIE: I think that really the
House is prepared to take note of the
suggestion in the right hon. Gentleman's
speech that the House will not do it again.
That is, we shall not again see a Bill containing a sub-clause such as I have asked
Disabled Persons Bill
Adjournment
17 APRIL 1945
the House to insert on this occasion.
Under the circumstances I do not see any
object in insisting upon an Amendment
which, in the opinion of the Government,
would not do anything. I do not express
any opinion on that point, but I ask leave
to withdraw the Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
the Third time."-(Sir Roland Nugent.)
Resolved, "That this Bill do now pass."

claims
whether he can state how many
for contribution in respect of land
drainage have been received by the
Ministry of Agriculture during the
past twelve months, and. how many
payments in respect of these claims are
in arrear.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
ask this question because it has come to
my knowledge that there is considerable
delay in settling the claims for land
drainage. This may be due to financial
reasons, though it may be due to carelessness on the part of the owners of land
themselves, or to lack of labour, such as
The fact
inspectors, in the Ministry.

remains that if land is not drained it
cannot produce good crops. We are now
being asked to grow more and more
food for starving Europe, and the way
to do that is to make the land more productive rather than to plough up more
land. That is the reason why I am putting this question, so that if there is any
difficulty in getting these claims put
through the matter can receive public
attention, and anything that is wrong
can be put right.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Under the
Land Improvement Scheme for the year
ended 31st March, 1945, the total
number of applications approved by the
Ministry of Agriculture was 19,456, and
the total number of claims paid during
the same year 18,992.
Of the applications received, the work
in 10,818 cases involved land drainage,
and in many cases other work as well.
In the 464 cases outstanding, the, work
has either not yet been completed or the
Questions to Minister

completed work is awaiting inspection,
but the hon. Senator will appreciate that
a large proportion of these cases also

Mr. MAGUIRE: It strikes me that
the Ministry are very slow in giving
grants for the clearing of shrubbery and
rough grazing. They have. turned down
a good many of the claims, and I think
that is very unwise at the present time,
considering that we are likely to have
an increase in unemployment. It would
be much better to give these grants for
the clearance of shrubbery and rough
grazing, because it would enable farmers
to keep on their workers during the
winter period.
Cottages in Rural Areas.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Maguire:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state what steps the
Government proposes to take to ensure
that in future local authorities in rural
areas will build cottages for farm and
other workers which are attractive. in
appearance and provide more adequate
accommodation than the cottages
erected in the last twenty years under
the Labourers Acts, and are sited convenient to such public services as water,
sewerage, light and transport, upon
land which can be cultivated for
gardens, etc.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I have been
approached by a good many people resident in country districts to draw the
attention of the Government to this
matter. The cottages built under the
Labourers Acts during the past twenty
years have a very drab and poor
appearance. In my opinion the great
majority of them resemble the road block
huts that have been erected during the
war. They look very shabby, and they
lack sanitary and other conveniences,
such as running water, while the rooms
are very small. I feel that the Government should lay down some minimum
standard of appearance and sanitary
convenience, including water supply. I
would also suggest that architects should
be, encouraged to go in for attractive
designs in keeping with the countryside.
The accommodation in these cottages
should be ample for families of average
size. In those erected up to the present
the rooms are very small, and in some
cases four people have to live in a small
room.
I think this is a very opportune
time for modern sanitary conveniences
to be supplied to these cottages. Most
of the cottages built in the early
stages possess only dry closets, and the
water has to be carried from wells
and pumps which are in many cases a long
distance from the cottages. I hope the
Minister will take this matter up with the
rural district councils and see if the
design and amenities of these cottages
cannot be improved to harmonise with the
beauty of the countryside, as well as to
enable the tenants to have a comfortable
home.
Mr. MCALLISTER: It occurs to me
that in all the discussions we have had in
this House on the various Housing Bills
proper attention was not paid to the
interests of those residing in country
districts. The discussions have to a great
extent centred round the city of Belfast
and the urban districts. I think those of
us who are conversant with the type of
house the labourers are called
upon to
inhabit in Northern Ireland are compelled, as public representatives, to stress
the necessity of improving this very
unsatisfactory state of affairs. As regards
the administration of the Labourers Acts
by local councils, there is a tremendous
differentiation in various localities. Some
counties made an honest effort to provide
accommodation for the workers in the
rural districts, but other counties made
no effort at all to put this valuable legislation into operation.
In the case of most of the houses erected
under the Labourers Acts the sites were
most unsuitable. The worst land in the
area was very often chosen. They were
insanitary sites, most unattractive, and
the situation made access to the houses
very difficult. I would impress upon the
Government the necessity of providing an
adequate number of reasonably good
houses rather than to go in for quality at
the present time. As I might call myself
Questions to Minister
Message from the Commons
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
a representative of rural areas I would
strongly urge the Government not to
concentrate their attention too much on
the cities and urban areas, but to direct
their attention more to the rural areas
where a great many of the cottages have
only earthen floors and leaky thatched
roofs. I think that in the past their
interests have not received from Parliament that priority attention they should
have received.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The Minister
of Health has every intention of doing
his best to ensure that rural cottages
which may be erected under the Housing
Acts shall be designed with elevations
which will be in harmony with and not
spoil the beauty of our countryside. With
this in mind, his Department has already
instructed
authorities that all
housing schemes, both in urban and rural
areas, are to be prepared by architects,
and it is hoped that the rural authorities
concerned will see to it that they secure
the most competent architectural services
available.
local
old
As regards the accommodation to be
provided in our new rural houses, the
Ministry of Health has already laid down
certain minimum standards of accommodation, which represent an advance on
the standards generally observed as regards cottages built under
built under the
Labourers' Code. On the question of
availability of public services for new
rural housing, the Minister of Health is
most anxious that the recommendations
in this connection of the Housing Committee of the Planning Advisory Board,
as contained in the Report published last
year, should, as far as reasonably possible,
be carried into effect: i.e., that rural
houses should be built in groups, and be
so sited as to render the provision of the

Mr. LESLIE: It seems to be left a
little in doubt as to whether the building
of houses is to wait for the provision of
these amenities or not. Are you going to
build houses at once, using such amenities
as there are which are not very many—
or are you going to wait until all these
Is the Governamenities are available?
ment aware that the County Council of
-
Antrim has just passed something in the
nature of a resolution against the building
of houses in villages, on the ground that
it is not suitable either for the agricultural
labourer or for the farmer for whom he
labours.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: It must not
be taken that anything I may say now
commits the Minister of Health and
Local Government with whom I have not
had an opportunity of discussing the
matter. As regards the provision of
amenities I imagine that will proceed
more or less concurrently with the building of houses. The main object, subject
to the many conditions which have to be
taken into account, would be to site the
houses where it would be reasonably possible to bring the amenities to them if
they did not already exist. With regard
to the view taken by the Antrim County
Council, they have merely emphasised
again the inescapable difficulty of all
rural housing, namely, that if you put a
cottage where the amenities are you may
build it too far from the man's work.
That is not always avoidable. I imagine
that what the rural authorities will do
will be to try to so site the cottages that
that unavoidable difficulty will arise as
infrequently as possible. The ideal, however, is not always possible to attain.

R.A.F. IN SENATE
CHAMBER.
Letter from Secretary of State for Air.
Mr. SPEAKER: Perhaps hon. Members would like to hear a letter which I
have received from the Secretary of State
for Air, Sir Archibald Sinclair. He wrote
to me as follow:
:-
Air Ministry,
Whitehall, S.W.1,
4th May, 1945.
Dear Lord Bangor,
In the spring of 1941, Belfast was
subjected to heavy bombing attacks by
the Luftwaffe. The importance of this
city and of Northern Ireland to the
war potential of the United Kingdom
led us to expect more attacks and, in
that expectation, to strengthen the
defences of the area with all speed. We,
therefore, decided to set up a complete
Fighter Group in Northern Ireland,
for which the first requirement was extensive accommodation.
Time was short and new construction impossible. No suitable buildings could be found, so we turned for
help to the Parliament of Northern
Ireland.
At a special meeting held with your
Prime Minister on the 28th July, 1941,
and subsequently at a Secret Session of
Parliament on the 29th July, it was
agreed to transfer to the use of the
Royal Air Force a substantial part of
the Parliament Buildings at Stormont,
including the Senate Chamber. As a
result we were able to set up the Fighter
Group without delay and bring it into
operation by September, 1941. From
then onwards it played a powerful part
in our defence system.
The German occupation of Norway
and France had made it necessary for
all shipping entering or leaving the
United Kingdom to be routed from the
North Irish Channel. In addition to
its responsibilities for static defence
this Group undertook the close escort
of the large convoys bringing supplies
of American troops and material to
this country. The protection of these
vital shipping lanes was of the greatest
importance so long as long-range German aircraft were an active menace
over the Eastern Atlantic.
The Senate Chamber and other
buildings at Stormont have now been
restored to the Parliament of Northern
Ireland. It is therefore a fitting
moment for me to write to you not
only to mark an event unique in the
annals of the Royal Air Force, the
occupation of one of the Chambers of
a Legislature by an operational Headquarters, but also to convey my
appreciation and thanks to you and to
the Parliament of Northern Ireland
for this act of generosity and for the

Supply of Cigarettes.
I
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
beg to ask the Leader of the House
whether he will take steps to secure. for
the workers and other citizens of Belfast
a reasonable supply of cigarettes, so
that they will no longer be compelled
to go from shop to shop like beggars to
purchase same.
I put down this question to the Leader
of the House on account of the very great
shortage of cigarettes there is in the city
of Belfast. I learn from the wholesale
firms that they are in a difficulty because
the available supply of cigarettes is not
nearly sufficient to meet the requirements
of the small retail shops. It
be said
may
that cigarettes are a luxury to the
workers, but in the case of men who have
to go to work at eight o'clock in a
morning and are confined behind walls
for the greater part of the day it is very
necessary for them to be supplied with
cigarettes. They would be in a very difficult position if they were deprived of
cigarettes altogether, and to be refused
a supply adequate to meet their needs is
almost as bad. I find that returned
prisoners of war have the same difficulty
in getting a reasonable supply of
cigarettes in the City of Belfast. That
is a hardship that men who have endured.
so much suffering and privation ought
not to be asked to undergo. I have also
noticed that a considerable number of
ordinary Service men who are home on
leave in Belfast find it equally impossible
to satisfy their needs in this respect. I
hope that, as a result of this question,
there will be an improvement in the
supply of cigarettes available in the city
of Belfast.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): The subject of the.
question raised by the hon. Senator is
something of a mystery. I am informed
that the factories which make cigarette
tobacco are working to full capacity and
that there. has been no reduction in the
allocation of tobacco leaf, nor in the
output of cigarettes for the Northern
Ireland market. In actual fact, it is
larger than at any period during the
war. The shortage of which the hon.
Senator complains does not appear to be
widespread nor of a serious nature, and,
while it is difficult to ascertain the cause,
it may be due to the presence in certain
areas of Belgian troops for whom
N.A.A.F.I. arrangements had not been
completed. That, I understand, has now
been remedied. It is just possible that
these troops bought cigarettes in large
quantities for their own use. That might
cause a shortage in a particular shop, and
a private individual seeking to buy in
that shop and failing to get cigarettes
might rush to buy large quantities in
another shop. That is the only explanation we can think of. The supply is
better than it has ever been, but we are
having inquiries made to try to get to the
bottom of the apparent shortage, which
is rather a mystery. I understand there
has been no shortage during the past
week.
Major DOBBS: Is it not a fact that
the Belgian troops now here have been
practically without cigarettes? I believe.
they have no money with which to purchase them, so I do not think that that
can be the cause of the shortage.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: It is the
only explanation we can think of. It is
very curious that there is a bigger supply
than there has ever been, and yet there is
this shortage in the shops. However, we
are trying to get to the bottom of it.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I am
thankful for the information. I can

Greenmount Agricultural College.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs:
To ask the Leader of the House (1)
whether he can state when the Greenmount Agricultural College at Muckamore will again open for the instruction of young persons, and (2) whether
he is aware of the interest displayed
by young farmers in local courses of
instruction, notably in the case of
agricultural tractors; and will he urge
on the Ministry of Agriculture
the importance of holding displays
and demonstrations of agricultural
machinery in country districts throughout Northern Ireland.
Major DOBBS: I have been asked by
young farmers in my district to put the
question, or rather two questions, standing in my name. During the past two
years I have had the good fortune to
come into close contact with these young
farmers, and I have been surprised
at their keenness. If 30 or 40
years ago one asked a farmer why he
was doing something the reply one would
have got was, "I have always done it
that way", or "My father and my grandfather did it that way." When you ask
a young farmer to-day why he is doing
something he will give you a reason for it.
He will probably tell you that the Ministry of Agriculture's inspector suggested
it; he may tell you he read it somewhere;
or that a friend in County Down suggested the method to him. The young
farmers are very keen. This keenness is
partly due to the compulsory tillage and
the mechanisation of the land. They
have tractors and they can get things
done. I am sure the Leader of the House
will agree with me when I say that these
young farmers should be helped to
acquire practical and technical knowledge
of farming. If that is done it will be a
good thing for the future prosperity of
Northern Ireland.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The buildings at Greenmount Agricultural College
are still occupied by part of the staff of
the Ministry of Agriculture for which
alternative accommodation is not yet
available. It is, however, the Ministry's
intention to re-open the College for
educational purposes as soon as this can
be arranged.
The Minister of Agriculture is well
aware of the interest displayed by young
farmers in matters relating to agricultural
machinery, and he has been gratified at
the response made to the facilities which
have been provided during the last two
years for instruction in the care and
maintenance of agricultural tractors and
the use of tractor, implements. A number
of machinery demonstrations have
already been held, and others will be
arranged as opportunities permit.
VICTORY IN EUROPE.
ADDRESS TO HIS MAJESTY.
Motion by Leader of the House.
The following Motion stood on the Order
Paper in the name of Sir Roland Nugent:
"That an Humble Address be presented
to His Majesty as follows:
:-
Most Gracious Sovereign,
We, Your Majesty's most dutiful and
loyal subjects, the Senate of Northern
Ireland, in Parliament assembled, beg
leave to tender to Your Majesty this
expression of devoted allegiance and of
joyous gratitude that, under the
gracious hand of Almighty God, peace
has been restored to Europe through
victory gloriously won by Your
Majesty's Forces and those of the Allied
Nations.
During the dark and anxious years
of war Your Majesty's lofty example
and deep spirit of self-dedication, so
fully shared by Her Majesty the Queen.
and all the members of the Royal
House, have been a never-failing source
of inspiration to Your Majesty's loyal
subjects. Their courage has also been
sustained and their faith kept strong
by the noble service of Your Majesty's
Medicines and Pharmacy Bill 154
Victory in Europe
15 MAY 1945
*
Ministers, under far-seeing and fearless leadership, and supremely by the
self-sacrificing valour of all the Fighting Services. The people of Northern
Ireland are deeply conscious that new
lustre has been added to the British
name, and that the fires of war have
forged new bonds of international
friendship which will long endure.
We beg leave to express the earnest
hope that Your Majesty may be divinely
blessed to guide the peoples of the
Commonwealth and Empire through
such trials as may still confront them,
so that in the East, as in the West,
right may triumph, bringing tranquillity, security and new life to all
nations.'
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: It is not
infrequently the duty of the Leader of
this House on great State occasions to
propose a formal Address to His Majesty,
but this is not an ordinary occasion of
State, nor can the Motion which stands
in my name be regarded as merely a
normal expression of loyalty. This is an
occasion on which the House could not
dare to vote for such a Motion unless they
felt that the conduct of the House and
of the country had been such as to justify
them in assuming so high a privilege.
What has been the attitude of this
Province during the past six years? Mr.
Churchill has, as so often, found the
perfect phrase. It has been friendly and
loyal. There are two points in which this
friendship and loyalty have been shown,
which, I think, it is peculiarly appropriate to recall on this occasion.
The first you have already heard by the
reading of the letter from the Secretary
of State for Air. The lending of the
Senate Chamber was not only a matter
unique in the history of the Royal Air
Force, but it was, as far as I am able to
ascertain, unique in the history of Parliamentary institutions anywhere in the
world. For the first time a House of
Parliament voluntarily placed its Chamber
at the disposal of the armed forces who
used it as the operational headquarters of
a fighting formation in actual combat
with the enemy. The House, by serving
the Royal Air Force, also wrote a new
chapter in constitutional history. It will
always be a pleasure and pride to the
House to feel they did so. If it commends itself to you, Mr. Speaker, and the
House, it might be appropriate to commemorate this fact by some suitable inscription or tablet in the Senate Chamber.
(Hon. Members: Hear, hear.)
It was
The second point I would recall to the
memory of the House is of far greater
importance. In the dark days of 1940,
when France fell and Britain stood alone,
this tiny corner of the Empire had the
honour to occupy the most important
strategic point in the whole world. Ulster
was the bastion of the North-Western
approaches. If Ulster fell that channel
was closed. If that channel was closed
Britain fell, and liberty, justice, mercy,
and even common human decency, would
have vanished from the world.
granted to us in that terrible but splendid
year to see our duty clearly and to perform it with resolution. By that act of
friendliness and loyalty we earned the
right to a place with that small band of
brother nations who were faithful throughout, without faltering or looking back.
Therefore, my Lord, I think that we may
proceed with a clear conscience and with
a modest but just pride to pass the Motion
which stands upon the Order Paper. I
beg to move.

there. That fact should not be overlooked. I am heartily in agreement with
the Address moved by the Leader of the
House.
