ORDER OF THE DAY.
MEDICINES, PHARMACY AND
POISONS BILL.
Order for Second Reading read.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Medicines and Pharmacy Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
Second time." The object of this Bill is
to bring pharmacy and poison legislation
in Northern Ireland into harmony with
that of Great Britain. Fortunately for
me the Bill is more or less self-explanatory. The first part of the Measure,
Clauses 1 to 8, deal with certain measures
mainly for the protection of the public,
such as the prohibition of advertisements
relating to certain diseases and similar
matters. The next part, Clauses 8 to 24,
deals with measures for improving the
status of the pharmaceutical profession by
raising the qualification of its members
and generally improving the profession.
The third part of the Bill, Clauses 25 to
33, amends, improves, and strengthens
the safety measures concerned with the
sale and distribution of poisonous substances. The fourth and final part of the
Bill deals with a number of general
matters, including the important subjects
of enforcement and inspection. The Bill
is in all important particulars an agreed
Measure which I can commend with confidence to the House.
Question proposed.
Rev Professor CORKEY: I wish to
make two observations on this Bill, but
first of all I should like to say that it
commends itself to me in principle. I
think it is a good Bill and a necessary
piece of legislation, and that it is good
enough to protect thoughtless and illinformed people from exploitation. The
war has taught us the power of propaganda, and those who did not know so
before know now that unthinking people
can be made to believe almost anything
by having it reiterated to them time and
again. This Bill will prevent
scrupulous people from preying upon the
weaknesses of others by misleading
advertisements.
un-
My only complaint about the Bill in
general is that it does not go far enough.
I would like, for example, to have seen
in it a Clause which would have prevented altogether the advertising of a
certain type of alleged medicine. I refer
to medicated wines which are only a
camouflaged form of intoxicants in the
opinion of a great mass of medical people,
and which do a great deal of mischief.
Professor Simms Woodhead, a very distinguished professor of pathology in the
University of Cambridge, said these wines
are a most pernicious institution, and are
doing an enormous amount of harm. I
think the Government would have been
justified, either with or without the precedent of the Imperial Government, in
introducing another Clause in addition to
that which I have already suggested. The
Clause I suggest should require a label
to be put on bottles containing various
types of intoxicants, of which there are
a great variety at present, and which
contain what is technically described in
pharmaceutical books as poison. I raise
this point because it transpired during
the inquiries of the Committee on Adulterated Wines, of which I was a member,
that the great mass of the people had no
idea at all of the alcoholic strength of
these wines, and were entirely mislead
by the name and were using them as
though they were as
harmless as
lemonade.
were
A great number of samples
analysed at the time, and it was found
that these so-called Colonial or Imperial
wines they had a large number of
denominations, such as Red Biddycontained as much alcohol as strong port,
and yet young people were drinking them
as though they were perfectly harmless.
It would have been a safeguard if they
had had the contents set out on the
label. The Report of that Committee
stated:-
We are satisfied that the drinking
of these wines by young persons who
may not realize that they are powerfully alcoholic is a very undesirable
development.
I think that such an analysis as that
upon these bottles would have afforded
as analagous a protection to the public
as that which is provided in this Bill so
far as certain types of alleged medicines
are concerned. The only other point in
the Bill to which I want to call attention is a small point of a legal character
to which I think the Government might
direct their attention. It is, I may say,
in sub-clause (2) of Clause 3. It will be
within the recollection of the House that
when we were recently considering a Bill
for the rehabilitation of people leaving
the Services we discovered that words in
that Bill gave to the courts power to
presume that certain people were guilty
before they had been proved guilty.
This House was very critical of that
Clause and those words on that account.
It was discovered, for example, that when
a limited company was guilty of an
offence, under the terms of that Bill the
Medicines and Pharmacy Bill--
Second Reading
15 MAY 1945
courts could assume that any director
or official of the company was guilty
before he had been proved to be guilty
at all.
There is a Clause in this Bill which
gives to the courts an analagous power,
and I am not sure if the House would be
right in passing it, at any rate without
amendment. These words to which I
refer are to the effect that if an advertisement has been issued which according
to the terms of this Bill would be an
illegal advertisement, and if in the advertisement the name of another person,
apart from the advertiser, happened to
be mentioned as either the manufacturer
or the seller of the commodity in question, then, unless the contrary is proved,
it shall be presumed that that person
took part in the publication of the advertisement. I maintain that that is
giving to the court a power which the
court should not have to assume that a
person is guilty before he has been proved
guilty. One may presume that the man
is guilty, but mere presumption does not
make it fact.
a
I
am reminded of
Abraham Lincoln's reply to a question
about a sheep and its legs. He was
asked, "How many legs has a sheep if
you presume that the tail is a leg?"
Lincoln's answer was "Four, because to
presume that the tail is a leg does not make
the tail a leg."
To presume that these people are guilty
because their name happens to be
mentioned in the advertisement is not to
establish the fact that they actually are
guilty. With a very slight change the
Clause could be brought into line with
our principle of equity. The words,
"unless the contrary is proved," might be
omitted, and some such words as, “if
it is proved that the advertisement was
published with his knowledge he shall be
presumed to have taken part" substituted. I beg to support the Second
Reading of the Bill.
Mr. LESLIE: The Bill before us this
afternoon, if I may venture to say so, is
really a very big Bill. It is not only
fairly voluminous, but it does deal at some
length with a great many subjects which
are of very great importance. It is not
a particularly easy Bill to follow, and I
say that deliberately, having endeavoured
to spend some little time in an attempt,
not perhaps entirely successful, to master
some of its provisions.
The Bill, I may remind the House, is
First
based on four Acts of Parliament.
of all, although this is a minor one, we
get our Dangerous Drugs Act, 1924.
Then we get our Medicines and Pharmacies Act, 1925, which comes very much
into the picture. Then we get the United
Kingdom Act of 1933, to which this Bill
owes very much indeed, and, finally, we
get an Act passed a few years ago-in
1941-by the Parliament of the United
Kingdom. It is that Act of 1941 to
which we owe the first two Clauses of the
Bill before us, but you have to consider,
if you are trying to follow the Bill, all
these four Acts, and see how they run
into each other. I shall have one or two
remarks to make about that at a later
date.
I am glad in one way to recognise the
Bill as a kind of birthday present, a
coming-of-age present-it is a year or two
early-for the Pharmaceutical Society of
Northern Ireland, which was established,
for practical purposes, in 1925 by the Act
which I have mentioned. That Society
has been going ever since with, I gather,
increasing importance and increasing
value to the public. So far as we can
tell it has done very well indeed. I think
that Society is perfectly qualified and perfectly entitled to come to Parliament and
ask for further powers. The further
powers which they ask-I assume the Bill
is promoted by them, although that is
not entirely the case, as it comes from
the Ministry of Home Affairs-are to improve the standard of those engaged in
their profession, so as to keep them on a
par with their comperes in England, and
thus preserve that sense of parity
which exists between the pharmaceutical
chemists in England and Northern Ireland
and vice versa. That seems to be of great
importance. Then, again. the Society
asks for power in exercising more discipline and preserving in that sense better
order amongst those connected with it.
That is, undoubtedly, a matter which is
accepted in numerous other walks of life,
the solicitors' profession being suggested.
The Jockey Club will occur to some of us.
It is not at all an unusual thing that some
sort of internal power should be given to a
governing body. That is what is asked
for on the present occasion.
We differ in this Bill in one or two
instances from the provisions of the
United Kingdom Acts of 1933 and 1941.
In the very first Clause we have intro-
159, Medicines and Pharmacy Bill--

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Leslie]
duced amongst those diseases specified,
cancer. I cannot find that included in
any United Kingdom Act. I imagine,
though I do not know, that the introduction of cancer in this place is due to the
report of the committee referred to in
another place by the right hon. Gentleman the Minister of Home Affairs. I
think that probably is so. I do not
imagine for a moment that anybody in
this House will offer the smallest objection
because, as we know, that particular
disease is one of those susceptible to treatment if tackled in time, and is also, I am
afraid, exposed more than many other
diseases to the danger of quack
treatment. That being so one is glad
to welcome the provision in this Bill.
That is the first difference of any consequence that I notice between the proposed legislation and the legislation across
the water.
If we pass to Clause 12, which deals
with the construction of the council,
society and so on, we find on looking at
the United Kingdom Statutes that the
Privy Council is given certain powers.
Those powers
are given here to the
Minister of Home Affairs. That is a very
distinct difference, and it is a difference
which, I think, merits a certain amount
of consideration. I do not think that the
effect of that consideration would be to
induce Members of this House to raise
any objection to the proposal. I think
we all agree that for practical purposes
the method adopted in the Bill is more
desirable than the other. Our arrangements as regards Privy Council matters
and so on in this country are not quite
the same as across the water. I do not
think there is any reason why we should
bluntly assimilate our procedure under
this Bill to the procedure in England in
that matter.
While speaking on the subject I may
remind the House that we are not taking
away or seeking to take away any prerogatives from the Privy Council.
Privy Council of Northern Ireland never
had this prerogative. This Statutory
The
Committee is an entirely new device on
which we are embarking to-day, and,
therefore, we are perfectly entitled, so to
speak, to place the appointment where we
wish. If the House looks further into
the matter they will see at a later stage
in the Bill that the present powers are
carefully preserved. There is the approval
of the Government under the Governor in
the one case, and the approval of the
Privy Council as regards poisons and so
on in the other. In this new proposal
there is nothing which is in any way a
derogation of any form of prerogative.
Again, we have not established, and
are not seeking to establish a Poisons
Board, such as that given birth to
across the water. I should be inclined
to say that the. intention is in fact to
substitute the Medical Faculty of Queen's
University for the Poisons Board. At
all events, the method suggested in the
Bill seems to me to be a perfectly workable proposal, and I understand from
what was said by the right hon. Gentle-
Iman the Leader of the House that the
proposals have been agreed by all those
who are really competent to express a
valuable opinion on the subject.
When we come to Clause 1, I think
we come to the. first thing approaching a
difficulty. That is where we refer to a
local authority. I am very much afraid,
for the sake of the reference in the first
Clause, that we shall have to define what
we mean by "local authority." On
looking through the Bill hon. Members
will see the expression "local authority
occurs not only in the first Clause, where
it is taken, as I said before, from the.
English Act of 1941, but it also occurs
again at later stages. In one case dealing
with licensing it is carefully defined as a
county council and so forth. In another
""
case it is defined as being a local authority
having power to administer the Sale of
Food and Drugs Acts, and yet in a third
case no definition is given, but is implied.
We shall have to do something to make
it clear in this particular Clause what we
mean. On the other hand we do not
intend to go too far-that is the intention
of the Clause-but we must be careful
that we are not unduly restricted. The
best thing would be to put something
down on paper which would serve as the
basis for discussion-I cannot do
thing else and then we can see if we
are bound to do something and, if so,
what. I am very much afraid we must
do something.
any.
The next point to which I must direct
attention is the provision in Clause 30 of
the Bill-that in certain circumstances
where a licence for the sale of poisons
and so on has been refused by the local
authority there is a right of appeal. The
161 Medicines and Pharmacy Bill--

15 MAY 1945
Second Reading
right of appeal is given, and it is given
not only to the rejected applicant but
also to the Society. The appeal goes to
the Minister of Home Affairs. In the
corresponding English measure-I do not
think in this particular the measure applies
to Scotland-the appeal is given to quarter
sessions, not to the Minister of Home
Affairs. That is a matter which, I think.
the House ought to consider with some
little care. As the House will remember,
the Minister of Home Affairs is not now
the Minister directly concerned with
local government, and in that respect
alone it would not be unreasonable for the
House to say that, having regard to the
small number of cases likely to arise
and the trivial nature of the injury
inflicted, and also having regard to
the undoubted weight to be attached to
the opinion of the Society, the solution
provided in the Bill if not ideally
perfect-I do not think it is ideally
perfect myself-will probably be recognised as the best course to take.
I do not myself propose to suggest any
alteration at a later stage, but I thought
it was a matter which should be brought
to the attention of the House in order
that hon. Members may form their own
opinion and see whether they are prepared
to consent to what is a startling difference,
although a matter of small moment, between our Bill and the legislation across
the water, and as to whether they will
accept the new kind of bureaucratic control rather than give it direct to the
courts. For the reason I have given, I
think the House will decide to leave the
Bill as it stands.
In one or two other Clauses there are
slight details of phraseology which will be
Idealt with when the time comes. I cannot refrain from pointing out that Clause
35 is of great interest. The special subclauses are sub-clauses 2 and 4. I cannot
help thinking that those two sub-clauses
will be of very special interest to those
Members of this House who remember the
recent discussions on the Disabled Persons
Bill. Those two sub-clauses differ from
what appears in the Disabled Persons Bill.
I think hon. Members will find the Bill
before us very interesting in that respect.
I am bound to say that in one respect subclause 4 is rather better than the corresponding Clause in the Act of 1933 to
which 1 have referred, because, although
it goes as far as our twelve months, it
does avoid the absurdity of all eternity
and then some, which occurs in one or two
Acts of Parliament and is not absent from
the Disabled Persons Bill. I shall not
suggest any alteration in either of those
two sub-clauses.
The right hon. and reverend Gentleman
referred to something he regarded as
omissions in the Bill before us-medicated
wines, Red Biddy-and also he had a
legal objection to sub-clause (2) of Clause
3. I am afraid that, as regards the medicated wines and so on, my own opinion is
that any attempt in the Bill before us to
Ideal with that matter would take us outside the title of the Bill. If further
legislation is required as regards those
matters it must come, so to speak, in its
proper place. I do not think an argument on that line belongs to the Bill
before us. As regards the other matter,
I welcome, as I always do, any assistance
to keep to constitutional practice. The
particular words to which the reverend
Gentleman referred will be found in
Section 10 of the Act of 1941 which I have
mentioned more than once. We cannot
neglect the provision of paragraph (b) of
sub-clause (2). It has to be proved that
the articles were manufactured, sold or
offered by the person charged. That has
got to be proved first. Then we presume
that that person took part in the publication of the advertisement. I do not know
whether the House will agree with a provision of that sort in the Bill. I say that
remembering what the right hon. and
reverend Gentleman and I said about a
wider passage in another Bill before us.
I do not think you can, in this case, make
anything of it.
There is only one other matter I want
to refer to, and that arises at a very much
later date; in fact, I think I can base my
remarks on the Fourth Schedule for convenience. The Fourth Schedule is that
which deals with enactments repealed.
There are two things I want to say about
that. The first is with reference to the
Arsenic Act of 1851. I cannot help feeling rather sorry that the old Arsenic Act
of 1851 has now disappeared. There is
a lovely provision in it about mixing soot
with crude arsenic. As a matter of fact
I am afraid that, in practice, that has
been gone for some years owing to other
methods, and it is now replaced by a reference in Clause 32, sub-clause (1), paragraph (e).
The other thing is more important. I
am quite sure that painstaking Members
163 Medicines and Pharmacy Bill

THE SENATE
Adjournment
[Mr. Leslie]
who have gone through the Bill will have
found some little difficulty in ascertaining
what sub-sections 8, 9, and 10 of Section
7 of the Pharmacy and Poisons Act, 1925,
were about. If hor Members have tried
to look them up I cannot help thinking
that they may have found the search as
profitable as a reference to last Thursday's Times. I am not going to tell anybody where to find them. The only thing
I will say is that you will not find them
in the edition of the Statutues downstairs
of the Act of 1925. That leads me
to say that surely to goodness when we
have passed this Bill we should have a
consolidation of these poisons Acts. If
you look at the matter carefully and remember how much we are drawing on
those Acts, the Acts of 1933 and 1941,
and knocking about our own Act of 1925,
I think you will agree that the time has
come when we ought to consolidate. We
could not do that properly until we have
passed this Bill, but, having done that,
we ought to consolidate the various Acts.
It is almost impossible for the layman to
find his way about these Acts. The Bill
now before the House is a very excellent
Bill.
Without prejudice to what anybody else may have to say, there are only
two or three very trivial matters which
will require attention at a later stage but,
taking the Bill as a whole, I have no
hesitation in commending it to the House.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I rise only
to acknowledge the support which has
now.
been given to the Bill, and to say I think
that probably the points that have beer
raised by both right hon. Members are
points on which further discussion will be
more fitting on the Committee stage than
With regard to the point about the
Privy Council, perhaps I might air a
little dangerous knowledge. I believe the
position is that in England the Privy
Council has committees of the Council to
deal with such matters as medical research
and so on, and that it has become almost
the established practice for the Lord
President of the Council to be the Member of the Cabinet who is responsible for
all scientific matters. That is probably
why the Privy Council finds itself in the
English Bill, and why our Privy Council
is not mentioned in this Bill. The other
points I hope to be better equipped to
answer on the Committee stage.

The National Trust Bill.
A Message was brought from the House
of Commons to acquaint this House that
they had ordered:
That the promoters of the National
Trust Bill which has been introduced
into this House in the present Session of
Parliament shall have leave to introduce
the same in the next Session of Parliament, provided that notice of their intention to do so be lodged with the Clerk
of the Parliaments not later than One
o'clock on the day prior to the close of
the present Session, and that all fees
due thereon up to that period be paid.
That such Bill be deposited in the
office of the Clerk of the Parliaments
not later than Three o'clock on or before the third day on which the House
shall sit after the next meeting of Parliament, with a declaration annexed
thereto, signed by the agent, stating
that the Bill is the same in every respect as the Bill at the last stage of the
proceedings thereon in this House in
the present Session.
That the proceedings on such Bill
shall be pro forma only in regard to
every stage through which the same
shall have passed in the present Session,
and that no new fees be charged in
regard to such stages.
That the Standing Orders by which
the proceedings on Bills are regulated
shall not apply to such Bill in regard to
any of the stages through which the
same shall have passed during the present Session.
Ordered, That the said Message be
taken into consideration to-morrow.

point I am on in this Amendment is this,

after school children in the matter of

immediate future, and it seems to me
that there is a very great danger of the
authorities concerned quite inadvertently
transgressing the strict wording of the
Bill before us. The Committee will see
that the definition of advertisement is
extremely wide. It is not limited to
advertisements in the newspapers, which
is what we are really getting at, but it
is so wide as to cover any sort of bill or
poster. The authorities in the schools
looking after the health of the children,
by giving instruction as to the use of
authorised medicines, may inadvertently
transgress the provisions of this Bill. It
may be that the Government thinks it
possible to deal with that by administrative action, and it may be that it is
thought that the arrangements are such
that the sanction of the Minister of
Health and Local Government could be
obtained without further trouble. But
on the face of things it seems to me that
we ought to put in "Minister of Education," and accordingly I beg to move.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The Government would be very reluctant to add the
Minister of Education to the Minister of
Home Affairs and the Minister of Health
and Local Government. The Minister of
Home Affairs administers the Dangerous
Drugs Act, and the Minister of Health
and Local Government is concerned with
public health, which includes the health
of the children, and we are afraid that
if you put in the Minister of Education
it might give rise to confusion. We
believe it would be perfectly easy for the
Minister of Education to communicate
with the other Ministers and obtain their
sanction, and therefore we think it would
safer to leave the wording as it is.
Mr. LESLIE: I think that is the
answer-that the right hon. Gentleman
agrees that what is necessary can be done
by administrative action, and therefore it
is not necessary to insert any additional
words in the Bill. Accordingly I ask
leave to withdraw the Amendment.

a
Mr. LESLIE: This is another troublesome little matter which I mentioned on
the Second Reading. We have got this
expression "local authority" in other.
passages in the Bill. We have got it in
Clause 30 as the licensing authority
where local authority is expressly defined
as a county council or the council of a
county borough. We have got it again
elsewhere as the authority being entrusted
with the administration of the Sale of
Food and Drugs Act, and we have got it
in a third place where we have no definition at all, but where it is obvious that
the definition is the definition in Clause
30, a county council or county borough
council. But we are given no help as to
what we mean here. The words "local
authority" are just by themselves, and I
think it is necessary that we should know
what we do mean.
The local authority comes in twice.
They are given the privilege of issuing
advertisements if they want to, and they
are given the privilege of receiving advertisements if they cannot escape them,
but the point is that it is made an offence
to send these advertisements to people
who are not under that privilege or
exemption, and those people are the local
authority. The question arises, what do
we mean by local authority? If it had
not been for the other two passages in
the Bill I should not have been disposed
to have troubled the Committee with this
Amendment, but I do think it is desirable to say here what we do mean by
the expression "local authority". Accordingly I included in the Amendment every
conceivable authority I could think of
except a rural district council. There is a
slight error in the wording of the Amendment as printed which I should not like
to think was due to my bad writing. The
word "or" should read "a". I beg to
move, at the end of the Clause to insert,
"the expression 'local authority' includes
Medicines and Pharmacy Bill-
Committee
22 MAY 1945
the council of a county, a county borough
or urban district, or board of guardians
and the Commissioners of a town."
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The expression "local authority" was left to stand
by itself in this case because there was
not the same necessity to define it as
there was in Clauses 30 and 37, where
power was imposed on the local authority,
and the local authority to receive this
power was defined as narrowly as possible.
It was thought desirable to leave it as
wide as possible in the case covered by
the Amendment. The real object is to
give a defence to a person who more or
less in good faith has sent an advertisement to a suitable sort of person, and we
want to leave it as wide as we reasonably
can. If we narrow it we shall be depriving of a good defence a person who really
ought to have a good defence because he
has probably acted in good faith. That
is the reason why we cannot agree to the
Amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think I can
press the Amendment. The right hon.
Gentleman might have added, I think,
that the phrase is not defined in the
English Act, but, of course, against that
the English Act does not include anything like Clause 30 which has been referred to. That is the only point, I
think, the possibility of someone being
misled and addressing such a notice to
someone who is not entitled to receive it.
Against that you have got the protection
that it lies on the Attorney-General to
authorise the prosecution. I think the
explanation given by the right hon.
Gentleman should be satisfactory, and I
am
quite prepared to ask leave to
withdraw the Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :

At the end of Clause 1 to add-"and
the expression surgical appliances '
includes any article used as ancillary to
the treatment of cataract or glaucoma."
Mr. LESLIE: This is a little more
troublesome. Here we are stating the
class of firms who may receive these
advertisements, and in looking through
the list of diseases I find cataract. One
of the articles that may be used in connection with cataract is spectacles or

tinted glasses. You cannot say that a
pair of shades or tinted spectacles are
not an article, nor can it be contended
that they are not used in the treatment
of cataract, but are they surgical
appliances? We have got the expression
"surgical appliances" in this Clause, and
we have got it a little further on, and I
think we must put in something here to
cover these simple articles, tinted glasses.
I do think this is a case where we must
be explicit. We do not want to deprive
the ordinary optician of the right to receive circulars relating to the manufacture of tinted glasses and such things,
and this is the shortest way I can think of
of doing it, by saying that surgical
appliances does cover such things as tinted
glasses. I beg to move the Amendment
standing in my name.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Again, we
must resist the Amendment, because we
are afraid it will give rise to a dangerous
interpretation. If there is a specific mention of any type of appliance it may be
held to exclude types that are not mentioned, or which are not of a like
character. If that were so the effect
would be to take away a defence that
would otherwise be a good defence. I
understand that no actual judicial interpretation has ever been given of "surgical
appliance." It is a pretty wide expression, and we prefer to leave the expression as "surgical appliances," and
not run the risk of misinterpretation.
Mr. LESLIE: On this occasion I find
it very difficult to agree with the right
hon. Gentleman. Surgical appliances
surely cannot be held to cover tinted
glasses. All I have done is to suggest
that we should leave it open for anybodywho deals in things of that sort to receive circulars about them. I find it very
difficult to agree that surgical appliances
covers these things.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: If at a later
stage we put before the word "surgical"
the word "optical and" would that meet
with the approval of the right hon. and
learned Gentleman.
Mr. LESLIE: If you are prepared to
substitute for "surgical appliances" the
words "optical or surgical appliances" I
should very much prefer that expression
to the words I have on the Paper.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
36, sub-clause (3), after the word "Inspector" (The Inspector) to insert the words,
"on production of his authority.' I
think we must have here the right of the
person inspected to require. the production of the authority of the inspector.
It is not the inspector I am afraid of; it
is the bogus inspector against whom every
householder ought to be protected. The
man who goes to look at the gas meter
does not always look at the meter.
man who goes away with the silver spoons
in his pocket is not altogether extinct.
If we put in these words once it would
do for the whole Clause without repeating
it. The country generally is entitled to
require, that an inspector should produce
his authority when entering premises.
This provision is common in many similar
Acts, but in many cases it is not necessary
for the inspector to produce. his authority,
because he is perfectly well known to the
householder. I think we are entitled to
insist that a man should produce his
authority.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The Clause
is drafted so as to permit the continuance
of the present practice whereby an inspector is provided with a warrant which
he produces when asked for it. There is
no Statutory obligation on him to produce
that warrant, but it is obvious that some
persons would not allow him to inspect
their premises without producing his
authority. The Government would be
prepared to accept the Amendment in
some such form as "on production of his
authority if required to do so." We do
not want him to have to produce his
authority at once, because, although
hitherto he has been mainly concerned
with poisons, he will now be dealing with
all kinds of medicines, and if he had to
produce his authority before entering the
premises I am afraid it would not be a
very effective inspection.
Mr. LESLIE: I am quite content with
that. I can quite see the objection to
requiring an inspector to wave his authority from the corner of the street and so
give everybody notice that he is coming. I
will ask leave to withdraw the Amendment if the right hon. Gentleman will put
down a similar Amendment on the Report
stage.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Clause ordered to stand part of the Bill.
CLAUSES 37 (Enforcement of certain
provisions of Part I by local authorities
executing Sale of Food and Drugs Acts),
38 (Interpretation: general provisions),
39 (Repeal), 40 (Commencement of Act)
and 41 (Short title, construction and
citation), ordered to stand part of the
Bill.
FIRST, SECOND, THIRD and
FOURTH SCHEDULES agreed to.
PREAMBLE and TITLE agreed to.
Ordered, That the Bill be reported
without amendment to the House.
Ordered, That the House do now
resume.
House resumed accordingly.
Bill reported without Amendment.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That this Report be now received." We will put down the Amendments I have indicated for the Third
Reading.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

THE NATIONAL TRUST BILL.
Order read for taking into consideration
the Commons Message of 22nd May.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I beg to
move, "That this House agrees to the
Resolution of the House of Commons:
That the promoters of the National
Trust Bill which has been introduced
into this House in the present Session
of Parliament shall have leave to introduce the same in the next Session of
Parliament, provided that notice of
their intention to do so be lodged with
the Clerk of the Parliaments not later
than One o'clock on the day prior to
the close of the present Session, and that
all fees due thereon up to that period
be paid."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Resolved, "That such Bill be deposited
in the office of the Clerk of the Parliaments not later than Three o'clock on or
before the third day on which the House
shall sit after the next meeting of Parliament, with a declaration annexed thereto,
signed by the agent, stating that the Bill
is the same in every respect as the Bill at
the last stage of the proceedings thereon
in this House in the present Session.".
(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Resolved, "That the proceedings on
such Bill shall be pro forma only in regard
to every stage through which the same
shall have passed in the present Session,
and that no new fees be charged in regard
to such stages."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Resolved, "That the Standing Orders
by which the proceedings on Bills are regulated shall not apply to such Bill in
regard to any of the stages through which
the same shall have passed during the present Session."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Ordered, That a Message be sent to the
House of Commons to acquaint them that
the Senate agree to their Resolution
suspending the said Bill.
Business of the House.

This Bill makes the necessary financial
provision for certain Government services,
and its introduction at this stage is
caused by the approaching dissolution of
this Parliament. In other words, the
country's bill has to be paid until the
new Parliament has assembled.

BELFAST COUNTY BOROUGH
ADMINISTRATION BILL.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time".-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
In
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time". This Bill places, the
Belfast Corporation in exactly the same
position as it would have been in had
the Belfast County Borough Administration Act of 1942 not been passed.
Clause 2 provision is made to ensure the
transfer of the functions of the City
Administrators back to the Corporation
without making it necessary for the
Corporation to commence anew any
business which has already been passed.
181 Belfast Administration Bill-

23 MAY 1945
Second Reading
This Clause also provides an idemnity for
the Administrators in respect of any
action taken by them in the course of
their duties. The remaining Clause provides for the safeguarding of the position
of the officers of the Corporation in fulfilment of the pledge given when the Act
of 1942 was passed. It is proposed that
the Bill will come into force on 1st July,
1945.
I
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
should like to say a word in the interests
of the City Council to say how thankful
and pleased we are that the Government
has seen fit to introduce this Bill to restore
them their previous powers. I feel now
as I felt before, that there was never any
justification for the attitude taken up by
the Government at the time the Act of
1942 was passed, but I suppose one must
be thankful that they have seen the error
of their ways and that they are trying to
improve matters before the dissolution of
Parliament. It does look ridiculous and
very queer that the same Parliament that
took away the powers of the Corporation
and introduced a Bill to deprive the
citizens of their rights and privileges are
now introducing a Bill to restore those
powers. I would not like to think it is
caused by a change of Ministers
Mr. MCALLISTER: By the general
election.
No,
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
by a change from the Ministry of Home
Affairs to the Ministry of Health and
Local Government. Perhaps one is better
acquainted with the position than the
other. I am very glad to see the Bill
being introduced, and I am sure there
will not be the slightest opposition to it.
Everyone seems to think the proper course
has been adopted and I am sure there
will be no objection in this House any
more than there was in the other House.
Mr. LESLIE: Is not the real point of
the matter this: that it has now been
found possible to shorten the period during which the Act of 1942 was to have
currency. It would in any case have
come to an end on 31st March, 1946, if I
Circumremember the date correctly.
stances have changed one
way and
another a good deal since 1942 and the
Government have decided that it is now
practical, reasonable and generally desirable, as I say, to shorten the termination
of the currency of the original Act. That
is all the House is asked to do.
Mr. LENNON: One can, of course,
only welcome a Bill which terminates any
interference with the ordinary workings of
democratic government. That being so,
this Bill can only commend itself to the
House. As was said by a speaker before
me, it does not appear as if there could
be any possible exception taken to the
Bill. What does strike me as being very
peculiar is that whereas a special Act of
Parliament is introduced for one council
which has been relegated to the limbo of
forgotten things, no mention has been
made of one other council which has been
put out of existence, and has been out of
existence for very many years.
The urban district council of Armagh
has not been functioning since, I believe,
1933 or 1934, whereas the Belfast Corporation has ceased to function for merely a
matter of approximately two years. While
one naturally welcomes any step on the
part of the Government back into the
realm of decent democratic government,
one is apt to ask, why is a council such
as Armagh Urban District Council left
abandoned, and why this Bill to restore
to the Belfast Corporation its functions.
while another council which, may I say,
can claim to be the more ancient of the
two, is left in its suspended state, being
administered by a Commissioner appointed
by the Government. While, as I say, one
can only welcome this Bill, one can only
view with disfavour the disparity which
exists, and will exist once this Bill becomes
an Act, between the city of Armagh and
the city of Belfast.
As a
Rev. PROFESSOR CORKEY:
member of the House at the time when the
Corporation Bill was passed in 1942 I
should like to add my word of welcome to
this Bill bringing the transitional state of
affairs to an end. I suppose the less said
about the circumstances which led up to
the 1942 Bill the better, but I might say
very briefly that the whole episode ought
to remind public-spirited people in the
city that democracy makes great demands
upon the time and energy of all of us,
and I think that we might well take to
heart the lesson that our institutions cannot be worked in a successful way unless
people with time and ability and any gifts.
for public service are prepared to come
forward and play their part in the management of public affairs.
The Belfast Corporation is a great and
worthy body and has done fine work in the
Belfast Administration Bill-
Third Reading
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
past for the city, and I consider that so far
as social services are concerned-and it is
in the social services side of the city's activities that I am chiefly interested the city
councillors stand in the front line of the
battle against evil and for social reform.
But I welcome this Bill and wish all joy
and happiness and prosperity to those who
have the obligation and duty of carrying
on the work of the Corporation.
Mr. MCALLISTER: As one who
opposed the 1942 Bill as strenuously as I
possibly could on the general grounds that
it sabotaged the most elementary principles of democracy, I welcome the Bill. I
should like to know from the Leader of
the House why the Government, after two
years' experience of this Bill in the Belfast
Corporation, on the eve of a general election, are on the stool of repentance now
as regards the city of Belfast, but have
made no gesture, good, bad or indifferent
with regard to the city of Armagh which
has been disfranchised for a great number
of years.
and
I remember I was satisfied that no substantial reason had been given of maladministration by the Belfast Corporation
which justified the passing of the 1942
Bill, but it was not on that ground that I
opposed the Bill. It was on the general
principle of democratic government. But
we have this Bill introduced to-day,
the Leader of the House, speaking for the
appropriate Ministry, gave no reason good,
bad or indifferent for the change of policy
of the Government on the eve of a general
election and the re-establishment of democracy in the city of Belfast. It is peculiar
that this Bill is passed at this very
moment on the last day on which this
Parliament functions and is passed
through all its stages on one day-an
important Bill like that on the eve of a
general election! We know that in the
city of Armagh the Government will not
be affected. It would not confer upon
them any advantage because they could
not win a seat in Armagh if you gave the
franchise back to Armagh to-day. I think
it is up to the Government to give some
explanation of the differentiation between
the city of Belfast and the city of Armagh
in re-establishing democracy in the one
and refusing it in the other.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I wish to
reply as briefly as possible. In the first
place, with regard to the Belfast Bill a
definite promise had been given to the
Belfast Corporation that their powers
should be restored by 1st July a considerable time before any decision was
taken with regard to an election. Of
course we all knew an election would come
this year. The House is carrying out the
promise. Otherwise the Government
could not keep the promise as to 1st July.
With regard to Armagh Urban Council
I do not want to enter into the merits
now, but Belfast Corporation never went
out of existence and consequently it does
not need an election. It would be impossible to restore the powers of Armagh
Council until this Parliament or a subsequent Parliament repealed the Act
preventing a local government election
taking place. It is a technical point, but
a point of great importance.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Is this not an
amending Act to-day?
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Yes, but the
other meant repealing the whole Act
which would effect the whole Province.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Resolved, "That this House do resolve
itself into Committee on the said Bill".-

ADDRESS TO HIS MAJESTY.
Message from His Grace the Governor.
Mr. SPEAKER reported to the House
that the following Message had been
received from His Grace the. Governor in
reply to the Address to His Majesty:
I have received the King's command
to convey to the Senate of Northern
Ireland the thanks of His Majesty for
their expression of loyalty and devotion
to himself, the Queen and the Royal
House, and his hope. that the people
of Northern Ireland may enjoy the
blessings of the peace which they have
done so much to achieve.
THE SENATE ELECTION.
List of Retiring Members.
Mr. SPEAKER: I should like to inform the House that the following
Senators retire on the 11th June, 1945,
in accordance with the provisions of the
Fourth Schedule of the Government of
Ireland Act, 1920:-William Barclay,
Major J. C. Boyle, Gerald Browne, Professor Corkey, Joseph Cunningham, J. C.
Glendinning, J. G. Leslie, T. S. McAllister, John McHugh, Joseph Maguire,
W. E. Stevenson, George Whaley.
Owing to the fact that a Dissolution of
Parliament takes place on Friday next, it
will not be possible to hold a Senate
Election until the new House of Commons
has been elected and sworn, and this will
mean that the Senate will not be at full
strength again until that election takes
place. Consequently, when the House
resumes after the General Election there
will only be fourteen serving Senators.
House adjourned during pleasure.
House resumed.

The Commons agree to the Amendments
made by the Senate to the Medicines,
Pharmacy and Poisons Bill without
amendment.

PROROGATION OF PARLIAMENT.
Mr. SPEAKER: I have it in command
from His Grace the Governor to read
forthwith in this Chamber to the Senators
and Commons of Northern Ireland therein
assembled His Grace's Proclamation proroguing this present Session of Parliament.
Resolved, "That the Gentleman. Usher
of the Black Rod be commanded to inform the Commons that the Proclamation
proroguing the present Session of Parliament is to be read forthwith, and inviting
the Commons to attend in order to hear
the same." (Sir Joseph Davison.)
:-
The Commons, being at the Bar with
their Speaker, the Proclamation was read
to both Houses of Parliament by the
Speaker of the Senate (in pursuance of
His Grace's command), as followeth :
BY HIS GRACE THE GOVERNOR
OF NORTHERN IRELAND.
A PROCLAMATION
For Proroguing the present Parliament
of Northern Ireland.
WHEREAS it is provided by section
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance of
Prorogation
Prorogation
24 MAY 1945
certain enactments amending the said
Act, that the Governor of Northern
Ireland shall, in His Majesty's name,
summon, prorogue and dissolve the
Parliament of Northern Ireland:
NOW I, JAMES ALBERT EDWARD,
DUKE OF ABERCORN, Knight of
the Most Noble Order of the Garter,
Knight of the Most Illustrious Order of
Saint Patrick, Governor of Northern Ireland, by this Proclamation do publish
and declare His Majesty's pleasure that
the said Parliament be, as from the
reading of this Proclamation in the
Parliament of Northern Ireland, prorogued to Tuesday, the fifth day of June,
one thousand nine hundred and fortyfive, to be then holden.
Given this twenty-third day of May,
one thousand nine hundred and fortyfive.
(Signed by his own hand),
ABERCORN.
GOD SAVE THE KING.
End of the Eighth Session (opened

Tuesday, 20th February, 1945) of the
Fifth Parliament of Northern Ireland, in
the Ninth year of the Reign of His
Majesty King George the Sixth.
A PROCLAMATION.
For dissolving the Fifth Parliament of Northern
Ireland, and declaring the Calling of another.
WHEREAS it is provided by section eleven of the
Government of Ireland Act, 1920, as
modified
by and in pursuance of certain enactments amending
the said Act, that the Governor of Northern Ireland
shall, in His Majesty's name, summon, prorogue and
dissolve the Parliament of Northern Ireland:
AND WHEREAS it is desirable that the present
Parliament of Northern Ireland, which stands
prorogued to Tuesday the Fifth day of June next,
should be dissolved and that a new Parliament should
be called:
NOW THEREFORE I, JAMES ALBERT
EDWARD, Duke of Abercorn, Knight of the Most
Noble Order of the Garter, Knight of the Most
Illustrious Order of Saint Patrick, Governor of
Northern Ireland, by this Proclamation do publish and
declare His Majesty's Pleasure that the said Parliament be, as from the date of this Proclamation,
dissolved, and that the Senators and Members of the
House of Commons be discharged from their Meeting
and Attendance on the said TUESDAY, the FIFTH .
day of JUNE next:
AND I do Hereby make known to all His Majesty's
loving subjects His Royal Will and Pleasure to call a
new Parliament of Northern Ireland, and do further
declare that I have taken Order that Writs shall be
issued forthwith in His Majesty's name under the Great
Seal of Northern Ireland, in due Form and according
to Law, for calling a Parliament of Northern Ireland
to meet at Stormont, Northern Ireland, on TUESDAY
the SEVENTEENTH day of JULY next, and for
causing the Senators and Commons who are to serve
in the said Parliament to be duly returned to and
give their Attendance in His Majesty's said Parliament
on TUESDAY the SEVENTEENTH day of JULY
next, which Writs are to be returnable in due course
of Law.
GIVEN under my Hand and the Great Seal of
Northern Ireland this twenty-fifth day of May, in the
year of our Lord one thousand nine hundred and
forty-five.
ABERCORN.
GOD SAVE THE KING.
